What Is Mantiq?
beginner
5 min
Mushtarak Lafzi: One Word, Multiple Meanings
Author defines mushtarak and distinguishes its two types: lafzi and ma'nawi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify mushtarak lafzi and distinguish it from mushtarak ma'nawi.

Core Idea
Mushtarak lafzi is a single word placed for multiple distinct meanings through separate word placements.

In Islamic logic, the term 'mushtarak' often refers to mushtarak lafzi—words identical in form but with different, unrelated meanings. This lesson unpacks the definition from the commentary, helping you spot this key category and avoid confusion in textual interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
The passage says 'mushtarak' i.e., lafzi, because the meanings share the word. Note that the definition specifies 'multiple meanings' and 'separate word placements'.
2
Identify the key feature
The core feature is that the word has been placed (وضع) for more than one distinct meaning, each with its own placement. This is what linguists call homonymy.
3
Note the inclusion of proper names
The passage explicitly includes proper names (أعلام) when they refer to multiple individuals, e.g., the name 'Muhammad' for different people. This is a subtype of mushtarak lafzi.
4
Compare with mushtarak ma'nawi
Contrast with the second type: mushtarak ma'nawi is a universal meaning that applies to many particulars, not multiple meanings. This comparison clarifies the boundary.
The same word may be used for both types, but the logic is distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قَوْلُهُ : فَمُشْتَرَكُ) أي : لفظي؛ نسبةً للفظ؛ لاشتراك المعاني في اللفظ الموضوع لها، ويدخل فيه العلم إذا تعدد، وأما المشترك المعنوي؛ فهو المعنى الكلي الصادق على جزئيات كما في المتواطئ والمشترك، فمعنى اللفظ فيهما مشترك معنى؛ لاشتراك الأفراد في المعنى الموضوع له اللفظ.
(His saying: 'so it is mushtarak') i.e., lafzi (verbal); attributed to the word; because the meanings share the same word placed for them, and it includes proper names when multiple [bearers]. As for mushtarak ma'nawi (meaning-based), it is the universal meaning that applies to particulars, as occurs with mutawāti' (univocal) and mushtarak, so the meaning of the word in these two [types] is shared in meaning; because the individuals share in the meaning for which the word was placed.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage defines 'mushtarak' (the shared term) and differentiates between its two principal types: mushtarak lafzi and mushtarak ma'nawi. The commentator begins by glossing the phrase 'so it is mushtarak' from the matn, explaining that it means mushtarak lafzi — a term that is one in pronunciation but has multiple unrelated meanings due to separate word placements (وضع) for each meaning. An example is the word 'ayn (عين), which can mean eye, spring, etc. This type includes proper names (like 'Muhammad') when it refers to multiple individuals. The second type is mushtarak ma'nawi, which is not a term with multiple distinct meanings but rather a universal meaning that applies to many particulars. This is the same as mutawāti' (univocal) and some definitions of mushtarak in a broader sense. The key point is that in mushtarak ma'nawi, the word is placed for a single meaning (a universal concept) that applies to many individuals, while in mushtarak lafzi, the word is placed for multiple distinct meanings. The passage clarifies that the term 'mushtarak' without qualification usually refers to the lafzi type, and the ma'nawi type is actually the same as univocal terms. This distinction is crucial for understanding the classification of words in logic and usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
مشترك لفظي (mushtarak lafẓī): A single word that has been placed for multiple distinct meanings through separate word placements; the meanings are unrelated and the word is homonymous. [Note: This term is consistent; some later texts may refer to it simply as 'mushtarak'.]
مشترك معنوي (mushtarak maʿnawī): A word that is placed for a universal meaning that applies to many particulars; it is synonymous with mutawāti' (univocal) in this context. [Note: This usage is less common; many texts distinguish between mutawāti' and mushtarak as separate categories. Here, the author seems to merge them under 'mushtarak ma'nawi'.]
متواطئ (mutawāṭiʾ): A term that applies to its individuals equally (univocal); e.g., 'human' applies to Zaid, 'Amr, etc. with equal force. [Note: Stable term in logic; often contrasted with mushtarak and mushakkik.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of mushtarak lafzi as described in the passage?
A) The word 'insan' (human) applies to Zaid, Bakr, and Khalid meaning the same thing.
B) The word 'ayn' (عين) can mean eye, spring, or a spy.
C) The word 'kitab' (book) is used for any bound document.
D) The word 'shams' (sun) is a proper name for a unique entity.

Key Takeaway
Mushtarak lafzi is a word with multiple unrelated meanings due to separate word placements; it is the classic case of homonymy in Arabic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Give an example of mushtarak lafzi other than 'ayn' and explain why it fits the definition from the passage.
?
Identify whether the word 'lugha' (language) is mushtarak lafzi or mushtarak ma'nawi, and justify your answer.

What Is Mantiq?

Mushtarak Ma'nawi: Universal Term Shared Among Particulars
Author defines mushtarak and distinguishes its two types: lafzi and ma'nawi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify mushtarak ma'nawi and explain its relationship with mutawāti'.

Core Idea
Mushtarak ma'nawi is a universal meaning shared by many particulars—same as mutawāti'.

Not all 'shared' terms have multiple meanings. Some have a single meaning that applies to many individuals. This is mushtarak ma'nawi, which the author equates with univocal terms. Understanding this category is essential for accurate definition and classification in logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of mushtarak ma'nawi
The passage says it is 'the universal meaning that applies to particulars'. This means the word is placed for a single concept that many individuals share.
2
Note the synonym with mutawāti'
The author explicitly says 'as occurs with mutawāti' and mushtarak'—in this context, mushtarak ma'nawi is identical to mutawāti' (univocal). They are not opposites.
Some later texts distinguish them, but here they are merged.
3
Contrast with mushtarak lafzi
In mushtarak lafzi, the sharing is at the level of the word: one word, multiple meanings. Here, the sharing is at the level of individuals under one meaning. This is the fundamental difference.
4
Find an example
The word 'insan' (human) applies equally to Zaid, 'Amr, etc. It has a single meaning that covers all humans. That is mushtarak ma'nawi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قَوْلُهُ : فَمُشْتَرَكُ) أي : لفظي؛ نسبةً للفظ؛ لاشتراك المعاني في اللفظ الموضوع لها، ويدخل فيه العلم إذا تعدد، وأما المشترك المعنوي؛ فهو المعنى الكلي الصادق على جزئيات كما في المتواطئ والمشترك، فمعنى اللفظ فيهما مشترك معنى؛ لاشتراك الأفراد في المعنى الموضوع له اللفظ.
(His saying: 'so it is mushtarak') i.e., lafzi (verbal); attributed to the word; because the meanings share the same word placed for them, and it includes proper names when multiple [bearers]. As for mushtarak ma'nawi (meaning-based), it is the universal meaning that applies to particulars, as occurs with mutawāti' (univocal) and mushtarak, so the meaning of the word in these two [types] is shared in meaning; because the individuals share in the meaning for which the word was placed.

[Note: This usage is less common; many texts distinguish between mutawāti' and mushtarak as separate categories. Here, the author seems to merge them under 'mushtarak ma'nawi'.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between mushtarak ma'nawi and mutawāti'?
A) They are opposites.
B) Mushtarak ma'nawi is a type of mutawāti'.
C) They are the same thing.
D) Mutawāti' is a type of mushtarak ma'nawi.

Key Takeaway
Mushtarak ma'nawi is a universal term whose meaning applies equally to many particulars; it is synonymous with mutawāti' (univocal).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Defining Haqiqah and Majaz: Objections and Responses
Commentary defines haqiqah and majaz and addresses two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define haqiqah and majaz and distinguish the two objections raised against their classification, along with the logical responses provided by the commentators.

Core Idea
Haqiqah and majaz are not rigid categories; they depend on perspective and usage, and terminological differences exist among scholars.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies that when the base text says 'otherwise it is haqiqah and majaz', it does not force the second meaning to be majaz—it could be kinaya (allusion). Two objections are then raised: first, that classifying haqiqah and majaz under words with multiple meanings implies that every haqiqah has a majaz, which is false; second, that both are conditioned by usage, leaving an unaccounted category of unused words. The responses by Abd al-Hakim and Abu al-Fath refine our understanding of these terms. Let us walk through the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections
First, the classification implies that haqiqah itself has multiple meanings and that every haqiqah has a majaz—but that is not true. Second, haqiqah and majaz require usage, so words before usage fall outside the division. Notice that the first objection targets the symmetry of the pair, the second targets the exhaustiveness.
The passage states the objections via Abu al-Fath.
2
Analyze Abd al-Hakim's response to the first objection
He explains that a word can be called both haqiqah and majaz from two different perspectives: haqiqah in its original meaning, majaz in its transferred meaning. Thus haqiqah itself does not need to have multiple meanings, and the objection dissolves.
This shows that the classification is not about the word itself but about its different usages.
3
Analyze the responses to the second objection
Abd al-Hakim argues that unused words are excluded from the division because the purpose of language is communication. Abu al-Fath offers an alternative: logicians might not condition haqiqah and majaz on usage at all, due to differing terminology.
Both responses preserve the exhaustiveness of the division by either excluding irrelevant cases or redefining the condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : وَإِلَّا ، فَحَقِيْقَةٌ وَمَجَانٌ) لا يتعيَّنُ أنْ يكونَ مَجازاً، بل يحتمل أن يكون كِنايةٌ، فَلَا بُدَّ أنْ يكونَ ذلك المجازُ هُنَا على سبيلِ التَّمثيل إلى حقيقة ومجاز أو كناية، أو المرادُ مِنَ المجاز أعمُّ مِنَ المجازِ والكنايةِ مَجازا، ويجوز أنْ يكونَ المجاز عند المنطقيين أعمَّ مِنْهُمَا مِن بَابِ تَخالُفِ الاصطلاحين . قال أبو الفتح : ثم هَهُنا بَحثانِ ؛ الأَوَّلُ : أَنَّ عَدَّ الحقيقة والمجازِ مِن أقسام اللَّفْظِ الَّذِي تَعدَّدَ مَعناهُ؛ يُشعِرُ بأنَّ الحقيقةَ مِمَّا يتعدَّدُ مَعناه، وأنَّ لِكُلِّ حقيقةٍ مَجازاً، وليس كذلك، وأما أنَّ كُلَّ مَجاز له حقيقةٌ ، فَنَعَمْ، فَالإشكالُ بالنِّسبة للحقيقة، الثَّاني : أَنَّ كُلَّا مِنَ الحقيقةِ والمجاز مشروط بالاستعمال ؛ فاللَّفْظُ قبل الاستعمال ليس حقيقةً ولا مجازاً، فيبقى واسطةٌ بين الأقسام، وأجابَ عبد الحكيم عن الأوَّلِ؛ بأنَّ معنى قولهم : فَحقيقةٌ ومَجاز ؛ أي: يُسمَّى اللَّفْظُ المنقولُ بِاسمي الحقيقة والمجاز باعتبارَيْنِ، فَلَا يردُ أَنَّ الحقيقة لا يلزم أن يكون معناها كثيراً، وعَنِ الثَّاني؛ بأنَّ اللَّفْظَ المذكور لَمَّا كَانَ ساقطاً عن درجة الاعتبار ؛ لأنَّ المقصود من وضع الألفاظ الإفادة والاستفادة؛ أُسْقِطَ مِنَ التَّقسيم ا.هـ. ، وأجابَ أبو الفتح بجواز أن تكون الحقيقة والمجاز عند المنطقتين غيرَ مَشَروطيْنِ بِالاستعمالِ مِن بابِ تخالف الاصطلاحين ا.هـ. ولبعض الحواشي هنا كلامٌ تَمجُهُ الأسماعُ .
His statement: 'Otherwise it is haqiqah and majaz' does not necessitate that it is majaz, rather it could be kinaya (allusion). So that majaz here must be by way of example; either haqiqah and majaz or kinaya, or the meaning of majaz is more general including majaz and kinaya metaphorically, and it is permissible that majaz among the logicians is more general than both due to difference of terminology. Abu al-Fath said: There are two investigations here: First, that counting haqiqah and majaz among the divisions of the word that has multiple meanings implies that haqiqah has multiple meanings and that every haqiqah has a majaz, but it is not so. As for every majaz having a haqiqah, yes. So the problem is with haqiqah. Second, that both haqiqah and majaz are conditioned by usage; a word before usage is neither haqiqah nor majaz, so there remains an intermediary between the divisions. Abd al-Hakim answered the first: the meaning of their statement 'so it is haqiqah and majaz' is that the transferred word is called by the names haqiqah and majaz from two perspectives, so it does not follow that haqiqah necessarily has many meanings. And regarding the second: that the aforementioned word, since it falls below the level of consideration because the purpose of coining words is communication and understanding, is excluded from the division. Abu al-Fath answered by saying that haqiqah and majaz among the logicians are not conditioned by usage, due to difference of terminology. And some glosses here have speech that repulses the ears.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of haqiqah (literal) and majaz (metaphorical) as divisions of a word with multiple meanings. The author begins by clarifying that when the matn says 'otherwise it is haqiqah and majaz', it does not mean the second meaning is necessarily majaz; it could be kinaya (allusion). So the term 'majaz' here is used as an example, or it is taken in a broader sense to include both. The commentator notes that logicians may use 'majaz' more broadly than others. Then Abu al-Fath raises two objections. First, classifying haqiqah and majaz under words with multiple meanings suggests that haqiqah itself can have multiple meanings and that every haqiqah has a corresponding majaz, which is not true. The problem is specifically with haqiqah, because while every majaz has a haqiqah, the converse does not hold. Second, haqiqah and majaz are defined by usage; a word before usage is neither, so there is a third category (unused words) not accounted for. Abd al-Hakim answers the first objection by explaining that calling a word both haqiqah and majaz refers to two different senses: the word is haqiqah in its original meaning and majaz in its transferred meaning, so haqiqah itself does not have multiple meanings. For the second objection, he says such words are excluded because they are not used, and the purpose of language is communication. Abu al-Fath gives an alternative answer: perhaps the logicians do not condition haqiqah and majaz on usage at all, due to different terminology. The passage concludes with a remark that some marginal notes contain unacceptable speech.

Key Terms
حقيقة
مجاز
كناية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the answer to the objection that counting haqiqah and majaz under words with multiple meanings implies that haqiqah itself has multiple meanings?
A) The word is called haqiqah and majaz from two different perspectives.
B) The objector misquoted the original text.
C) Haqiqah does have multiple meanings, but that is acceptable.
D) The word in question is not a haqiqah at all.

Key Takeaway
Haqiqah and majaz are not symmetrical; every majaz has a haqiqah, but not every haqiqah has a majaz, and their classification depends on perspective and usage conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the word 'gharqad' (boxthorn) in a hadith is used as haqiqah or majaz. The hadith mentions a tree of the Jews; is that a literal or metaphorical usage?
?
Construct a scenario from Usul al-Fiqh where a word is used as haqiqah in one context and majaz in another, and explain the shift using the two perspectives from Abd al-Hakim's response.

Defining the Particular (Juz'i) in Logic
Author defines the particular as that which cannot be supposed true of many by mere conception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given concept is particular or universal based on the logical definition, and explain the role of 'mere conception' in that judgment.

Core Idea
The particular is defined by the mind's judgment of impossibility to apply to many, based solely on the concept itself.

In this passage, the author provides the precise logical definition of the particular (juz'i). Unlike everyday usage, where 'particular' might simply mean 'individual', the logician's definition hinges on a mental judgment: can the concept be supposed true of many without any external proof? Let's unpack this definition step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is 'particular' (juz'i). Note that the logicians discuss it incidentally because their main concern is the universal (kulli), its opposite.
2
Extract the definition
The definition states: 'If it is impossible to suppose it true of many by the mere occurrence of it in the mind.' This means the mind judges that the concept cannot be predicated of many individuals when considering only the concept itself, without any external proofs.
The phrase 'by the mere occurrence' (bi mujarrad husulih) is crucial: it excludes any additional evidence.
3
Understand 'supposition' (fard)
'Supposition' here means judicial supposition (al-fard al-wuqu'i), which is the mind's judgment of possibility or impossibility. It does not mean mere estimation (taqdir), because the mind can estimate impossible things without judging them to be true.
This distinction prevents the objection that one can always imagine a particular being true of many.
4
Test with an example from the passage
The example of the Necessary Being (Wajib al-Wujud) is given: when considered together with the proof of His oneness (burhan al-wahdaniyya), the concept becomes particular because it cannot be true of many. Without the proof, the concept 'Necessary Being' is universal.
This shows how the judgment of particularity depends on what is included in the mental consideration.
5
Defend against the objection
A potential objection is that no particular can be supposed true of many, but the definition holds because 'supposition' means the mind's authorization of possibility, not an actual impossibility. For example, in a conditional statement like 'If Zayd were true of many, he would not be particular', the mind can present the supposition without judging it as actual.
This mirrors the use of 'supposition' in definitions like body (jism) as 'a substance in which the supposition of three dimensions is possible'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات إمَّا جُزئي أو كُلِّيّ. [الجزئي] لأنه بمجرَّدِ حصولِهِ في العقل. قَوْلُهُ : إِمَّا جُزْئِيٌّ أَوْ كُلّي فيه إشارة إلى أنَّ الكُلِّيَّةَ والجزئيَّةَ مِن عوارض المعاني، وحينئذٍ فَوصفُ اللفظ المفردِ الدَّالّ على المعنى الكُلّي بالكُلِّيَّة، والدَّالّ على المعنى الجزئيّ بالجزئيَّةِ، مِن وصفِ الدَّالّ بوصف المدلول. ثمَّ إِنَّ بحثَ المناطقةِ عن الجزئي ليس مقصوداً بالذات، بل على سبيل الاستطراد، وذلك لأنَّهم يبحثون قصداً عن الكُلِّيّ؛ لأنَّه مبادئ التصورات، والجزئي ضد له، والضّدُّ أقرب خطوراً بالبال عندَ ذِكْرِ ضِدِّهِ، فَلِذَا بحثوا عنه. قَوْلُهُ: بِمُجَرَّدِ حُصُولِهِ الباء متعلّقة بامتنع، وإضافة مجرَّدٍ لِمَا بعدَهُ مِن إضافة الصِّفةِ لِلموصوف؛ أي: لأنَّهُ إن امتنع صدقُهُ على كثيرينَ بالنَّظرِ لحصوله في العقل المجرَّدِ عن مُلاحظةِ الأدلَّة، وإنَّما قُيّد بذلك لأنَّه لو نُوحِظَ مع حصولِهِ في العقلِ البرهان لَصارَ الكُلِّي مجزئيًا، ألا ترى أنَّ واجب الوجودِ لو لُوحِظُ معَ حصولِهِ في العقل برهان الوحدانية كانَ مُمتنعاً صدقه على كثيرين، فيكون جزئيا. قَوْلُهُ : إِنِ امْتَنَعَ فَرْضُ صِدْقِهِ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ بِمُجَرَّدِ حُصُولِهِ فِي الْعَقْلِ. قَوْلُهُ: إِنِ امْتَنَعَ أي : استحال فرضُ صدقِهِ، المراد بالفرض هنا الفرضُ الوقوعيُّ الرَّاجِعُ لِلحُكم، فالمعنى أنَّه استحال أن يحكم العقل بصدقه على كثيرين، وليس المراد بالفرض هنا التَّقدير لأنَّ العقل يفرضُ المحال ويقدِّرُه أي لأجل أن يتصوَّره وَلَا يحكم به أصلاً. قَوْلُهُ : عِنْدَ العَقْلِ ظرفٌ لِلامتناع، ولم يعبر بِهِ فِي سابِقِهِ لأنَّ السَّابِقَ في حصول المعنى، وهو حاصل في النَّفْسِ بطريق الارتسام، وما هنا في حكم العقل، وهو حاصل بطريق الملاحظة لا الارتسام. قَوْلُهُ : فَرْضُ صِدْقِهِ أي : حَمْلُهُ حملَ مُواطأة لأنَّهُ المعتبر هنا، قال عبدُ الحكيم أي: يجوزُ حمله إيجاباً دونَ التَّقديرِ والاعتبارِ كَمَا في تعريف المتصلة حيثُ قَالُوا صِدْقُ التّالي على فَرْضِ صِدْقِ المقدَّم، فإنَّ لِلعقل تقدير كُلِّ شيءٍ، ولو لم يكن له تقديرُ الصِّدقِ في الجزئي وتصوُّرُهُ كيفَ يحكم يسليه عنه؟ ا.هـ. أي فَالفرضُ المأخوذُ في تعريف الجزئي بمعنى التَّجويز أي محكم العقل بالجواز لا بمعنى التقدير المعتبر في مقدَّمِ الشَّرطيَّةِ، واستعمالُ الفَرْضِ بهذا المعنى الشَّائِعِ في كَمَا في تعريفهم الجسم بأنَّهُ جوهرٌ يمكنُ فرْضُ الأبعادِ الثَّلاثةِ فِيهِ فَلَا يرد على تعريف الجزئي أنَّه لا يصدقُ على شيءٍ مِنَ الجزئيات إذْ مَا مِن مُجُزئيّ إِلَّا ويمكنُ فرضُ صِدقِهِ على كثيرين، فإنَّهُ يقعُ مقدَّم الشَّرطيَّةِ المبنية على فرضِ الصِّدقِ، وتقديرُهُ كَمَا في قولِكَ إنْ كانَ زيدٌ صادقاً على كثيرين لم يكن جزئيا، ويقعُ أيضاً تالياً لِتِلكَ القضيَّةِ كَقولِكَ إن لم يكن زيد جزئيا كان صادِقًا على كثيرين.
Section: On the Principles of Concepts. Either particular or universal. [The particular] because by the mere occurrence of it in the mind. His saying: 'Either particular or universal' contains an indication that universality and particularity are accidents of meanings, and then describing the singular word indicating the universal meaning as universal, and that indicating the particular meaning as particular, is describing the indicator by the attribute of the indicated. Then, the logicians' investigation of the particular is not intended in itself, but by way of digression, because they intentionally investigate the universal, as it is the principle of concepts, and the particular is its opposite, and the opposite is most likely to come to mind when its opposite is mentioned, so they investigated it. His saying: 'by the mere occurrence of it' – the preposition 'by' is attached to 'is impossible', and adding 'mere' to what follows is the addition of the adjective to the described; meaning: because it is impossible for it to be true of many with consideration of its occurrence in the mind stripped of observing proofs. It was restricted by that only because if it were observed together with its occurrence in the mind of the proof, the universal would become particular. Do you not see that if the Necessary Being were observed together with its occurrence in the mind of the proof of oneness, its being true of many would be impossible, so it would be particular. His saying: 'If it is impossible to suppose it true of many by the mere occurrence of it in the mind.' His saying: 'If it is impossible' means: the supposition of its truth is impossible; what is meant by 'supposition' here is the actual supposition that returns to judgment, so the meaning is that it is impossible for the mind to judge it as true of many, and what is meant by 'supposition' here is not estimation, because the mind supposes the impossible and estimates it, i.e., in order to conceive it, and does not judge by it at all. His saying: 'at the mind' is an adverb for impossibility, and he did not express it in its predecessor because the predecessor is in the occurrence of the meaning, and it occurs in the soul by way of impression, while what is here is in the judgment of the mind, and it occurs by way of observation, not impression. His saying: 'supposition of its truth' means: predicating it with a predication of agreement, because that is what is considered here. 'Abd al-Hakim said: It is permissible to predicate it positively without estimation and consideration, as in the definition of the conditional where they said: 'the truth of the consequent on the supposition of the truth of the antecedent', for the mind estimates everything, and if it had no estimation of truth in the particular and its conception, how would it judge about its negation? End quote. So the supposition taken in the definition of the particular is in the sense of authorization, i.e., the mind's judgment of permissibility, not in the sense of estimation considered in the antecedent of the conditional. And the use of 'supposition' in this common sense is as in their definition of body as 'a substance in which the supposition of three dimensions is possible', so there is no objection to the definition of the particular that it is not true of any particular, because there is no particular except that it is possible to suppose it true of many, for it occurs as the antecedent of the conditional built on the supposition of truth, and its estimation is like your saying: 'If Zayd were true of many, he would not be particular', and it also occurs as the consequent of that proposition, like your saying: 'If Zayd were not particular, he would be true of many.'
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage defines the particular (جزئي) in the context of logic. It begins by stating that the principles of concepts are either particular or universal. The commentary notes that universality and particularity are accidental attributes of meanings, not of words directly; rather, words are described as universal or particular by virtue of the meanings they indicate. The particular is discussed only incidentally because the logicians' main concern is the universal, which is the basis of concepts, and the particular is its opposite. The core definition is: 'If it is impossible to suppose it true of many by the mere occurrence of it in the mind.' The commentator explains that 'by the mere occurrence' means the mind considers the concept in itself, stripped of any external proofs. For example, if the concept of the Necessary Being is considered together with the proof of His oneness, it becomes particular because it cannot be true of many. The term 'supposition' (فرض) here means judicial supposition (الفرض الوقوعي) – the mind's judgment of possibility – not mere estimation (تقدير), because the mind can estimate impossible things without judging them true. The commentator also clarifies that 'supposition of its truth' means predicating it with a predication of agreement (حمل مواطأة). The passage then defends the definition against a potential objection: that no particular can be supposed true of many. It explains that by 'supposition' is meant the mind's authorization of the possibility, not an actual impossibility, as in the definition of body where 'supposition' is used in a similar sense. Thus, the definition holds, as Zayd can be supposed true of many in a conditional statement like 'If Zayd were true of many...'

Key Terms
جزئي (juz'i): A concept that cannot be judged true of many individuals by the mind based solely on its conception, without external proofs. [Note: No significant shift; this definition is consistent across later logical texts.]
كلي (kulli): A concept that can be predicated of many individuals; the opposite of particular. [Note: No significant shift.]
فرض (fard): Here, judicial supposition (الفرض الوقوعي) meaning the mind's judgment of possibility, not mere estimation (تقدير) which allows impossible things. [Note: In earlier texts, فرض sometimes means mental estimation; here it is specifically distinguished as judgment.]
مجرّد حصوله (mujarrad husulih): The mere occurrence of the concept in the mind without any additional considerations or proofs. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which example from the passage illustrates how a universal concept becomes particular when considered together with a proof?
A) The concept 'horse' when considered with the proof that horses are animals.
B) The concept 'Necessary Being' when considered with the proof of His oneness.
C) The concept 'Zayd' when considered with the proof that he is a human.
D) The concept 'body' when considered with the proof that it has three dimensions.

Key Takeaway
The particular is defined by the mind's judgment of impossibility to be true of many, based solely on the concept itself, not by external proofs or mere estimation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Determine whether the concept 'human' (insan) is particular or universal according to the definition. Explain your reasoning by checking if the mind can judge it true of many by mere conception, without any external proofs.
?
Task 2: Consider the following definition from a fiqh text: 'Salah is a specific set of actions and words.' Does this definition use the 'mere conception' criterion? Why or why not? How would the logical definition of particularity apply to the concept 'salah'?

Definition of Universality and Particularity
Author defines universality and particularity and refutes an objection about multiple minds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the definition of universality and particularity and defend it against the objection of multiple minds.

Core Idea
Universality is about logical possibility, not actual multiplicity of mental images.

In this passage, the author defines the fundamental concepts of universality and particularity, the building blocks of conceptual knowledge. Al-Dasuqi then expands on the definitions and addresses a classic objection: if a particular concept is conceived by many people, doesn't that make it universal? The answer reveals a crucial distinction about what it means for a concept to be shared.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions
Find where the author defines universality and particularity. What exact phrase does he use?
The author says: 'Universality is the possibility of assuming sharing, and particularity is its impossibility.'
2
Understand 'possibility of assuming sharing'
Why does the definition use 'possibility' (imkan) rather than actual sharing? What does that imply about universality?
It means the mind judges that the concept could apply to many, even if in reality there is only one instance.
3
Extract the objection
What is the objection? How does it try to prove that every particular could be universal?
The objection: If a group conceives a particular (e.g., Zayd), the mental forms match, so it would be universal.
4
Analyze the response
How does al-Dasuqi distinguish between mental forms considered abstractly and those considered with relation to their loci?
If taken abstractly, they are one, not two separate things; if taken with relation to minds, they are incompatible.
5
Apply the definition
Test a concrete example: Is 'Zayd' a universal or particular? Why? Apply the definition.
Zayd is particular because it is impossible for his essence to be shared among many individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات
(فَكُلِّي).
فالكلِّيَّةُ إمكانُ فرضِ الاشتراك، والجزئية استحالته
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ: فَكُلّيّ) وهو الذي يتركَّبُ منه طريقُ التَّصورِ الموصلة إليه، وقدَّمَها وأخويها (١) على الطَّريقِ الموصلة للتّصديقِ ؛ لتقدم التصورِ على التصديق طبعاً كما تقدَّم .
(قَوْلُهُ : فَالْكُلِّيَّةُ) أي : الَّتي هي وصفُ الكُلّي، والجزئية ، هي وصفُ الجزئي المذكورين في كلام المصنّف ، وقوله : (إمكان) فرضِ الاشتراك)؛ أي: إمكان حكمِ العقل على المعنى بأنَّهُ مُشترك بين كثيرين .
وأوردَ عليه : أنَّ كلَّ جزئي إذا تصوَّرَهُ طائفةٌ ؛ فالصُّورة الحاصلة في ذهن زيدٍ مثلاً ؛ مُطابقةٌ لِلصُّورة الحاصلة في ذهن الآخرين، فيجبُ أنْ يكونَ كُلِّيًّا والجواب : أنَّ معنى شركة الكثير : أن يكونَ الكثيرون أفراده، ويعتبر هو مطابقاً لها وصادقاً عليها، والصُّورتان الحاصلتان في ذهن زيدٍ وعمرو إنْ أُخِذَنَا معَ قطعِ النَّظر عن الإضافة إلى المحلّين ؛ فهما مُتَّحدانِ بالذَّاتِ والمفهوم ولا اثنينيَّة ؛ أي: لا تعدُّدَ بينهما حتَّى يتحقَّق المطابقة، وإن أخذنًا مع اعتبار الإضافة إلى المحلّين؛ فَلَا تتم المطابقةُ والتَّصادِقُ بينهما لِمَا بينَ تلكَ الصُّورِ مِن التَّباين.
Chapter on the principles of concepts (the universal). Universality is the possibility of assuming sharing, and particularity is its impossibility. al-Dasuqi: His statement: (the universal) - it is that from which the path of conception leading to it is composed, and he gave precedence to it and its two siblings over the path leading to assent because conception precedes assent by nature as previously mentioned. His statement: (universality) - that which is the description of the universal, and particularity is the description of the particular mentioned in the author's words. His statement: (possibility of assuming sharing) meaning: the possibility of the mind judging the meaning as shared among many. It was objected: every particular, when a group conceives it, the form in Zayd's mind matches the form in others' minds, so it must be universal. The answer: The meaning of sharing among many is that the many are its individuals, and it is considered as matching and true of them. The two forms in Zayd and 'Amr's minds: if taken without regard to their relation to the two loci, they are identical in essence and concept with no duality, i.e., no multiplicity between them such that matching would be realized. If taken with regard to the relation to the two loci, then matching and truthfulness are not realized between them due to the incompatibility between those forms.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage defines universality (kulliyya) and particularity (juz'iyya) in the context of the principles of concepts. The author states that universality is the possibility of assuming sharing (imkan fard al-ishtirak), while particularity is its impossibility. Al-Dasuqi explains that universality is a description of the universal concept, and particularity is a description of the particular. The possibility of assuming sharing means the mind can judge that the meaning is shared among many individuals. Then an objection is raised: if every particular concept is conceived by a group, the mental forms in each mind seem to match, so the particular would be universal. The response distinguishes: the sharing of many means that the many are individuals falling under the concept, and the concept is true of them. The two mental forms in Zayd and 'Amr, if considered abstracted from their relation to the minds, are one in essence and concept, not numerically distinct, so matching is not between two separate things. If considered with relation to the minds, they are distinct and cannot match. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
كُلِّي
كُلِّيَّة
جُزئي
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The actual sharing of a concept among many minds.
B) The possibility of assuming that a concept is shared among many individuals.
C) The mental image that matches between two persons.
D) The impossibility of applying a concept to more than one.

Key Takeaway
Universality is defined by the logical possibility of multiple individuals falling under a concept, not by the accidental similarity of mental images across minds.

The Real Particular: That Which Prevents Sharing
Definition of the real particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the real particular (al-juz'i al-haqiqi) and explain why it cannot be shared in conception.

Core Idea
The real particular is unique: its very concept excludes sharing.

The passage begins by clarifying the term 'particular' in logic. It first defines the real particular, which is the foundation for understanding individuals and singular terms. This definition is crucial for distinguishing between concepts that can be predicated of many and those that cannot.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic text: الجزئي الحقيقي (the real particular).
2
Extract the definition
The definition is: 'that which prevents its own conception from occurring in it [sharing]'. Understand that this means the concept itself makes it impossible for it to apply to more than one thing.
3
Contrast with the relative particular
Note that the relative particular (al-juz'i al-idafi) is different: it can be shared (e.g., 'human' is shared by many individuals). The real particular is exclusive.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider 'Zayd' as a real particular: the concept of Zayd refers only to that individual, not to anyone else. Contrast with 'human', which is a universal.
This distinction is vital in Kalam when discussing specific entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات. أي: كما يقال: الجزئي للجزئي الحقيقي المذكور، وهو الذي يمنع نفس تصوره من وقوع الشركة فيه. وقال السيد في حاشيته على شرح المطالع: المشهور أن الكلي له مفهوم واحد يقابل الجزئي الحقيقي تقابل العدم والملكة، ويقابل الجزئي الإضافي تقابل التضايف. واعترضه مير زاهد بأن المفهوم الواحد لا يمكن أن يقابل بمفهوم تقابل العدم والملكة، وهو بعينه مقابل لمفهوم آخر تقابل التضايف؛ ضرورة أنه على الأول: معنى غير إضافي، وعلى الثاني معنى إضافي، فالحق أن للكلي معنى واحدا يقابل الجزئي الحقيقي تقابل العدم والملكة، وللجزئي معنيين: أحدهما مقابل للأعم من شيء تقابل التضايف، والثاني يقابل الكلي تقابل العدم والملكة.
Chapter: On the principles of concepts. That is: as it is said: the particular (al-juz'i) is for the real particular mentioned, and it is that which prevents its own conception from occurring in it [i.e., sharing]. And al-Sayyid said in his gloss on Sharh al-Matali': The well-known [view] is that the universal has one concept which opposes the real particular in the opposition of privation and possession, and opposes the relative particular in the opposition of correlation. Mir Zahid objected: A single concept cannot oppose one concept in the opposition of privation and possession, and at the same time oppose another concept in the opposition of correlation; necessarily because on the first [opposition] it is a non-relational meaning, and on the second it is a relational meaning. So the truth is that the universal has one meaning which opposes the real particular in the opposition of privation and possession, and the particular has two meanings: one opposes the more general of something in the opposition of correlation, and the second opposes the universal in the opposition of privation and possession.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage defines the particular (al-juz'i) and its two types. It begins as a chapter on the principles of concepts. The first definition states that the real particular (al-juz'i al-haqiqi) is that which by its very conception prevents sharing, meaning it cannot be predicated of many. The commentary then transitions to a debate between al-Sayyid and Mir Zahid regarding whether the universal has one meaning or two. Al-Sayyid states the well-known view: the universal has one concept that opposes the real particular in the opposition of privation and possession (like sight and blindness), and opposes the relative particular (al-juz'i al-idafi) in the opposition of correlation (like father and son). Mir Zahid objects that a single concept cannot have two different types of opposition. The resolution is that the universal has only one meaning opposing the real particular in privation/possession, while the particular has two meanings: one is relative (opposing the more general by correlation), and the other is real (opposing the universal by privation/possession). This clarifies the definitions and avoids confusion.

Key Terms
الجزئي الحقيقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It can be predicated of many things.
B) Its very conception prevents sharing.
C) It is relative to a more general term.
D) It has two meanings.

Key Takeaway
The real particular is defined by its inability to be shared in conception.

The Relative Particular and the Twofold Meaning of Particular
Definition of the relative particular and the two meanings of particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the relative particular (al-juz'i al-idafi) from the real particular and explain the two meanings of the term 'particular'.

Core Idea
The particular can mean both the individual and the more specific term relative to a more general one.

The passage also introduces the relative particular, which is a term that is more specific than another. This expands the concept of 'particular' beyond individuals to include relative specificity, which is essential for understanding the hierarchy of universals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the relative particular
Find the term الجزئي الإضافي in the passage. It is defined implicitly as the more specific (al-akhas) relative to something else.
2
Understand the opposition of correlation
The relative particular opposes the more general term in the opposition of correlation (taqabul al-tadha'if), meaning they are interdependent (e.g., father and son).
3
Contrast with real particular
While the real particular cannot be shared, the relative particular can be shared (e.g., 'human' is a relative particular compared to 'animal', but it can be predicated of many individuals).
4
Apply to Islamic science
In fiqh, 'salat al-fajr' is a relative particular compared to 'salat'. Understanding this helps in classifying actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات. أي: كما يقال: الجزئي للجزئي الحقيقي المذكور، وهو الذي يمنع نفس تصوره من وقوع الشركة فيه. وقال السيد في حاشيته على شرح المطالع: المشهور أن الكلي له مفهوم واحد يقابل الجزئي الحقيقي تقابل العدم والملكة، ويقابل الجزئي الإضافي تقابل التضايف. واعترضه مير زاهد بأن المفهوم الواحد لا يمكن أن يقابل بمفهوم تقابل العدم والملكة، وهو بعينه مقابل لمفهوم آخر تقابل التضايف؛ ضرورة أنه على الأول: معنى غير إضافي، وعلى الثاني معنى إضافي، فالحق أن للكلي معنى واحدا يقابل الجزئي الحقيقي تقابل العدم والملكة، وللجزئي معنيين: أحدهما مقابل للأعم من شيء تقابل التضايف، والثاني يقابل الكلي تقابل العدم والملكة.
Chapter: On the principles of concepts. That is: as it is said: the particular (al-juz'i) is for the real particular mentioned, and it is that which prevents its own conception from occurring in it [i.e., sharing]. And al-Sayyid said in his gloss on Sharh al-Matali': The well-known [view] is that the universal has one concept which opposes the real particular in the opposition of privation and possession, and opposes the relative particular in the opposition of correlation. Mir Zahid objected: A single concept cannot oppose one concept in the opposition of privation and possession, and at the same time oppose another concept in the opposition of correlation; necessarily because on the first [opposition] it is a non-relational meaning, and on the second it is a relational meaning. So the truth is that the universal has one meaning which opposes the real particular in the opposition of privation and possession, and the particular has two meanings: one opposes the more general of something in the opposition of correlation, and the second opposes the universal in the opposition of privation and possession.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage defines the particular (al-juz'i) and its two types. It begins as a chapter on the principles of concepts. The first definition states that the real particular (al-juz'i al-haqiqi) is that which by its very conception prevents sharing, meaning it cannot be predicated of many. The commentary then transitions to a debate between al-Sayyid and Mir Zahid regarding whether the universal has one meaning or two. Al-Sayyid states the well-known view: the universal has one concept that opposes the real particular in the opposition of privation and possession (like sight and blindness), and opposes the relative particular (al-juz'i al-idafi) in the opposition of correlation (like father and son). Mir Zahid objects that a single concept cannot have two different types of opposition. The resolution is that the universal has only one meaning opposing the real particular in privation/possession, while the particular has two meanings: one is relative (opposing the more general by correlation), and the other is real (opposing the universal by privation/possession). This clarifies the definitions and avoids confusion.

Key Terms
الجزئي الإضافي

A) Privation and possession
B) Correlation (tadha'if)

Key Takeaway
The relative particular is defined by its relation to a more general term, not by its inability to be shared.

The Debate on the Universal's Opposition: One Meaning or Two?
Scholarly debate on the universal's opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the debate between al-Sayyid and Mir Zahid about the universal's meaning and explain the resolution.

Core Idea
The universal has one meaning opposing the real particular, while the particular has two meanings.

The passage concludes with a scholarly dispute. Al-Sayyid claimed the universal has one concept that opposes both types of particular differently. Mir Zahid objected that a single concept cannot have two different types of opposition. The resolution clarifies the meanings of both universal and particular.

Guided Path
1
State al-Sayyid's claim
Al-Sayyid says the universal has one concept that opposes the real particular by privation/possession and the relative particular by correlation.
2
Identify Mir Zahid's objection
Mir Zahid objects: one concept cannot have two different relationships; one is non-relational, the other relational.
3
Find the resolution
The truth: the universal has only one meaning (opposing real particular by privation/possession), while the particular has two meanings.
4
Evaluate the logic
Consider whether the resolution satisfies both sides: it preserves the universal's unity and accounts for the two types of particular.
This is a standard pattern in Islamic logic: clarifying conceptual ambiguity.

فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات. أي: كما يقال: الجزئي للجزئي الحقيقي المذكور، وهو الذي يمنع نفس تصوره من وقوع الشركة فيه. وقال السيد في حاشيته على شرح المطالع: المشهور أن الكلي له مفهوم واحد يقابل الجزئي الحقيقي تقابل العدم والملكة، ويقابل الجزئي الإضافي تقابل التضايف. واعترضه مير زاهد بأن المفهوم الواحد لا يمكن أن يقابل بمفهوم تقابل العدم والملكة، وهو بعينه مقابل لمفهوم آخر تقابل التضايف؛ ضرورة أنه على الأول: معنى غير إضافي، وعلى الثاني معنى إضافي، فالحق أن للكلي معنى واحدا يقابل الجزئي الحقيقي تقابل العدم والملكة، وللجزئي معنيين: أحدهما مقابل للأعم من شيء تقابل التضايف، والثاني يقابل الكلي تقابل العدم والملكة.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate

Genus: Definition and Predication Types
Author defines genus and explains the three types of predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of genus (jins), distinguish identity predication (haml muwaata'ah) from derivative and composite predication, and identify which predication type is used in a given definitional statement.

Core Idea
Genus is defined by identity predication, where predicate and subject share the same essence but differ in concept.

In Islamic logic, every definition hinges on the precision of its terms. Genus is the first of the five universals, and its definition rests on how it is predicated. In this lesson, we unpack the classical definition of genus and the three types of predication that clarify how genus applies to its species. Understanding these distinctions is essential for constructing correct definitions in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the definiendum 'jins' in the Arabic passage. The author introduces it as 'al-awwal: al-jins'. This is the term we will define.
2
Extract the core definition
The definition is: 'al-maqul 'ala al-kathrah al-mukhtalifah al-haqiqah fi jawab ma huwa'. Break it into components: 'maqul' (predicated), 'ala al-kathrah' (of many), 'al-mukhtalifah al-haqiqah' (differing in reality), 'fi jawab ma huwa' (in answer to 'what is it?').
Each component distinguishes genus from other universals.
3
Understand the predication type: haml muwaata'ah
The commentator specifies that 'al-maqul' means predicate by identity predication (haml muwaata'ah). This is the predication where subject and predicate are identical in essence but differ in concept, e.g., 'Zayd is standing'. Distinguish this from derivative predication (haml ishtiqaq), e.g., 'Zayd is a striker', and composite predication (haml tarkib), e.g., 'Zayd is a possessor of property'.
Only identity predication is relevant for genus definitions.
4
Check if the definition is a hadd or rasm
Al-Dasuqi states that this definition is a rasm (description) because 'being said of many' is an accidental property of genus, not constitutive. Genus in itself is an essential universal; its predication of many is an accident. This distinction is crucial for evaluating definitional quality.
A true limit (hadd) would define by essential, not accidental, properties.
5
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider the statement 'The human is an animal'. This is identity predication because 'animal' and 'human' share the same essence (animality) but differ in concept. If we said 'Zayd is a scholar', that is derivative (from 'scholarship'). If we said 'The wealthy man is a possessor of wealth', that is composite. Practice identifying the type in fiqh definitions.
In Usul al-Fiqh, defining 'riba' requires identity predication of its genus, e.g., 'Riba is an exchange of foodstuffs with delay'.

وذلك لأنَّ الجنس في نَفْسِهِ هو الكُلّي الذَّاتي; سواء كانَ يُقال على الحقائقِ أَمْ لا، وأمَّا مَقوليّتُهُ عليها، وكونه صالحاً لذلك ; فَمِمَّا يعرضُ لها بعد تقويمها . (قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى الْكَثْرَةِ) أي على ذي الكثرة; أي على الأفراد المتصفة بالكثرة; بمعنى الزّيادة على الواحد، فإذا قِيلَ : مَا هو الإنسانُ والفرس؟ أو قِيلَ : مَا الإنسان، والفرس، والبغل، والحمار؟ قيل في الجواب: حيوان; لأنَّ مَا يُسأَلُ بها عن تمام المشترك بين الأمور وتمام المشترك بين الحقائق المذكورة; الحيوان. Section: On the Principles of Concepts
[Genus]
(The first: genus: it is what is said of many differing realities in answer to 'what is it?')
Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: 'it is what is said') i.e., the predicate by way of identity predication (haml muwaata'ah), which is predication of identity, such as when it is said 'Zayd is standing', so one judges regarding it of difference according to the mind, and unity according to the external, because what is considered in the universality of the universal absolutely, whether it is genus or other, is this predication, not derivative predication (haml ishtiqaq), which is predication of the root by means of predication of the derived term, like predication of 'striking' concerning Zayd in 'Zayd is a striker', conveying his performance of it by means of predicting 'striker' of him; nor composite predication (haml tarkib), which is predication of 'possessor of', like predication of 'property' concerning Zayd in 'Zayd is a possessor of property', conveying his attachment to it by means of predicting this composite phrase of him.
And his saying: ('it is what is said') i.e., the predicate, i.e., that which is suitable for being predicated, and this definition is a description (rasm), and it is only a description because the universal, even if it is a genus, yet being said of many is an accidental matter for it, not constitutive (muqawwim) of it, and it is only mentioned so that the phrase 'of such' or 'in answer to such' may be attached to it.
Al-Attar: ... restricts it to what excludes the comprehensive matters and their opposites, and some generalized them; said by Abd al-Hakim. I only transmitted his statement in its entirety for two reasons: first, for the generality of its benefit, and second, as an alert to what occurred in some glosses here, for he took it and distributed it in places, silent about attribution. (His saying: 'The first: genus') is an Arabic word, meaning al-darb (kind), and it is more general than species according to al-Sihah; and what the commentary of al-Matali' suggests that it is Greek does not correspond to reality. (His saying: 'it is what is said') i.e., the predicate by identity predication, because it is what is considered in the chapter of universals, as is its reality according to the shaykh; and in al-Asas it is shared between predication of identity and predication of 'possessor of', which includes composite predication and derivative predication. And since there is disagreement whether these definitions are limits or descriptions, and preferring one side cannot be known except by knowing that...
(His saying: 'according to the mind... etc.') By 'mind' is meant the concept. They have stipulated two conditions for identity predication: difference in concept to be beneficial, and unity of essence in the external to be correct, for what is distinct is not predicated of what is distinct from it. And what is reported of unity in essence and concept like 'poetry is poetry' is interpreted. The prevalence of this predication over its two companions repels the objection that the shared term is included in the definition. End quote by al-Sharnubi.
And that is because genus in itself is the essential universal, whether it is said of realities or not; as for its being predicated of them and being suitable for that, it is among things that accrue to it after its constitution. (His saying: 'of many') i.e., of the possessor of multiplicity, i.e., of individuals described by multiplicity, meaning more than one. So if it is said: 'What is the human and the horse?' or it is said: 'What is the human, the horse, the mule, and the donkey?' it is said in answer: 'animal', because that by which one asks about the complete commonality between the matters and the complete commonality between the mentioned realities is 'animal'. (His saying: 'differing reality') excludes the true species, their proximate differentiae, and their properties.
And his saying: ('in answer') excludes the common accident, for it is not said in the answer.
Al-Attar: The term is the coining of words for any meaning, and for anything considered in the concept of the word, and that is difficult. The author took the more cautious route and remained silent about whether they are limits or descriptions. In the author's commentary on the original, this definition is a description, because being predicated is accidental, and definition by accident is a description; he mentioned the attachment of 'of many' and 'in answer to such'. End quote. And in the commentary of al-Jalal there is what suggests it is a nominal limit.
(His saying: 'of many') Al-Harawi said: He only used the word 'many' (kathrah) as opposed to oneness, rather than 'many things' (kathirin), to indicate that the inclusion of two different species in it is sufficient. (His saying: 'differing reality') in the individuals, and in another version: realities (haqa'iq) in the plural. Every plural in this art refers to what is more than one, as has been stated. So it does not exclude from the definition what is said of two realities, if the genus is confined to two species, but it excludes the genus confined to a single reality of two [aspects?]. The better view is that by 'realities' is meant their genus, so it includes the single reality, on the basis that every universal has potential individuals, even if in the external it has only two or one individual, for example; the plural is with respect to those potential individuals. If it is said: Reality (haqiqah) is the quiddity existing in the external, then what is said of many differing quiddities, without the reality, from genera not existing in the external would be excluded from the definition. It is answered that the restriction of 'reality' to existing quiddity is only initially understood in...
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage defines genus (jins) and explains the type of predication used in definitions. The author begins by stating that genus is the first of the principles of concepts. The definition given is: 'Genus is what is said of many differing realities in answer to 'what is it?''. The commentary by al-Dasuqi then clarifies each part.

First, the phrase 'what is said' (al-maqul) means the predicate, but specifically by way of identity predication (haml muwaata'ah). This is the predication where the subject and predicate are identical in essence but differ in concept, like 'Zayd is standing'. In the mind, we distinguish between the concept of Zayd and the concept of standing, but externally they are the same entity. This is distinguished from derivative predication (haml ishtiqaq), where the predicate is derived from a root, e.g., 'Zayd is a striker' implies striking is a property of Zayd. And from composite predication (haml tarkib), where the predicate is a phrase like 'possessor of property', conveying possession. In the chapter of universals, only identity predication is relevant.

Al-Dasuqi then explains that this definition is a description (rasm) rather than a true limit (hadd) because 'being said of many' is an accidental property of genus, not constitutive. Genus is in itself an essential universal; its being predicated of many is something that comes later. The definition includes the phrases 'on many' and 'in answer to' to distinguish genus from other universals.

Al-Attar's commentary expands further. He notes that some scholars restrict the definition, while others generalize. The word 'jins' is Arabic, meaning kind, and is more general than species. He confirms that the predication considered in universals is identity predication, though some sources also include other types.

There is a note by al-Sharnubi on identity predication: it requires difference in concept (to be informative) and unity of essence in the external (to be correct). For example, 'poetry is poetry' is tautological and only apparent unity.

Finally, the passage elaborates on 'al-kathrah' (multiplicity). 'Many' means more than one individual. Examples: if asked 'What are human and horse?' or 'What are human, horse, mule, donkey?', the answer is 'animal' because animal comprehends all. 'Differing reality' excludes species and their differentiae and properties. 'In answer' excludes common accident, like 'white' for snow and cloth, because white is not said in answer to 'what is it?'.

Al-Attar then discusses whether the definition is a limit or description, leaning toward description because predication is accidental. He also discusses the plural 'realities'—even a genus with only one species can have potential individuals, so the plural is justified.

Throughout, the passage emphasizes the precision needed in defining logical terms and the types of predication that apply.

Key Terms
الجنس (al-jins)
المقول (al-maqool)
حمل مواطأة (haml muwaata'ah)
حمل اشتقاق (haml ishtiqaq)
حمل تركيب (haml tarkib)
رسم (rasm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following statements uses derivative predication (haml ishtiqaq) as described in the passage?
A) Zayd is standing
B) Zayd is a striker
C) Zayd is a possessor of property
D) The human is an animal

Key Takeaway
Genus is defined by identity predication where the predicate and subject share the same essence, differing only in concept; this distinguishes it from derivative and composite predications.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

For each, state whether it is identity, derivative, or composite predication.

Genus as Part of Quiddity and Its Priority
Defines genus, explains its priority over specific difference and accident, and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus in any definition and explain why it is given priority over specific difference and universal accident in the order of definition.

Core Idea
Genus is a part of the quiddity; what is internal is prior to what is external.

In defining things, we ask 'What is it?' (ال ماهية). The answer must include the genus because genus is part of the essence. Other predicates like specific difference (rational) and universal accident (laughing) are external to the quiddity. This lesson explains why genus is logically prior.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase 'What is it?' in the passage.
The passage begins with the definitional question 'ما هو؟' (What is it?). This question is answered by the genus and species together. Note that genus alone is not the full answer; it is a part.
The full answer includes genus and differentia.
2
Extract the definition of genus from the passage.
Genus is defined as a part of the quiddity (الجنس جزء لها). It is internal (داخل) while specific difference and universal accident are external (خارجان).
Memorize: internal vs external.
3
Understand the priority rule.
The internal is given priority over the external. So genus comes before specific difference and universal accident in the order of definition. This is because the essence is defined first by its broad category, then by its distinguishing feature.
Example: In defining human, we say 'animal' first, then 'rational'.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example.
Consider the definition of 'salah' in fiqh. Its genus is 'actions and sayings' that include takbir and taslim. The genus 'actions' encompasses many types; the differentia 'with takbir and taslim' distinguishes salah. So genus is prior.
In fiqh definitions, the genus is often implicit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات. "ما هو؟" قَدَّمَ الجنس على الخاصَّة والعَرَض العام؛ لأنَّهما خارجان عن الماهية، والجنس جُزْءٌ لها. وقوله: (ما هو؟) يخرج الفصول البعيدة، وسائر الخواص، ما عدا خواص الأنواع، فإنَّ شيئاً منها لا يُقالُ في جوابِ ما هو؟. وقوله: والجنس جزء لها أي: فهو داخل فيها، وهما خارجان عنها، والداخل مُقدَّم على الخارج. ثم بيان أن الجنس يصدق عليه حين كونه مقولاً على مختلفين أنه مقول على متفقين أعني الحصص، فلا بد من قيد الحيثية. وظهر من هذا قول أبي الفتح: إن كل كلّي له أفراد في نفس الأمر، فهو نوع حقيقي بالقياس إلى حصصه المضافة إلى تلك الأفراد، وإن كان بالقياس إلى تلك الأفراد واحداً من الأقسام الباقية. مثلاً: الحيوان جنس بالقياس إلى الأفراد الإنسانية أو الفرسية، ونوع بالقياس إلى حصصه المضافة إليها. وحينئذ فلا بد من اعتبار قيد الحيثية في تعاريفها احترازاً عن مادة الاجتماع. وقيل: كون الجنس جزءاً للماهية ومقولاً عليها غير معقول؛ لأن الجزء يتقدم على الكل في الوجودين، والمحمول متحد الوجود بالموضوع في الخارج. قلنا: ليس المراد أن الخبر محمول من حيث إنه جزء يكون محمولاً، بل المراد أن معروض الجزئية هو معروض المحمولية. مثلاً: الحيوان المأخوذ بشرط أن يدخل فيه الناطق نوع، وبشرط أن لا يدخل فيه جزء، والمأخوذ بحيث يمكن أن تعرض له الجزئية والنوعية. وقدَّم الجنس على الفصل لاحتياجنا في معرفة الفصل القريب والبعيد إلى الجنس. وعلى النوع لتوقف معرفة قسم من النوع وهو النوع الإضافي على الجنس. وترك من تعريف الجنس وسائر الكليات لفظ "الكلِّي"؛ لأن المقول على الكثرة مغن عنه.
Chapter: On the principles of concepts. "What is it?" He gave priority to genus over specific difference and universal accident because both are external to the quiddity, while genus is a part of it. And his saying: "What is it?" excludes remote differentiae and all properties except the properties of species, for none of them is said in answer to "What is it?". And his saying: "Genus is a part of it" means it is internal to it, while the two are external, and the internal is given priority over the external. Then an explanation that genus, when it is predicated of different things, is also predicated of agreeing things, namely the individual cases, so a qualification of aspect is necessary. From this emerges the saying of Abu al-Fath: Every universal that has individuals in reality is a true species relative to its sub-parts added to those individuals, even if relative to those individuals it is one of the remaining divisions. For example: animal is a genus relative to human or horse individuals, and a species relative to its sub-parts added to them. Therefore, it is necessary to consider the qualification of aspect in their definitions to avoid the material of commonality. It is said: The fact that genus is part of the quiddity and predicated upon it is unintelligible because the part precedes the whole in both existences, while the predicate is united in existence with the subject in the external. We say: The intention is not that the predicate, from the aspect that it is a part, is predicated; rather the intention is that the substrate of part-hood is the same as the substrate of predicate-hood. For example: animal taken with the condition that rational enters into it is a species, and taken with the condition that rational does not enter into it is a part, and taken in a way that allows part-hood and species-hood to befall it. And he gave priority to genus over differentia because we need the genus to know the proximate and remote differentiae. And over species because the knowledge of a type of species, namely the relative species, depends on the genus. And he omitted from the definition of genus and all the universal the word "universal" because "predicated of many" suffices for it.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory passage from a commentary on Tahzeeb al-Mantiq. It begins with the chapter heading 'On the principles of concepts' and immediately addresses the question 'What is it?' (ma huwa) which is the standard question for definitions of quiddities. The author states that genus (jins) is given priority over specific difference (fasl) and universal accident ('arad 'amm) in the order of definition because genus is a part (juz') of the quiddity (mahiyya), while the other two are external to it. This is a key logical principle: the internal (al-dakhil) is prior to the external (al-kharij). The commentator explains that the phrase 'What is it?' when used in definition excludes remote differentiae (fusul ba'ida) and all properties (khawas) except those that are actual species-specific properties, since these are not given in answer to 'What is it?' but rather to 'What kind of thing is it?' or similar. Next, the author clarifies that genus is predicated of both different things (e.g., animal predicated of human and horse) and also of agreeing things (i.e., the individual instances). This dual predication necessitates adding a qualification (qayd al-haythiyya) to avoid equivocation. The passage then cites Abu al-Fath's principle: any universal that has real individuals is a true species relative to its own subparts (e.g., animal is a species relative to the sub-category 'rational animal'? Actually, the example: animal is a genus relative to human and horse individuals, but a species relative to its sub-parts like 'sentient animal'? The text says 'animal is a genus relative to human or horse individuals, and a species relative to its sub-parts added to them.' This illustrates the relative nature of genus and species depending on perspective. The author then addresses an objection: how can genus be both a part of the quiddity and predicated of it, since a part precedes the whole in existence (mental and external) while a predicate is united with its subject in external existence? The response distinguishes that it is not the genus qua part that is predicated, but rather the same meaning (ma'na) that can be considered as a part (under one condition) or as a whole/species (under another). The example of 'animal' is given: if taken with the condition that 'rational' enters into it, it is a species; if with the condition that 'rational' does not enter, it is a part; and if taken without condition, it can be considered either. Thus, the substrate of part-hood and of predicate-hood are the same. Finally, the passage gives reasons for prioritizing genus over specific difference (fasl): we need genus to know both proximate and remote differentiae (e.g., to know that 'rational' is the proximate differentia of human, we first know the genus 'animal'). Genus is also prior to species because a certain kind of species—the relative species (naw' idafi)—is defined by genus (e.g., the definition of 'animal' as a species relative to 'living body' includes its genus 'body'). The author also notes that the word 'kulli' (universal) is omitted from the definition of genus and other universals because the phrase 'predicated of many' (al-maqul 'ala al-kathra) already conveys the meaning of universality succinctly.

Key Terms
الجنس
الماهية
الفصل
العرض العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Genus is predicated of many, while the others are not.

Key Takeaway
Genus is the internal part of the quiddity, making it the foundational element in any definition, prioritized over external predicates.

The Qualification of Aspect in Genus and Species
Defines genus, explains its priority over specific difference and accident, and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a qualification of aspect (qayd al-haythiyya) is needed in definitions of genus and species and apply Abu al-Fath's principle of relative universality.

Core Idea
A universal can be both genus and species depending on how you look at it.

The same term, like 'animal', can be a genus when compared to human and horse, and a species when compared to 'living body'. This dual nature requires a qualification of aspect in definitions to avoid confusion. The passage introduces Abu al-Fath's principle to clarify this relative character.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dual predication of genus.
Genus is predicated of different species (e.g., animal predicated of human and horse), but also of the individual instances (حصص). The same genus is 'predicated of many' in two senses.
This leads to potential equivocation.
2
Introduce the qualification of aspect (قيد الحيثية).
To avoid contradiction, we add a condition: 'considered as predicated of species' vs 'considered as predicated of indivisible individuals'. This is the الحيثية.
Example: 'animal' as a genus is considered with respect to its species; as a universal with individuals, it is also a true species relative to its sub-parts.
3
Apply Abu al-Fath's principle.
The passage quotes Abu al-Fath: Any universal with real individuals is a true species relative to its sub-parts added to those individuals. For example, 'animal' is a genus relative to human or horse individuals, and a species relative to its sub-parts added to them.
The sub-parts could be 'sentient animal' etc.
4
Recognize the necessity of qayd in definitions.
The passage says: 'Therefore, it is necessary to consider the qualification of aspect in their definitions to avoid the material of commonality.' This means definitions must specify which aspect is meant.
This is especially important in complex concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Resolving the Objection: How Genus Can Be Both Part and Predicate
Defines genus, explains its priority over specific difference and accident, and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection that genus cannot be both part of the quiddity and predicated of it, and explain the resolution using the distinction between conditions.

Core Idea
It is not the genus as part that is predicated, but the same meaning under a different condition.

A puzzle: If genus is a part of the essence, how can it be predicated of the whole (species)? The passage presents this objection and its resolution, clarifying that the same term 'animal' can be considered a part (when rational is excluded) or a species (when rational is included). This subtle point ensures consistency in logical definitions.

Guided Path
1
State the objection clearly.
The objection: If genus is part of the quiddity, then like all parts, it should precede the whole in existence. But in a proposition like 'Human is animal', the predicate (animal) is united with the subject in existence. This seems contradictory.
The objection centers on the relation of part to whole vs. predicate to subject.
2
Find the response in the passage.
The author says: 'ليس المراد أن الخبر محمول من حيث إنه جزء يكون محمولا' – the intention is not that the predicate, from the aspect that it is a part, is predicated. Rather, the substrate of part-hood is the same as the substrate of predicate-hood.
This means the same meaning can be considered under different aspects.
3
Understand the example of 'animal'.
Animal taken with the condition that rational enters into it is a species (human). Animal taken with the condition that rational does not enter into it is a part (genus). Animal taken without condition can be either. So, when we predicate 'animal' of human, we are predicating the meaning without the condition of being a part.
Thus, predication does not use the term as a part, but as a universal meaning.
4
Apply to an Islamic concept.
Consider the definition of 'knowledge' (العلم) as 'certain belief' (الاعتقاد الجازم). The genus 'belief' is part of the quiddity of knowledge. But when we say 'Knowledge is belief', we are predicating the genus without the differentia. The objection would ask: how can belief be part and also predicated? The resolution: the term 'belief' in the definition is taken without condition, while in the quiddity it is taken as part excluding certainty.
This shows the consistency of logical definitions.

Source Text
