What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
10 min
Essential vs. Existential Necessary Properties
Commentators define and exemplify the division of necessary properties into essential and existential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between lazim al-mahiyya and lazim al-wujud and correctly identify which type a given property belongs to.

Core Idea
A property necessary for the essence itself binds across all existences; one tied to a specific existence does not.

The commentators divide necessary properties into those that are essential to the essence itself (lazim al-mahiyya) and those that are only necessary for a specific existence (lazim al-wujud). This distinction is crucial for understanding how attributes attach to objects in both mental and external reality. In this lesson, we will unpack this division using the example of evenness for four and blackness for the Ethiopian, examining the corrections and objections that arise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
From the passage, extract the definitions: lazim al-mahiyya is necessary for the essence in both mental and external existence; lazim al-wujud is necessary only for a specific existence (e.g., external).
The passage states: 'the necessary [property] is either necessary for both existences or for a specific existence.'
2
Examine the examples
Evenness for four is given as lazim al-mahiyya, but the commentators correct it to 'even' for four. Blackness for the Ethiopian is given as lazim al-wujud, corrected to 'black' for the Ethiopian. Note the reason: the predicate must be predicated on individuals.
3
Test the division
Apply the distinction to a new example: Is 'odd' a lazim al-mahiyya for three? Consider: three is always odd in any existence. Yes. Is 'being a father' a lazim al-wujud for Zayd? It depends on his specific existence, not his essence.
Use examples from kalam or fiqh for practice.

كالزَّوجيَّةِ للأربعةِ، فإنَّها لازمةٌ لماهية الأربعة . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : كَالزَّوْجِيَّةِ) المناسبُ كَـ: «الزوج» للأربعة؛ لأنّ الكلام في الكُلّيّ الخارج عن ماهية أفرادِهِ الَّذي يحملُ على أفرادِ الماهية والزوجيَّة؛ لا تحمل على الأربعة، نعم يحملُ عليها زوج. واعلَمْ أَنَّ الزَّوجيَّةَ بالنظر للأربعة ؛ عرض عام؛ لأنَّها تُقال عليها وعلى غيرها مِن كُلِّ مَا انقسم بِمتساويَيْنِ كَالسِّتَّةِ، والثَّمانية، وبالنَّظر للعدد؛ أي: كون العددِ لا يخلو عنه خاصة . (قَوْلُهُ : لَازِمَةٌ لِمَاهِيَّةِ الْأَرْبَعَةِ) أي : ذهناً وخارجاً . العطار لِمَاهِيَّتِهِ، وفي العبارة المنقولة إشعار بذلك؛ حيثُ قالَ : لوجودِهِ وتَشخُصِهِ، فهذا تقسيم آخَرُ سوى التقسيم الذي ذكرناه، فإنَّ مُحصل هذا التقسيم أَنَّ اللَّازِمَ إِمَّا أَنْ يكونَ لازماً لكلا الوجودَيْنِ، أو لوجودٍ مُعيَّنٍ، فَهُما تَقسيمانِ مُتغايرانِ، إِلَّا أَنَّ القسم الأوَّلَ في كليهما يُسمَّى لازم الماهية ا.هـ. قال عبد الحكيم : ويرد عليهِ أنَّ المقسَّمَ لَازم الماهية؛ فكيف يندرج فيه لازمُ الشَّخص؟ وأنَّ التَّقسيمَ غَيرُ حاصرٍ ؛ لأنَّ اللَّازِمَ بِاعتبارِ الوجودين ليس لازماً لِلنَّوعِ وَلَا لِلشَّخص ا.هـ. (قَوْلُهُ : كَالزَّوْجِيَّةِ لِلْأَرْبَعَةِ) هذا وقولُهُ بعد: كَالسَّوادِ للحبشي؛ مِنَ المسامحات المشهورة في عباراتهم؛ كما قالَ السَّيِّدُ، والأمثلة المطابقةُ هِيَ الزَّوجُ والأسودُ ؛ لأنَّ الكلام في الكُلّيّ الخارج عَن ماهية أفرادِهِ، فَلَا بُدَّ أنْ يكونَ محمولاً على تلك الماهية وأفرادِهَا ، لكنَّهُم تَسامَحُوا فذكرُوا مبدأ المحمول بدله؛ اعتماداً على فهم المتعلّم مِن سياق الكلام ما هو مقصود . .ا.هـ. قال مير زاهد: ومِمَّا ينبغي يُعلم ؛ أَنَّ الوجودَ في اللوازم الثَّلاثِ ليس قيداً للمعروض، بل شرطاً للمعروض أو ظرفاً له، فإنَّ العوارضَ : كُلُّ مَا تعرضُ نفسُ الشَّيءِ مِن غير اعتبار قيد زائد. ا.هـ. (قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنَّهَا لَازِمَةٌ لِمَاهِيَّةِ الأَرْبَعَةِ) أي : في كلا وجودَيْهَا؛ الخارجي والذهني، وقولُ المحشّي : إنَّ في عبارةِ الشَّارحِ قُصوراً؛ حيثُ لم يُحقِّقُ مَعنى لازم الماهِيَّةِ، ولم يتعرض للازم الوجودِ الذهني ا.هـ. مدفوع، فإِنَّهُ اقتصر على التَّمثيل لِكُلِّ أنَّ مِن (بَدْء صفحة 403) فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات (أَوْ) بالنَّظر إلى الوُجُودِ) كالسَّوادِ للحبشي، فإنَّه لازم لوجودِ الحبشي وشخصه؛ لَا لِمَاهِيَّتِه ؛ إذْ ماهيته الإنسانُ والسَّوادُ لا يلزمه . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ: إِلَى الْوُجُوْدِ) أي: إلى الموجود؛ أي: الصنف الموجود، أو الفردِ الموجود خارجاً . (قَوْلُهُ : كَالسَّوَادِ الأَوْلَى كَـ «الأسْوَد» لِمَا مرَّ مِنْ أَنَّ الكلام في الكُلِّيِّ الخارج عن ماهيّةِ أفرادِهِ الَّذي يُحملُ عليها ، والذي يُحمل على أفراد الجنس ؛ أسودُ لَا سواد. (قَوْلُهُ : وَشَخْصِهِ) أي: الخارجي. العطار لازِم الماهية ولازم الوجودِ الخارجي ؛ اتكالاً على ظهور المراد، وأنَّ المتبادر مِنَ الوجودِ هُوَ الوجود الخارجيُّ ، فَمَثَلَ لَهُ وترك التمثيل لما يعرضُ في الوجودِ الذهني فقط ؛ لِعِلْمِهِ بطريقِ المقايسة، فإنْ قُلْتَ: ما حالُ السُّلوبِ اللازمة للماهيَّةِ المعدومةِ؟ ؛ قُلْنَا : الماهيَّةُ المعدومةُ لَا عارضَ لَهَا ؛ فَضْلاً عَن كَوْنِهِ لَازِماً ، وأَمَّا المعدوم في الخارج مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّهُ مُقدَّرُ الوجودِ ؛ فَهُوَ داخل في الماهية الموجودة تقديراً كَالعنقاء، فإنَّهُ يلزمُهُ كونه طائراً على تقدير وجوده. (قَوْلُهُ : كَالسَّوَادِ لِلْحَبَشِي) اعترضَهُ صاحب القسطاس بأنَّ السَّوادَ لا يلزمُ الحبشي، إذ لا يستحيلُ وجود حبشي أبيض ، ولجواز زوال سوادِهِ بعارض، وأجابَ الدَّوَّانيُّ : بأنَّ المراد بالحبشي ؛ الممتزج بالمزاج الصنفي المخصوص ؛ سواء كانَ من الحبش أو غيره؛ ليخرجَ مَنْ ليس له ذلك المزاج وإنْ تولَّد في الحبش، وأنَّ المراد بالسَّوادِ كونُهُ أسود بطبعه، والتَّخلُّفُ لِعارض لا يُنافي ذلكَ، على أنَّ المريض لم يبق على ذلك المزاج . .ا.هـ. قال أبو الفتح: وهو مبني على أنْ يكونَ شيء تابع لمزاجه المخصوص ؛ لا يتخلَّفُ عنه ، وأَنَّ سوادَ الحبشي لا يتخلَّفُ عَن مزاجِهِ، وَكِلاهُمَا مَمنوعٌ لَا بُدَّ لَهُ مِن بيانٍ ؛ اللَّهُمَّ إِلَّا أَنْ يُرادَ بِالمزاج المخصوص ؛ ماهيته المستلزمةُ لِلسَّوادِ وَلَوْ بانضمام عارض مِن عوارضهِ كَمُقارنةِ عِلَّةِ السَّوادِ، وحَمْل السَّوادِ والأسودِ على ما يقتضي طبعُهُ ومِزاجُهُ السَّواد؛ سواءٌ اتَّصفَ بالسَّوادِ بأن ارتفع المانع أيضاً، أو لا ؛ بأن لم يرتفع؛ ركيك جدا . ا.هـ. كل
Like evenness for four, for it is necessary for the essence of four. Al-Dusuqi: His statement: 'like evenness' – the appropriate [example] is 'even' for four, because the discussion is about the universal that is external to the essence of its individuals and is predicated on the individuals of the essence, and evenness is not predicated on four; rather 'even' is predicated on it. Know that evenness in relation to four is a general accident because it is said of it and of other things that divide into two equal parts like six and eight, and in relation to number, i.e., the fact that number is not devoid of it is a property. His statement: 'necessary for the essence of four' means mentally and externally. Al-Attar: For its essence, and in the transmitted phrase there is an indication of that, since he said: for its existence and its individuation. This is another division besides the division we mentioned. The sum of this division is that the necessary [property] is either necessary for both existences or for a specific existence; they are two distinct divisions, except that the first section in both is called necessary of essence. He said: Abd al-Hakim said: It is objected to him that the divided [term] is the necessary of essence, so how can the necessary of the person be included under it? And that the division is not exhaustive because the necessary in consideration of the two existences is not necessary for the species nor for the person. He said: His statement: 'like evenness for four' – this and his statement afterward: 'like blackness for the Ethiopian' are among the well-known laxities in their expressions, as al-Sayyid said. The matching examples are 'even' and 'black', because the discussion is about the universal external to the essence of its individuals, so the predicate must be predicated on that essence and its individuals. But they were lax and mentioned the source of the predicate instead, relying on the learner's understanding from context what is intended. Mir Zahid said: Among what should be known is that existence in the three necessities is not a condition for the subject, but rather a condition for the subject or a circumstance for it, for the accidents are everything that attaches to the thing itself without considering an additional condition. His statement: 'for it is necessary for the essence of four' means in both its existences: external and mental. The statement of the commentator: 'that in the expression of the commentator there is deficiency because he did not realize the meaning of necessary of essence and did not address the necessary of mental existence' is repelled, because he limited himself to the example for each that... (Start of page 403) Chapter: On the principles of conceptualizations. 'Or' in consideration of existence like blackness for the Ethiopian, for it is necessary for the existence of the Ethiopian and his person, not for his essence, since his essence is 'human' and blackness does not necessitate it. Al-Dusuqi: His statement: 'to existence' means to the existent, i.e., the existing kind or the individual existing externally. His statement: 'like blackness' – it is better to use 'black' because of what has passed that the discussion is about the universal external to the essence of its individuals that is predicated on them, and what is predicated on individuals of the genus is 'black' not 'blackness'. His statement: 'and his person' means the external person. Al-Attar: For the necessary of essence and the necessary of external existence, relying on the clarity of the intended meaning, and that the predominant understanding from 'existence' is external existence. So he gave an example for it and left the example for what occurs in mental existence only, because of his knowledge by way of analogy. If you say: What is the case of the negative properties necessary for the nonexistent essence? We say: The nonexistent essence has no accident, let alone being necessary. As for what is nonexistent externally in terms of being hypothetically existent, it is included in the existent essence hypothetically, like the 'anqa', for it necessitates it being a bird on the hypothesis of its existence. His statement: 'like blackness for the Ethiopian' was objected to by the author of al-Qistas: that blackness is not necessary for the Ethiopian, since it is not impossible for a white Ethiopian to exist, and because of the possibility of its blackness ceasing by an accident. al-Dawwani answered: that what is meant by the Ethiopian is the one blended with the specific kind temperament, whether from Ethiopia or elsewhere, to exclude those who do not have that temperament even if born in Ethiopia, and that what is meant by blackness is his being black by nature, and its failure to occur due to an accident does not negate that, given that the sick person does not remain on that temperament. Abu al-Fath said: This is based on something being a consequence of its specific temperament not failing to occur, and that the blackness of the Ethiopian does not fail to occur from his temperament, both of which are denied and require proof. Unless what is meant by the specific temperament is its essence that necessitates blackness even with the conjunction of an accident from its accidents, such as the conjunction of the cause of blackness, and interpreting 'blackness' and 'black' as that which his nature and temperament necessitate blackness, whether he is characterized by blackness due to the removal of a hindrance or not because it is not removed, is very flimsy. End. All.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the definition and examples of necessary properties (al-lawazim) in Islamic logic. The discussion revolves around two types of necessary properties: those that are necessary for the essence itself (lazim al-mahiyya) and those that are necessary only for a specific existence (lazim al-wujud). The commentators examine two illustrative examples: evenness (zawjiyya) for four, and blackness (sawad) for an Ethiopian. 

First, al-Dusuqi points out a subtlety: the example 'evenness' is not precisely correct because 'evenness' is an abstract attribute, whereas the universal that is predicated on individuals is 'even' (zawj). So the appropriate example is 'even' rather than 'evenness'. He then clarifies that evenness, when considered with respect to the number four, is a general accident ('ard 'amm) because it applies to other numbers as well, but with respect to number itself, it is a property (khassa) that number cannot be devoid of. 

Al-Attar then discusses the division of the necessary property: either it is necessary for both mental and external existence (i.e., for the essence) or for a specific existence. He notes that when al-Dusuqi says 'for its essence', he means both mental and external existence. This division is distinct from a previous one. Abd al-Hakim objects that the division is not exhaustive because the necessary of person cannot be subsumed under necessary of essence, and that the necessary considering both existences is not necessary for the species or person—only for the essence. 

The passage then addresses a common laxity (musamaha) in logical examples: the commentators use 'evenness' and 'blackness' instead of the proper predicates 'even' and 'black'. This is permissible because the context makes the intended meaning clear. Mir Zahid adds that existence is not a condition (qayd) for the subject of the necessary property but rather a condition or circumstance; the accident attaches to the thing itself without an additional condition. 

Turning to the second example, al-Dusuqi explains that the phrase 'to existence' in the text refers to 'the existent'—either the existing kind or an individual. The proper example is 'black' (aswad) not 'blackness' (sawad). Al-Attar elaborates that the example illustrates the necessary of external existence, not the necessary of essence. He anticipates an objection: what about negative properties (sulub) for nonexistent essences? He responds that a nonexistent essence has no accidents at all, so it has no necessary properties. However, something nonexistent in external reality but considered hypothetically existent (like the mythical anqa bird) is treated as if it had existence for the purpose of analysis, so it would require being a bird on the hypothesis of its existence. 

Finally, the passage addresses an objection from the author of al-Qistas that blackness is not genuinely necessary for an Ethiopian because one could imagine a white Ethiopian or blackness disappearing. al-Dawwani answers by specifying that 'Ethiopian' here means a person with a specific temperament that naturally produces blackness, regardless of geographical origin, and 'blackness' means being black by nature, not accidentally. The failure to be black due to a temporary accident (like illness) does not negate the necessary connection. Abu al-Fath finds this answer problematic because it assumes blackness is inseparably tied to that temperament, which is not proven. He dismisses the attempt to refine the definition as 'very flimsy' (rakik jiddan). The passage ends with a blunt critique, leaving the reader with a sense of scholarly disagreement over the adequacy of the example.

The division of necessary properties into essential (lazim al-mahiyya) and existential (lazim al-wujud) is foundational for correctly attributing predicates to their subjects across different modes of existence.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
8 min
Precision in Logical Examples: Predicate vs. Abstract Attribute
Commentators correct the examples from abstract attributes to concrete predicates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and correct the common laxity (musamaha) in logical examples, replacing abstract attributes with concrete predicates where necessary.

Core Idea
The correct example uses the concrete predicate that is predicated on individuals, not the abstract property name.

The commentators alert us to a common oversight: when giving examples of necessary properties, they often use abstract nouns like 'evenness' and 'blackness' instead of the adjectives 'even' and 'black'. This is a deliberate laxity (musamaha) to facilitate teaching, but the precise requirement is that the universal must be predicable of individuals. In this lesson, we examine why the correction is made and how it affects our understanding of the examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mistake
In the passage, al-Dusuqi corrects the example from 'evenness' to 'even' and 'blackness' to 'black'. The reason: the universal being discussed must be predicated on individuals.
We say 'four is even', not 'four is evenness'.
2
Understand the reasoning
The discussion is about a universal that is external to the essence of its individuals and is predicated on them. Abstract attributes are not predicated on individuals; concrete predicates are.
The passage states: 'the appropriate [example] is even for four'.
3
Apply the correction
Replace the abstract attribute with the correct predicate. For example, 'temporal origination for the world' should be 'temporally originated for the world' (hadith). Practice with other examples from Islamic sciences.

كالزَّوجيَّةِ للأربعةِ، فإنَّها لازمةٌ لماهية الأربعة . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : كَالزَّوْجِيَّةِ) المناسبُ كَـ: «الزوج» للأربعة؛ لأنّ الكلام في الكُلّيّ الخارج عن ماهية أفرادِهِ الَّذي يحملُ على أفرادِ الماهية والزوجيَّة؛ لا تحمل على الأربعة، نعم يحملُ عليها زوج. واعلَمْ أَنَّ الزَّوجيَّةَ بالنظر للأربعة ؛ عرض عام؛ لأنَّها تُقال عليها وعلى غيرها مِن كُلِّ مَا انقسم بِمتساويَيْنِ كَالسِّتَّةِ، والثَّمانية، وبالنَّظر للعدد؛ أي: كون العددِ لا يخلو عنه خاصة . (قَوْلُهُ : كَالزَّوْجِيَّةِ لِلْأَرْبَعَةِ) هذا وقولُهُ بعد: كَالسَّوادِ للحبشي؛ مِنَ المسامحات المشهورة في عباراتهم؛ كما قالَ السَّيِّدُ، والأمثلة المطابقةُ هِيَ الزَّوجُ والأسودُ ؛ لأنَّ الكلام في الكُلّيّ الخارج عَن ماهية أفرادِهِ، فَلَا بُدَّ أنْ يكونَ محمولاً على تلك الماهية وأفرادِهَا ، لكنَّهُم تَسامَحُوا فذكرُوا مبدأ المحمول بدله؛ اعتماداً على فهم المتعلّم مِن سياق الكلام ما هو مقصود . .ا.هـ. قال مير زاهد: ومِمَّا ينبغي يُعلم ؛ أَنَّ الوجودَ في اللوازم الثَّلاثِ ليس قيداً للمعروض، بل شرطاً للمعروض أو ظرفاً له، فإنَّ العوارضَ : كُلُّ مَا تعرضُ نفسُ الشَّيءِ مِن غير اعتبار قيد زائد. ا.هـ. (قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنَّهَا لَازِمَةٌ لِمَاهِيَّةِ الأَرْبَعَةِ) أي : في كلا وجودَيْهَا؛ الخارجي والذهني، وقولُ المحشّي : إنَّ في عبارةِ الشَّارحِ قُصوراً؛ حيثُ لم يُحقِّقُ مَعنى لازم الماهِيَّةِ، ولم يتعرض للازم الوجودِ الذهني ا.هـ. مدفوع، فإِنَّهُ اقتصر على التَّمثيل لِكُلِّ أنَّ مِن (بَدْء صفحة 403) فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات (أَوْ) بالنَّظر إلى الوُجُودِ) كالسَّوادِ للحبشي، فإنَّه لازم لوجودِ الحبشي وشخصه؛ لَا لِمَاهِيَّتِه ؛ إذْ ماهيته الإنسانُ والسَّوادُ لا يلزمه . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ: إِلَى الْوُجُوْدِ) أي: إلى الموجود؛ أي: الصنف الموجود، أو الفردِ الموجود خارجاً . (قَوْلُهُ : كَالسَّوَادِ لِلْحَبَشِي) اعترضَهُ صاحب القسطاس بأنَّ السَّوادَ لا يلزمُ الحبشي، إذ لا يستحيلُ وجود حبشي أبيض ، ولجواز زوال سوادِهِ بعارض، وأجابَ الدَّوَّانيُّ : بأنَّ المراد بالحبشي ؛ الممتزج بالمزاج الصنفي المخصوص ؛ سواء كانَ من الحبش أو غيره؛ ليخرجَ مَنْ ليس له ذلك المزاج وإنْ تولَّد في الحبش، وأنَّ المراد بالسَّوادِ كونُهُ أسود بطبعه، والتَّخلُّفُ لِعارض لا يُنافي ذلكَ، على أنَّ المريض لم يبق على ذلك المزاج . .ا.هـ. قال أبو الفتح: وهو مبني على أنْ يكونَ شيء تابع لمزاجه المخصوص ؛ لا يتخلَّفُ عنه ، وأَنَّ سوادَ الحبشي لا يتخلَّفُ عَن مزاجِهِ، وَكِلاهُمَا مَمنوعٌ لَا بُدَّ لَهُ مِن بيانٍ ؛ اللَّهُمَّ إِلَّا أَنْ يُرادَ بِالمزاج المخصوص ؛ ماهيته المستلزمةُ لِلسَّوادِ وَلَوْ بانضمام عارض مِن عوارضهِ كَمُقارنةِ عِلَّةِ السَّوادِ، وحَمْل السَّوادِ والأسودِ على ما يقتضي طبعُهُ ومِزاجُهُ السَّواد؛ سواءٌ اتَّصفَ بالسَّوادِ بأن ارتفع المانع أيضاً، أو لا ؛ بأن لم يرتفع؛ ركيك جدا . ا.هـ. كل
Like evenness for four, for it is necessary for the essence of four. Al-Dusuqi: His statement: 'like evenness' – the appropriate [example] is 'even' for four, because the discussion is about the universal that is external to the essence of its individuals and is predicated on the individuals of the essence, and evenness is not predicated on four; rather 'even' is predicated on it. Know that evenness in relation to four is a general accident because it is said of it and of other things that divide into two equal parts like six and eight, and in relation to number, i.e., the fact that number is not devoid of it is a property. His statement: 'necessary for the essence of four' means mentally and externally. Al-Attar: For its essence, and in the transmitted phrase there is an indication of that, since he said: for its existence and its individuation. This is another division besides the division we mentioned. The sum of this division is that the necessary [property] is either necessary for both existences or for a specific existence; they are two distinct divisions, except that the first section in both is called necessary of essence. He said: Abd al-Hakim said: It is objected to him that the divided [term] is the necessary of essence, so how can the necessary of the person be included under it? And that the division is not exhaustive because the necessary in consideration of the two existences is not necessary for the species nor for the person. He said: His statement: 'like evenness for four' – this and his statement afterward: 'like blackness for the Ethiopian' are among the well-known laxities in their expressions, as al-Sayyid said. The matching examples are 'even' and 'black', because the discussion is about the universal external to the essence of its individuals, so the predicate must be predicated on that essence and its individuals. But they were lax and mentioned the source of the predicate instead, relying on the learner's understanding from context what is intended. Mir Zahid said: Among what should be known is that existence in the three necessities is not a condition for the subject, but rather a condition for the subject or a circumstance for it, for the accidents are everything that attaches to the thing itself without considering an additional condition. His statement: 'for it is necessary for the essence of four' means in both its existences: external and mental. The statement of the commentator: 'that in the expression of the commentator there is deficiency because he did not realize the meaning of necessary of essence and did not address the necessary of mental existence' is repelled, because he limited himself to the example for each that... (Start of page 403) Chapter: On the principles of conceptualizations. 'Or' in consideration of existence like blackness for the Ethiopian, for it is necessary for the existence of the Ethiopian and his person, not for his essence, since his essence is 'human' and blackness does not necessitate it. Al-Dusuqi: His statement: 'to existence' means to the existent, i.e., the existing kind or the individual existing externally. His statement: 'like blackness' – it is better to use 'black' because of what has passed that the discussion is about the universal external to the essence of its individuals that is predicated on them, and what is predicated on individuals of the genus is 'black' not 'blackness'. His statement: 'and his person' means the external person. Al-Attar: For the necessary of essence and the necessary of external existence, relying on the clarity of the intended meaning, and that the predominant understanding from 'existence' is external existence. So he gave an example for it and left the example for what occurs in mental existence only, because of his knowledge by way of analogy. If you say: What is the case of the negative properties necessary for the nonexistent essence? We say: The nonexistent essence has no accident, let alone being necessary. As for what is nonexistent externally in terms of being hypothetically existent, it is included in the existent essence hypothetically, like the 'anqa', for it necessitates it being a bird on the hypothesis of its existence. His statement: 'like blackness for the Ethiopian' was objected to by the author of al-Qistas: that blackness is not necessary for the Ethiopian, since it is not impossible for a white Ethiopian to exist, and because of the possibility of its blackness ceasing by an accident. al-Dawwani answered: that what is meant by the Ethiopian is the one blended with the specific kind temperament, whether from Ethiopia or elsewhere, to exclude those who do not have that temperament even if born in Ethiopia, and that what is meant by blackness is his being black by nature, and its failure to occur due to an accident does not negate that, given that the sick person does not remain on that temperament. Abu al-Fath said: This is based on something being a consequence of its specific temperament not failing to occur, and that the blackness of the Ethiopian does not fail to occur from his temperament, both of which are denied and require proof. Unless what is meant by the specific temperament is its essence that necessitates blackness even with the conjunction of an accident from its accidents, such as the conjunction of the cause of blackness, and interpreting 'blackness' and 'black' as that which his nature and temperament necessitate blackness, whether he is characterized by blackness due to the removal of a hindrance or not because it is not removed, is very flimsy. End. All.

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the definition and examples of necessary properties (al-lawazim) in Islamic logic. The discussion revolves around two types of necessary properties: those that are necessary for the essence itself (lazim al-mahiyya) and those that are necessary only for a specific existence (lazim al-wujud). The commentators examine two illustrative examples: evenness (zawjiyya) for four, and blackness (sawad) for an Ethiopian. 

First, al-Dusuqi points out a subtlety: the example 'evenness' is not precisely correct because 'evenness' is an abstract attribute, whereas the universal that is predicated on individuals is 'even' (zawj). So the appropriate example is 'even' rather than 'evenness'. He then clarifies that evenness, when considered with respect to the number four, is a general accident ('ard 'amm) because it applies to other numbers as well, but with respect to number itself, it is a property (khassa) that number cannot be devoid of. 

Al-Attar then discusses the division of the necessary property: either it is necessary for both mental and external existence (i.e., for the essence) or for a specific existence. He notes that when al-Dusuqi says 'for its essence', he means both mental and external existence. This division is distinct from a previous one. Abd al-Hakim objects that the division is not exhaustive because the necessary of person cannot be subsumed under necessary of essence, and that the necessary considering both existences is not necessary for the species or person—only for the essence. 

The passage then addresses a common laxity (musamaha) in logical examples: the commentators use 'evenness' and 'blackness' instead of the proper predicates 'even' and 'black'. This is permissible because the context makes the intended meaning clear. Mir Zahid adds that existence is not a condition (qayd) for the subject of the necessary property but rather a condition or circumstance; the accident attaches to the thing itself without an additional condition. 

Turning to the second example, al-Dusuqi explains that the phrase 'to existence' in the text refers to 'the existent'—either the existing kind or an individual. The proper example is 'black' (aswad) not 'blackness' (sawad). Al-Attar elaborates that the example illustrates the necessary of external existence, not the necessary of essence. He anticipates an objection: what about negative properties (sulub) for nonexistent essences? He responds that a nonexistent essence has no accidents at all, so it has no necessary properties. However, something nonexistent in external reality but considered hypothetically existent (like the mythical anqa bird) is treated as if it had existence for the purpose of analysis, so it would require being a bird on the hypothesis of its existence. 

Finally, the passage addresses an objection from the author of al-Qistas that blackness is not genuinely necessary for an Ethiopian because one could imagine a white Ethiopian or blackness disappearing. al-Dawwani answers by specifying that 'Ethiopian' here means a person with a specific temperament that naturally produces blackness, regardless of geographical origin, and 'blackness' means being black by nature, not accidentally. The failure to be black due to a temporary accident (like illness) does not negate the necessary connection. Abu al-Fath finds this answer problematic because it assumes blackness is inseparably tied to that temperament, which is not proven. He dismisses the attempt to refine the definition as 'very flimsy' (rakik jiddan). The passage ends with a blunt critique, leaving the reader with a sense of scholarly disagreement over the adequacy of the example.

What Is Mantiq?

The Blackness Objection and Its Resolution
Commentators discuss the objection to the blackness example and the response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the objection to the blackness example and evaluate the proposed resolutions using the criteria of necessary properties.

Core Idea
An objection that blackness is not necessary for the Ethiopian fails to account for the intended meaning of 'by nature' and 'specific temperament'.

The example of blackness for the Ethiopian as a lazim al-wujud is challenged by the author of al-Qistas, who argues that blackness is not necessary because a white Ethiopian is possible. al-Dawwani responds by specifying that 'Ethiopian' here means a person with a specific temperament that naturally produces blackness, and that accidental whiteness due to illness does not negate the necessary property. The exchange illustrates how logical definitions are refined through objections.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
The author of al-Qistas objects: blackness is not necessary for an Ethiopian because a white Ethiopian can exist, and blackness can cease due to accident.
The objection is based on the possibility of exceptions.
2
Find al-Dawwani's response
Al-Dawwani redefines 'Ethiopian' as a person with a specific kind temperament, and 'blackness' as being black by nature. Accidental whiteness due to illness does not refute the natural necessity.
This response saves the example by specifying the intended meaning more precisely.
3
Critique by Abu al-Fath
Abu al-Fath says this reasoning is weak (rakik jiddan) because it assumes blackness is inseparable from that temperament without proof, and both premises are unproven.
4
Evaluate the exchange
Which side makes a stronger logical point? Use the definition of lazim al-wujud: it is necessary for a specific existence, not for the essence. Does the objection hold if we accept al-Dawwani's specification?

كالزَّوجيَّةِ للأربعةِ، فإنَّها لازمةٌ لماهية الأربعة . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : كَالزَّوْجِيَّةِ) المناسبُ كَـ: «الزوج» للأربعة؛ لأنّ الكلام في الكُلّيّ الخارج عن ماهية أفرادِهِ الَّذي يحملُ على أفرادِ الماهية والزوجيَّة؛ لا تحمل على الأربعة، نعم يحملُ عليها زوج. واعلَمْ أَنَّ الزَّوجيَّةَ بالنظر للأربعة ؛ عرض عام؛ لأنَّها تُقال عليها وعلى غيرها مِن كُلِّ مَا انقسم بِمتساويَيْنِ كَالسِّتَّةِ، والثَّمانية، وبالنَّظر للعدد؛ أي: كون العددِ لا يخلو عنه خاصة . (قَوْلُهُ : لَازِمَةٌ لِمَاهِيَّةِ الْأَرْبَعَةِ) أي : ذهناً وخارجاً . العطار لِمَاهِيَّتِهِ، وفي العبارة المنقولة إشعار بذلك؛ حيثُ قالَ : لوجودِهِ وتَشخُصِهِ، فهذا تقسيم آخَرُ سوى التقسيم الذي ذكرناه، فإنَّ مُحصل هذا التقسيم أَنَّ اللَّازِمَ إِمَّا أَنْ يكونَ لازماً لكلا الوجودَيْنِ، أو لوجودٍ مُعيَّنٍ، فَهُما تَقسيمانِ مُتغايرانِ، إِلَّا أَنَّ القسم الأوَّلَ في كليهما يُسمَّى لازم الماهية ا.هـ. قال عبد الحكيم : ويرد عليهِ أنَّ المقسَّمَ لَازم الماهية؛ فكيف يندرج فيه لازمُ الشَّخص؟ وأنَّ التَّقسيمَ غَيرُ حاصرٍ ؛ لأنَّ اللَّازِمَ بِاعتبارِ الوجودين ليس لازماً لِلنَّوعِ وَلَا لِلشَّخص ا.هـ. (قَوْلُهُ : كَالزَّوْجِيَّةِ لِلْأَرْبَعَةِ) هذا وقولُهُ بعد: كَالسَّوادِ للحبشي؛ مِنَ المسامحات المشهورة في عباراتهم؛ كما قالَ السَّيِّدُ، والأمثلة المطابقةُ هِيَ الزَّوجُ والأسودُ ؛ لأنَّ الكلام في الكُلّيّ الخارج عَن ماهية أفرادِهِ، فَلَا بُدَّ أنْ يكونَ محمولاً على تلك الماهية وأفرادِهَا ، لكنَّهُم تَسامَحُوا فذكرُوا مبدأ المحمول بدله؛ اعتماداً على فهم المتعلّم مِن سياق الكلام ما هو مقصود . .ا.هـ. قال مير زاهد: ومِمَّا ينبغي يُعلم ؛ أَنَّ الوجودَ في اللوازم الثَّلاثِ ليس قيداً للمعروض، بل شرطاً للمعروض أو ظرفاً له، فإنَّ العوارضَ : كُلُّ مَا تعرضُ نفسُ الشَّيءِ مِن غير اعتبار قيد زائد. ا.هـ. (قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنَّهَا لَازِمَةٌ لِمَاهِيَّةِ الأَرْبَعَةِ) أي : في كلا وجودَيْهَا؛ الخارجي والذهني، وقولُ المحشّي : إنَّ في عبارةِ الشَّارحِ قُصوراً؛ حيثُ لم يُحقِّقُ مَعنى لازم الماهِيَّةِ، ولم يتعرض للازم الوجودِ الذهني ا.هـ. مدفوع، فإِنَّهُ اقتصر على التَّمثيل لِكُلِّ أنَّ مِن (بَدْء صفحة 403) فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات (أَوْ) بالنَّظر إلى الوُجُودِ) كالسَّوادِ للحبشي، فإنَّه لازم لوجودِ الحبشي وشخصه؛ لَا لِمَاهِيَّتِه ؛ إذْ ماهيته الإنسانُ والسَّوادُ لا يلزمه . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ: إِلَى الْوُجُوْدِ) أي: إلى الموجود؛ أي: الصنف الموجود، أو الفردِ الموجود خارجاً . (قَوْلُهُ : كَالسَّوَادِ الأَوْلَى كَـ «الأسْوَد» لِمَا مرَّ مِنْ أَنَّ الكلام في الكُلِّيِّ الخارج عن ماهيّةِ أفرادِهِ الَّذي يُحملُ عليها ، والذي يُحمل على أفراد الجنس ؛ أسودُ لَا سواد. (قَوْلُهُ : وَشَخْصِهِ) أي: الخارجي. العطار لازِم الماهية ولازم الوجودِ الخارجي ؛ اتكالاً على ظهور المراد، وأنَّ المتبادر مِنَ الوجودِ هُوَ الوجود الخارجيُّ ، فَمَثَلَ لَهُ وترك التمثيل لما يعرضُ في الوجودِ الذهني فقط ؛ لِعِلْمِهِ بطريقِ المقايسة، فإنْ قُلْتَ: ما حالُ السُّلوبِ اللازمة للماهيَّةِ المعدومةِ؟ ؛ قُلْنَا : الماهيَّةُ المعدومةُ لَا عارضَ لَهَا ؛ فَضْلاً عَن كَوْنِهِ لَازِماً ، وأَمَّا المعدوم في الخارج مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّهُ مُقدَّرُ الوجودِ ؛ فَهُوَ داخل في الماهية الموجودة تقديراً كَالعنقاء، فإنَّهُ يلزمُهُ كونه طائراً على تقدير وجوده. (قَوْلُهُ : كَالسَّوَادِ لِلْحَبَشِي) اعترضَهُ صاحب القسطاس بأنَّ السَّوادَ لا يلزمُ الحبشي، إذ لا يستحيلُ وجود حبشي أبيض ، ولجواز زوال سوادِهِ بعارض، وأجابَ الدَّوَّانيُّ : بأنَّ المراد بالحبشي ؛ الممتزج بالمزاج الصنفي المخصوص ؛ سواء كانَ من الحبش أو غيره؛ ليخرجَ مَنْ ليس له ذلك المزاج وإنْ تولَّد في الحبش، وأنَّ المراد بالسَّوادِ كونُهُ أسود بطبعه، والتَّخلُّفُ لِعارض لا يُنافي ذلكَ، على أنَّ المريض لم يبق على ذلك المزاج . .ا.هـ. قال أبو الفتح: وهو مبني على أنْ يكونَ شيء تابع لمزاجه المخصوص ؛ لا يتخلَّفُ عنه ، وأَنَّ سوادَ الحبشي لا يتخلَّفُ عَن مزاجِهِ، وَكِلاهُمَا مَمنوعٌ لَا بُدَّ لَهُ مِن بيانٍ ؛ اللَّهُمَّ إِلَّا أَنْ يُرادَ بِالمزاج المخصوص ؛ ماهيته المستلزمةُ لِلسَّوادِ وَلَوْ بانضمام عارض مِن عوارضهِ كَمُقارنةِ عِلَّةِ السَّوادِ، وحَمْل السَّوادِ والأسودِ على ما يقتضي طبعُهُ ومِزاجُهُ السَّواد؛ سواءٌ اتَّصفَ بالسَّوادِ بأن ارتفع المانع أيضاً، أو لا ؛ بأن لم يرتفع؛ ركيك جدا . ا.هـ. كل
Like evenness for four, for it is necessary for the essence of four. Al-Dusuqi: His statement: 'like evenness' – the appropriate [example] is 'even' for four, because the discussion is about the universal that is external to the essence of its individuals and is predicated on the individuals of the essence, and evenness is not predicated on four; rather 'even' is predicated on it. Know that evenness in relation to four is a general accident because it is said of it and of other things that divide into two equal parts like six and eight, and in relation to number, i.e., the fact that number is not devoid of it is a property. His statement: 'necessary for the essence of four' means mentally and externally. Al-Attar: For its essence, and in the transmitted phrase there is an indication of that, since he said: for its existence and its individuation. This is another division besides the division we mentioned. The sum of this division is that the necessary [property] is either necessary for both existences or for a specific existence; they are two distinct divisions, except that the first section in both is called necessary of essence. He said: Abd al-Hakim said: It is objected to him that the divided [term] is the necessary of essence, so how can the necessary of the person be included under it? And that the division is not exhaustive because the necessary in consideration of the two existences is not necessary for the species nor for the person. He said: His statement: 'like evenness for four' – this and his statement afterward: 'like blackness for the Ethiopian' are among the well-known laxities in their expressions, as al-Sayyid said. The matching examples are 'even' and 'black', because the discussion is about the universal external to the essence of its individuals, so the predicate must be predicated on that essence and its individuals. But they were lax and mentioned the source of the predicate instead, relying on the learner's understanding from context what is intended. Mir Zahid said: Among what should be known is that existence in the three necessities is not a condition for the subject, but rather a condition for the subject or a circumstance for it, for the accidents are everything that attaches to the thing itself without considering an additional condition. His statement: 'for it is necessary for the essence of four' means in both its existences: external and mental. The statement of the commentator: 'that in the expression of the commentator there is deficiency because he did not realize the meaning of necessary of essence and did not address the necessary of mental existence' is repelled, because he limited himself to the example for each that... (Start of page 403) Chapter: On the principles of conceptualizations. 'Or' in consideration of existence like blackness for the Ethiopian, for it is necessary for the existence of the Ethiopian and his person, not for his essence, since his essence is 'human' and blackness does not necessitate it. Al-Dusuqi: His statement: 'to existence' means to the existent, i.e., the existing kind or the individual existing externally. His statement: 'like blackness' – it is better to use 'black' because of what has passed that the discussion is about the universal external to the essence of its individuals that is predicated on them, and what is predicated on individuals of the genus is 'black' not 'blackness'. His statement: 'and his person' means the external person. Al-Attar: For the necessary of essence and the necessary of external existence, relying on the clarity of the intended meaning, and that the predominant understanding from 'existence' is external existence. So he gave an example for it and left the example for what occurs in mental existence only, because of his knowledge by way of analogy. If you say: What is the case of the negative properties necessary for the nonexistent essence? We say: The nonexistent essence has no accident, let alone being necessary. As for what is nonexistent externally in terms of being hypothetically existent, it is included in the existent essence hypothetically, like the 'anqa', for it necessitates it being a bird on the hypothesis of its existence. His statement: 'like blackness for the Ethiopian' was objected to by the author of al-Qistas: that blackness is not necessary for the Ethiopian, since it is not impossible for a white Ethiopian to exist, and because of the possibility of its blackness ceasing by an accident. al-Dawwani answered: that what is meant by the Ethiopian is the one blended with the specific kind temperament, whether from Ethiopia or elsewhere, to exclude those who do not have that temperament even if born in Ethiopia, and that what is meant by blackness is his being black by nature, and its failure to occur due to an accident does not negate that, given that the sick person does not remain on that temperament. Abu al-Fath said: This is based on something being a consequence of its specific temperament not failing to occur, and that the blackness of the Ethiopian does not fail to occur from his temperament, both of which are denied and require proof. Unless what is meant by the specific temperament is its essence that necessitates blackness even with the conjunction of an accident from its accidents, such as the conjunction of the cause of blackness, and interpreting 'blackness' and 'black' as that which his nature and temperament necessitate blackness, whether he is characterized by blackness due to the removal of a hindrance or not because it is not removed, is very flimsy. End. All.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the definition and examples of necessary properties (al-lawazim) in Islamic logic. The discussion revolves around two types of necessary properties: those that are necessary for the essence itself (lazim al-mahiyya) and those that are necessary only for a specific existence (lazim al-wujud). The commentators examine two illustrative examples: evenness (zawjiyya) for four, and blackness (sawad) for an Ethiopian. 

First, al-Dusuqi points out a subtlety: the example 'evenness' is not precisely correct because 'evenness' is an abstract attribute, whereas the universal that is predicated on individuals is 'even' (zawj). So the appropriate example is 'even' rather than 'evenness'. He then clarifies that evenness, when considered with respect to the number four, is a general accident ('ard 'amm) because it applies to other numbers as well, but with respect to number itself, it is a property (khassa) that number cannot be devoid of. 

Al-Attar then discusses the division of the necessary property: either it is necessary for both mental and external existence (i.e., for the essence) or for a specific existence. He notes that when al-Dusuqi says 'for its essence', he means both mental and external existence. This division is distinct from a previous one. Abd al-Hakim objects that the division is not exhaustive because the necessary of person cannot be subsumed under necessary of essence, and that the necessary considering both existences is not necessary for the species or person—only for the essence. 

The passage then addresses a common laxity (musamaha) in logical examples: the commentators use 'evenness' and 'blackness' instead of the proper predicates 'even' and 'black'. This is permissible because the context makes the intended meaning clear. Mir Zahid adds that existence is not a condition (qayd) for the subject of the necessary property but rather a condition or circumstance; the accident attaches to the thing itself without an additional condition. 

Turning to the second example, al-Dusuqi explains that the phrase 'to existence' in the text refers to 'the existent'—either the existing kind or an individual. The proper example is 'black' (aswad) not 'blackness' (sawad). Al-Attar elaborates that the example illustrates the necessary of external existence, not the necessary of essence. He anticipates an objection: what about negative properties (sulub) for nonexistent essences? He responds that a nonexistent essence has no accidents at all, so it has no necessary properties. However, something nonexistent in external reality but considered hypothetically existent (like the mythical anqa bird) is treated as if it had existence for the purpose of analysis, so it would require being a bird on the hypothesis of its existence. 

Finally, the passage addresses an objection from the author of al-Qistas that blackness is not genuinely necessary for an Ethiopian because one could imagine a white Ethiopian or blackness disappearing. al-Dawwani answers by specifying that 'Ethiopian' here means a person with a specific temperament that naturally produces blackness, regardless of geographical origin, and 'blackness' means being black by nature, not accidentally. The failure to be black due to a temporary accident (like illness) does not negate the necessary connection. Abu al-Fath finds this answer problematic because it assumes blackness is inseparably tied to that temperament, which is not proven. He dismisses the attempt to refine the definition as 'very flimsy' (rakik jiddan). The passage ends with a blunt critique, leaving the reader with a sense of scholarly disagreement over the adequacy of the example.

Checkpoint
According to al-Dawwani, what is meant by 'Ethiopian' in the example?
A) Any person from Ethiopia
B) A person with a specific temperament that produces blackness
C) A person who is currently black
D) A person who will always be black

Key Takeaway
Objections to logical examples often require specifying the intended meaning more precisely, but such responses must themselves be logically sound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Summarize the objection of the author of al-Qistas and al-Dawwani's response in your own words.
?
Evaluate Abu al-Fath's critique: is it valid? What would you need to prove to defend al-Dawwani's position?

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
7 min
The Natural Universal is a Mental Consideration
Author defends mental existence of natural universal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that the natural universal is an i'tibari concept and refute the objection that its part must exist externally.

Core Idea
The natural universal exists only in the mind; its parts are conceptual, not external.

In this passage, al-‘Attar defends the position that the natural universal (al-kulli al-tabi'i) is a mental consideration with no external reality. The main obstacle is the objection: if the natural universal is a part of the quiddity of external things, then that part must exist externally. Al-‘Attar responds by distinguishing the part as a conceptual constituent, not an external one. Let's walk through the argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The author asserts that the natural universal is an i'tibari concept with no external existence. What exists externally are only particulars that bear the form of the universal.
2
Understand the objection
Later scholars object: if the natural universal is a part of the quiddity of external individuals, then that part must exist externally (since whole-part unity requires co-existence).
3
Follow the response
The author argues that the part is that by which the thing is constituted, considered irrespective of existence. The quiddity is constituted by the part in the mind, not in external reality. The part is not required to exist externally; it is a conceptual part.
4
Examine the example
The triangle example: the line is a part of the triangle in the sense that the concept of triangle cannot be formed without the concept of line, but the line does not have to exist externally for the triangle to exist externally. This illustrates that the part is conceptual.
5
Conclude the refutation
The objection fails because it conflates the mental part (constituent of the quiddity) with an external part (which would have independent prior existence). The natural universal is a mental consideration, not an external reality.

فَلِذا كانَ التَّحقيقُ : أنَّ الكُلّي الطَّبيعي أمر اعتباري لا وجود له خارجاً أصلاً، والموجود في الخارج جزئيات على صورةِ الكُلّيّ المرتسمة في العقلِ . ذاتيا كما حَقَّقَهُ السَّيِّدُ في حاشيةِ التَّجريدِ؟ (بل نجزمُ بأَنَّهُ) أي : الشَّيء الَّذِي حمل عليهِ الحيوانُ (مُتقوِّم ذلكَ الشَّيءُ بِهِ ) أي : بالحيوان؛ لأَنَّهُ جزءهُ، فَهُوَ داخل في قوامِهِ وحقيقتِهِ وَلَا نعني بالجزء إِلَّا مَا يتقوَّمُ بِهِ الشَّيء) وعطف قوله : (ولا يمكنُ تحصيلُ ما هَيَّتِهِ بِدونِهِ) تَفسيرُ، ثُمَّ مَثَلَ لِذلكَ بِمثالٍ مَحسوس فقال : (كَالمُثَلَّثِ) أي : السَّطحِ المثلث وهو ما أحاطَ بِهِ ثلاث خطوط (فَإِنَّهُ لا يتقوَّمُ وَلَا يتحصَّلُ بدونِ الخطّ لأنَّ الخطّ جزؤهُ، والمراد جنس الخطّ لأنَّهُ أَحاطَ بِهِ ثلاث خطوط (والشطحُ) المحاط به ؛ لأنَّه جزءهُ الثَّانِي مَعَ قطع النَّظرِ عَنْ وجودِهِ) أي : وجودِ ذلكَ الشَّيء المتقوم بالجزء (وعدمِهِ) وإِنَّما قطعْنَا النَّظَرَ عَن وجودِ ذلكَ الشَّيء وعدمِهِ؛ لأنَّ الكلامَ مَفروضٌ في تقوم الماهِيَّةِ المركَّبةِ بِجزئها ؛ فَيشمل سائرَ الماهيَّاتِ المركَّبة موجودة في الخارج أوَّلاً ، فإذا ثبتَ تَقَوُّمُهَا بِالجزء وعرضَ لَهَا الوجود خارجاً؛ يجب أن يوجد جزؤها خارجاً ضرورة اتحادِ الكُلِّ والجزء في ظرفِ الوجودِ، فَلِذلك قالَ : وَلَا شَلَّ أَنَّ مَا يُنقوَّمُ بهِ الموجود يجب أن يكونَ موجوداً، وخُلاصتُهُ) أي: خلاصةُ الدَّليلِ السَّابقِ كَمَا هو المتبادرُ ، لكنَّ الكلام المذكور يفيدُ أَنَّهُ دليلٌ آخَرُ وهوَ الظَّاهِرُ ، وحينئذٍ فَالمراد : وخُلاصة الاستدلال (أَنَّهُ لا شَكٍّ أَنَّ بعضَ الأشخاص) كَالإنسانِ يشارك بعضاً آخَرَ) كَالفرس (دونَ بعض) كَالشَّجرِ (في أمرٍ وهِيَ الحيوانيَّة (مَعَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنِ الوجودِ وَمَا يَتبعُهُ مِنَ العوارضِ) أي : الآثار المترتبة عليه، وإنّما قطعنَا النَّظرَ عَنِ الوجودِ هُنَا؛ لِأَنَّهُ عام لسائر الموجوداتِ، ونحن لم نعتبر المشاركة فيهِ؛ بلْ إِنَّما اعتبرنَاهَا في الماهِيَّةِ المندرج تحتها تلك الأشخاصُ كَمَا مِثْلُنَا فَذلِكَ الأمرُ المشترك تتقوَّمُ بِهِ الأشخاص حد ذاتِهَا أي: مَعَ قطع النَّظرِ عَنِ الوجودِ وَمَا يتبعه من العوارض. وأمَّا قولُهم في تعريف زيدٍ : إِنَّهُ حيوان ناطق ؛ فهو تعريف ماهيته الاعتبارية لا الحقيقة . زاهد : لؤ لم تكن الأشياء حاصلةٌ بِنَفْسِهَا في الخارج؛ لم تكن حاصلةٌ بِنَفْسِهَا في الذهن، ولم تكن الذَّاتِيَّاتُ مُتحقِّقَةٌ في الوجودَينِ ا.هـ. ومَبْناهُ مَا سَلَفَ مِنْ أَنَّ التحقيق أنَّ الحاصل في الذهن هو نَفْسُ الماهية الموجودةِ خَارِجاً؛ لا شبحُها ومثالُها (فَـ) ظهرَ أنَّ جزء الموجودِ مَوجودٌ (اندفع الاعتراض) مِنَ المتأخّرينَ المنكرين لوجودِ الكُلِّيِّ الطَّبيعي في الخارج على المتقدمين القائلين بذلك (الَّذي تلقاه الفحولُ بِالقبولِ وَ) ذلك الاعتراض هو أنَّهُ إذا أُريدَ أَنَّهُ جزءٌ لَهُ في الخارج؛ فَمَمْنوع، بل هو أوَّلُ (المسألة) المتنازع فيها (وإنْ أُريدَ أَنَّهُ جزءٌ لهُ في الذَّاتِ ؛ فَلَا نسلّمُ أنَّ الجزء الذهني للموجودِ الخارجي يجب أن يكون موجوداً في الخارج وَ) وجه (ذلك) اندفاعِ الظَّاهِرُ (لأنَّ الجزء مَا يتقوَّمُ بِهِ الشَّيءُ وَلَا تعلَّقَ لَهُ بالخارج والذهنِ؛ بل تتقوَّمُ بِهِ الماهيَّةُ مَعَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنِ الوجود والعدم، نَعَمْ إِنَّهُ ينقسم إلى خارجي غيرِ (مَحمولٍ كَالخشب لِلسَّرير والجدار للبيتِ، فَلَا يُقالُ: البيتُ جدارٌ ; لأنَّ الحمل يقتضي الاتحاد في الوجودِ، والجزء الخارجي للشَّيء لهُ وُجودٌ مُتقدِّم عليه في الخارج، فَلَهُ وجودٌ مُغاير لوجود المركَبِ لِتقدُّمِهِ عليهِ، فلو حصلَ لَهُ مَعَ المركَبِ وجودٌ أَخَرُ؛ كانَ لَهُ وُجودانِ حينئذ ، وَهُوَ مُحالٌ (وذهني محمولٍ) في قولنا مثلاً : الإنسانُ حيوان، قالوا: وجزء الماهِيَّةِ إِنْ أخذَ بشرط لا شيء؛ أي: بشرط ا أنْ لا يكون معه زيادة مشخّصة، لا يكون محمولاً ، وإِنْ أُخِذَ مِنْ حيثُ هُوَ هُوَ ؛ أي: مِن غيرِ التِفَاتٍ إلى أنْ يكونَ مَعَهُ شيءٌ أو لا يكونُ؛ كانَ محمولاً، فَقَولُهُ: (بحسب اختلافِ اعتبارِهِ بِشرط لا شيء) المنافي لِلحَمْلِ وَلَا بشرط شيءٍ) المصحّحُ له ; راجع للجزء الذهني، وأمَّا الجزء الخارجي; فَلَا يحملُ عَلَى مَا حَقَّقَ في مَوضِعِهِ مِنْ كُتُبِ الحكمة والكلام المبسوطة، فإنَّ هذه المسألة شهيرة أطالوا فيها الكلام، وقد ذكرَهَا السَّيِّدُ في كثيرٍ مِنْ مُؤلَّفاتِهِ
So the verified position is that the natural universal is a mental consideration, having no existence externally at all. What exists externally are particulars in the form of the universal imprinted in the mind. ... (continues: the part is what constitutes the thing, and we do not mean by part except that by which the thing is constituted. Then he gave a sensible example: the triangle, which is a surface bounded by three lines; it is not constituted without the line because the line is its part. The part is considered irrespective of its existence or non-existence, because the discussion is about the constitution of the composite quiddity by its part. If it is established that it is constituted by the part and then it acquires external existence, its part must exist externally due to the unity of whole and part in the locus of existence. The gist of the proof is that there is no doubt that some individuals (like man) share with others (like horse) in something (animality), disregarding existence and its accidents. That common thing constitutes the individuals in themselves, i.e. disregarding existence and its accidents. As for their definition of Zayd as 'rational animal', it is a definition of his conceptual quiddity, not the reality. ... (then the objection is repelled:) the part is that by which the thing is constituted, and it has no relation to external or mental; rather the quiddity is constituted by it irrespective of existence and non-existence. Indeed, it divides into external (not predicated) like wood for a bed and wall for a house, so one does not say 'the house is a wall' because predication requires unity in existence, and the external part has an existence prior to the composite, so it has an existence different from the composite; if it had another existence along with the composite, it would have two existences, which is impossible. And mental (predicated) as in our saying 'man is animal'. The part of the quiddity, if taken with the condition of nothing else, is not predicated; if taken as it is, it is predicated. So his statement (according to the difference of its consideration: with the condition of nothing else, which prevents predication, and without condition, which corrects it) refers to the mental part. As for the external part, it is not predicated, as established elsewhere.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is a critical defense of the view that the natural universal (al-kulli al-tabi'i) is a mental consideration (amr i'tibari) with no external existence. The commentary of al-‘Attar builds on al-Dasuqi's text to refute the objection of later scholars who deny the existence of the natural universal externally. The core argument is that the part which constitutes a quiddity is considered abstracted from existence and non-existence; the quiddity is constituted by its parts in the mind, not necessarily in external reality. The example of the triangle illustrates that the line (as a part) is essential for the concept of the triangle, regardless of whether the triangle exists externally. The passage then distinguishes two types of parts: external parts (like wood for a bed) which are not predicated because they have a separate existence prior to the composite, and mental parts (like animal for man) which are predicated when taken without condition (la bi-shart). This distinction resolves the objection: the part required for the constitution of the quiddity is a mental part, not an external one, so its existence externally is not necessary. The commentary emphasizes that the definition of a particular (like Zayd as 'rational animal') is a definition of his conceptual quiddity (mahiya i'tibariyya), not the external reality. Finally, the passage notes that this issue is well-known in the books of philosophy and kalam, and al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani discussed it extensively.

Key Terms

جزء
اعتبارى
ماهية

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the objection that the part of an external thing must exist externally fail?
A) Because the part is always external and exists prior to the whole.
B) Because the part is considered in the quiddity regardless of existence; the quiddity's constitution is conceptual.
C) Because the natural universal is not a part at all.
D) Because the part only exists in the mind, not in reality.

Key Takeaway
The natural universal is a mental consideration; its parts are conceptual constituents of the quiddity, not external existents.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
6 min
External vs Mental Parts and Predication
Author distinguishes external and mental parts regarding predication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish external parts from mental parts and explain why mental parts can be predicated while external parts cannot.

Core Idea
Parts are of two kinds: external, which are not predicated, and mental, which are predicated when taken without condition.

The passage introduces a crucial distinction: parts of a thing can be external (like wood for a bed) or mental (like animal for man). This distinction affects predication: we say 'man is animal' but not 'bed is wood'. This is because predication requires unity in existence, but external parts have an existence prior to the composite, preventing that unity. Understanding this distinction is essential for correct definition and logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of parts
The passage distinguishes external parts (e.g., wood for a bed, wall for a house) and mental parts (e.g., animal for man). External parts are not predicated; mental parts can be predicated.
2
Understand the reason for external parts
External parts have an existence prior to and separate from the composite. Predication requires unity in existence, but here the part and whole have different existences. If the part were to have another existence along with the composite, it would have two existences, which is impossible.
3
Understand the condition for mental parts
Mental parts can be predicated when taken without condition (la bi-shart). If taken with the condition of nothing else (bi-shart la shay'), they prevent predication because they are too abstract.
4
Examine the examples
'Man is animal' is valid because animal is a mental part of the quiddity of man. 'The house is wall' is invalid because wall is an external part with independent existence.
5
Apply to definitions
Definitions should consist of mental parts (genus and differentia), not external parts. This is why definitions like 'man is rational animal' work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَلِذا كانَ التَّحقيقُ : أنَّ الكُلّي الطَّبيعي أمر اعتباري لا وجود له خارجاً أصلاً، والموجود في الخارج جزئيات على صورةِ الكُلّيّ المرتسمة في العقلِ . ذاتيا كما حَقَّقَهُ السَّيِّدُ في حاشيةِ التَّجريدِ؟ (بل نجزمُ بأَنَّهُ) أي : الشَّيء الَّذِي حمل عليهِ الحيوانُ (مُتقوِّم ذلكَ الشَّيءُ بِهِ ) أي : بالحيوان؛ لأَنَّهُ جزءهُ، فَهُوَ داخل في قوامِهِ وحقيقتِهِ وَلَا نعني بالجزء إِلَّا مَا يتقوَّمُ بِهِ الشَّيء) وعطف قوله : (ولا يمكنُ تحصيلُ ما هَيَّتِهِ بِدونِهِ) تَفسيرُ، ثُمَّ مَثَلَ لِذلكَ بِمثالٍ مَحسوس فقال : (كَالمُثَلَّثِ) أي : السَّطحِ المثلث وهو ما أحاطَ بِهِ ثلاث خطوط (فَإِنَّهُ لا يتقوَّمُ وَلَا يتحصَّلُ بدونِ الخطّ لأنَّ الخطّ جزؤهُ، والمراد جنس الخطّ لأنَّهُ أَحاطَ بِهِ ثلاث خطوط (والشطحُ) المحاط به ؛ لأنَّه جزءهُ الثَّانِي مَعَ قطع النَّظرِ عَنْ وجودِهِ) أي : وجودِ ذلكَ الشَّيء المتقوم بالجزء (وعدمِهِ) وإِنَّما قطعْنَا النَّظَرَ عَن وجودِ ذلكَ الشَّيء وعدمِهِ؛ لأنَّ الكلامَ مَفروضٌ في تقوم الماهِيَّةِ المركَّبةِ بِجزئها ؛ فَيشمل سائرَ الماهيَّاتِ المركَّبة موجودة في الخارج أوَّلاً ، فإذا ثبتَ تَقَوُّمُهَا بِالجزء وعرضَ لَهَا الوجود خارجاً؛ يجب أن يوجد جزؤها خارجاً ضرورة اتحادِ الكُلِّ والجزء في ظرفِ الوجودِ، فَلِذلك قالَ : وَلَا شَلَّ أَنَّ مَا يُنقوَّمُ بهِ الموجود يجب أن يكونَ موجوداً، وخُلاصتُهُ) أي: خلاصةُ الدَّليلِ السَّابقِ كَمَا هو المتبادرُ ، لكنَّ الكلام المذكور يفيدُ أَنَّهُ دليلٌ آخَرُ وهوَ الظَّاهِرُ ، وحينئذٍ فَالمراد : وخُلاصة الاستدلال (أَنَّهُ لا شَكٍّ أَنَّ بعضَ الأشخاص) كَالإنسانِ يشارك بعضاً آخَرَ) كَالفرس (دونَ بعض) كَالشَّجرِ (في أمرٍ وهِيَ الحيوانيَّة (مَعَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنِ الوجودِ وَمَا يَتبعُهُ مِنَ العوارضِ) أي : الآثار المترتبة عليه، وإنّما قطعنَا النَّظرَ عَنِ الوجودِ هُنَا؛ لِأَنَّهُ عام لسائر الموجوداتِ، ونحن لم نعتبر المشاركة فيهِ؛ بلْ إِنَّما اعتبرنَاهَا في الماهِيَّةِ المندرج تحتها تلك الأشخاصُ كَمَا مِثْلُنَا فَذلِكَ الأمرُ المشترك تتقوَّمُ بِهِ الأشخاص حد ذاتِهَا أي: مَعَ قطع النَّظرِ عَنِ الوجودِ وَمَا يتبعه من العوارض. وأمَّا قولُهم في تعريف زيدٍ : إِنَّهُ حيوان ناطق ؛ فهو تعريف ماهيته الاعتبارية لا الحقيقة . زاهد : لؤ لم تكن الأشياء حاصلةٌ بِنَفْسِهَا في الخارج؛ لم تكن حاصلةٌ بِنَفْسِهَا في الذهن، ولم تكن الذَّاتِيَّاتُ مُتحقِّقَةٌ في الوجودَينِ ا.هـ. ومَبْناهُ مَا سَلَفَ مِنْ أَنَّ التحقيق أنَّ الحاصل في الذهن هو نَفْسُ الماهية الموجودةِ خَارِجاً؛ لا شبحُها ومثالُها (فَـ) ظهرَ أنَّ جزء الموجودِ مَوجودٌ (اندفع الاعتراض) مِنَ المتأخّرينَ المنكرين لوجودِ الكُلِّيِّ الطَّبيعي في الخارج على المتقدمين القائلين بذلك (الَّذي تلقاه الفحولُ بِالقبولِ وَ) ذلك الاعتراض هو أنَّهُ إذا أُريدَ أَنَّهُ جزءٌ لَهُ في الخارج؛ فَمَمْنوع، بل هو أوَّلُ (المسألة) المتنازع فيها (وإنْ أُريدَ أَنَّهُ جزءٌ لهُ في الذَّاتِ ؛ فَلَا نسلّمُ أنَّ الجزء الذهني للموجودِ الخارجي يجب أن يكون موجوداً في الخارج وَ) وجه (ذلك) اندفاعِ الظَّاهِرُ (لأنَّ الجزء مَا يتقوَّمُ بِهِ الشَّيءُ وَلَا تعلَّقَ لَهُ بالخارج والذهنِ؛ بل تتقوَّمُ بِهِ الماهيَّةُ مَعَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنِ الوجود والعدم، نَعَمْ إِنَّهُ ينقسم إلى خارجي غيرِ (مَحمولٍ كَالخشب لِلسَّرير والجدار للبيتِ، فَلَا يُقالُ: البيتُ جدارٌ ; لأنَّ الحمل يقتضي الاتحاد في الوجودِ، والجزء الخارجي للشَّيء لهُ وُجودٌ مُتقدِّم عليه في الخارج، فَلَهُ وجودٌ مُغاير لوجود المركَبِ لِتقدُّمِهِ عليهِ، فلو حصلَ لَهُ مَعَ المركَبِ وجودٌ أَخَرُ؛ كانَ لَهُ وُجودانِ حينئذ ، وَهُوَ مُحالٌ (وذهني محمولٍ) في قولنا مثلاً : الإنسانُ حيوان، قالوا: وجزء الماهِيَّةِ إِنْ أخذَ بشرط لا شيء؛ أي: بشرط ا أنْ لا يكون معه زيادة مشخّصة، لا يكون محمولاً ، وإِنْ أُخِذَ مِنْ حيثُ هُوَ هُوَ ؛ أي: مِن غيرِ التِفَاتٍ إلى أنْ يكونَ مَعَهُ شيءٌ أو لا يكونُ؛ كانَ محمولاً، فَقَولُهُ: (بحسب اختلافِ اعتبارِهِ بِشرط لا شيء) المنافي لِلحَمْلِ وَلَا بشرط شيءٍ) المصحّحُ له ; راجع للجزء الذهني، وأمَّا الجزء الخارجي; فَلَا يحملُ عَلَى مَا حَقَّقَ في مَوضِعِهِ مِنْ كُتُبِ الحكمة والكلام المبسوطة، فإنَّ هذه المسألة شهيرة أطالوا فيها الكلام، وقد ذكرَهَا السَّيِّدُ في كثيرٍ مِنْ مُؤلَّفاتِهِ
So the verified position is that the natural universal is a mental consideration, having no existence externally at all. What exists externally are particulars in the form of the universal imprinted in the mind. ... (continues: the part is what constitutes the thing, and we do not mean by part except that by which the thing is constituted. Then he gave a sensible example: the triangle, which is a surface bounded by three lines; it is not constituted without the line because the line is its part. The part is considered irrespective of its existence or non-existence, because the discussion is about the constitution of the composite quiddity by its part. If it is established that it is constituted by the part and then it acquires external existence, its part must exist externally due to the unity of whole and part in the locus of existence. The gist of the proof is that there is no doubt that some individuals (like man) share with others (like horse) in something (animality), disregarding existence and its accidents. That common thing constitutes the individuals in themselves, i.e. disregarding existence and its accidents. As for their definition of Zayd as 'rational animal', it is a definition of his conceptual quiddity, not the reality. ... (then the objection is repelled:) the part is that by which the thing is constituted, and it has no relation to external or mental; rather the quiddity is constituted by it irrespective of existence and non-existence. Indeed, it divides into external (not predicated) like wood for a bed and wall for a house, so one does not say 'the house is a wall' because predication requires unity in existence, and the external part has an existence prior to the composite, so it has an existence different from the composite; if it had another existence along with the composite, it would have two existences, which is impossible. And mental (predicated) as in our saying 'man is animal'. The part of the quiddity, if taken with the condition of nothing else, is not predicated; if taken as it is, it is predicated. So his statement (according to the difference of its consideration: with the condition of nothing else, which prevents predication, and without condition, which corrects it) refers to the mental part. As for the external part, it is not predicated, as established elsewhere.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is a critical defense of the view that the natural universal (al-kulli al-tabi'i) is a mental consideration (amr i'tibari) with no external existence. The commentary of al-‘Attar builds on al-Dasuqi's text to refute the objection of later scholars who deny the existence of the natural universal externally. The core argument is that the part which constitutes a quiddity is considered abstracted from existence and non-existence; the quiddity is constituted by its parts in the mind, not necessarily in external reality. The example of the triangle illustrates that the line (as a part) is essential for the concept of the triangle, regardless of whether the triangle exists externally. The passage then distinguishes two types of parts: external parts (like wood for a bed) which are not predicated because they have a separate existence prior to the composite, and mental parts (like animal for man) which are predicated when taken without condition (la bi-shart). This distinction resolves the objection: the part required for the constitution of the quiddity is a mental part, not an external one, so its existence externally is not necessary. The commentary emphasizes that the definition of a particular (like Zayd as 'rational animal') is a definition of his conceptual quiddity (mahiya i'tibariyya), not the external reality. Finally, the passage notes that this issue is well-known in the books of philosophy and kalam, and al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani discussed it extensively.

Key Terms

جزء ذهني
بشرط لا شيء
لا بشرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is it invalid to say 'The house is a wall' according to this passage?
A) Because 'wall' is a mental part and cannot be predicated.
B) Because 'wall' is an external part with an existence prior to the house, so predication would require unity in existence which is absent.
C) Because the house is not made of wall.
D) Because the wall is not a part of the quiddity of house.

Key Takeaway
External parts are not predicated because they exist separately; mental parts are predicated when considered without condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Identify whether the following are external or mental parts: (a) foundation for a house, (b) rationality for a human, (c) ink for a written contract. Explain why each is or is not predicated.
?

The Three Types of Universals and the Scope of Logic
Author distinguishes three universals and explains why only natural is investigated in logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the logical, rational, and natural universals and identify which one is investigated in logic.

Core Idea
Logic examines only the natural universal because it aids in reasoning; others are excluded.

This passage from Tadhheeb Sharh clarifies three kinds of universals. Understanding their differences is crucial because logic is only concerned with the natural universal, which appears in definitions and syllogisms. The logical and rational universals are not needed for logical inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three universals
In the passage, locate the names: logical universal (al-kullī al-manṭiqī), rational universal (al-kullī al-'aqlī), natural universal (al-kullī al-ṭabī'ī).
2
Understand their definitions
From the commentary, note that the logical universal is predicated of many in the mind, the rational is even more abstract, and the natural is the essence found externally.
3
Analyze why only the natural is relevant to logic
The commentary explains that logic aims to lead to the unknown, and only the natural universal functions as genus, species, and differentia in definitions, thus it is essential.
4
Explore the justification for including the natural universal
It is included not as a metaphysical claim but to clarify examples used in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا الكلِّيُّ المنطقي والعقلي، فلم يثبت وجودهما في الخارج، والنظر فيه خارج عن الصناعة، الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : فَلَمْ يَثْبُتْ وُجُوْدُهُمَا فِي الْخَارج أي : لأنَّ وجودَهُما في الخارج يقتضي تَشخُصهما، وهو يُنافي كُلِّيَّتَهما . (قَوْلُهُ : خَارِجٌ عَن الصَّنَاعَةِ) أي: صناعة أهل المنطق؛ أي: خارج عن فنّ المنطق؛ لأنَّهُ إنَّما يبحث فيه عن المعلوماتِ التَّصوريَّةِ والتصديقيَّةِ مِن حيثُ إنَّها توصل إلى مجهول، والتَّوصُّلُ المذكورُ لا يتوقَّفُ على وجودِهما في الخارج. (قَوْلُهُ: وَالنَّظَرُ فِيْهِ) أي: في وجودِهِمَا خارجٌ عَنِ الصَّناعة؛ أي: صناعة المنطقِ؛ لأنَّها باحثةٌ عَمَّا لَهُ دَخل في الإيصال، قالَ الرَّازِيُّ في شرح الرسالة : لأنَّ فلهذا تَرَكَ البحث عن وجودهما . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : فَلِهَذَا ) أي : فَلِأجل أنَّ البحث عن وجودِهِما خارج؛ ترك المصنِّفُ البحث عن وجودِهِمَا، وتعرَّضَ لوجودِ الطَّبيعي ؛ لتعلقِ الغرض به؛ لأنَّه يوصل للمجهولِ التَّصوريِّ ؛ لأنَّهُ يكونُ جنساً ونوعاً، وفصلاً . وقد يُقالُ : إِنَّ البحث عن وجودِ الطَّبيعي أيضاً خارج عن الصناعة؛ لأنَّه من مسائل الحكمة الإلهية الباحثة عن أحوال الموجوداتِ مِن حيثُ إِنَّها موجودة، فَالأَظهَرُ أَنْ يُقالَ : إِنَّهُ بَيَّنَ وجودَ الطَّبيعي؛ لأنَّ فيه توضيحاً للأمثلةِ الَّتي مثلُوا بها لِلكُلِّي المنطقي ؛ كَحيوان وإنسان وناطق وضاحك وماشي. وهذا يُسوّغ البحث عنه في كتب الفنّ، وترك البحث عن وجودِ المنطقي؛ معَ أنَّ فيه توضيحاً لمفهوم المنطقي (۱) ؛ لأنَّ العادة هي التوضيح بالأمثلة، وترك البحث عن وجودِ العقليّ ؛ لمزيدِ غموضه . (قَوْلُهُ : : البَحْثِ عَنْ وجُودِهِمَا ) أي : في الخارج.
As for the logical universal and the rational universal, their existence in the external world has not been established, and inquiry into them is outside the art. Dassuqi: 'His statement: their existence in the external world has not been established means: because their existence in the external world would require their individuation, which contradicts their universality. His statement: outside the art means: the art of logicians; i.e., outside the discipline of logic, because logic only investigates conceptual and assertoric information insofar as it leads to the unknown, and the aforementioned leading does not depend on their existence in the external world. His statement: inquiry into them means: into their existence, outside the art, i.e., the art of logic, because it investigates only what has a role in conveyance. Al-Razi said in his commentary on the Risala: 'For this reason he omitted the investigation of their existence.' Dassuqi: 'His statement: for this reason means: because the investigation of their existence is outside, the author omitted the investigation of their existence and addressed the natural universal, due to the objective being connected to it, because it leads to the conceptual unknown, since it is genus, species, and differentia. It might be said: the investigation of the existence of the natural universal is also outside the art, because it is from the issues of divine philosophy investigating the states of existents insofar as they exist. So it is more apparent to say: he clarified the existence of the natural universal because it contains clarification of the examples they gave for the logical universal, like 'animal', 'man', 'rational', 'laughing', 'walking'. This justifies investigating it in books of the art. And he omitted investigating the existence of the logical universal, even though it contains clarification of the concept of the logical universal, because the custom is clarification by examples. And he omitted investigating the rational universal due to its greater obscurity. His statement: the investigation of their existence means: in the external world.'
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on a matn statement about universals. The author distinguishes three types of universals: logical (kulli mantiqi), rational (kulli 'aqli), and natural (kulli tabi'i). The logical universal is the concept considered as a universal in the mind, e.g., the concept 'animal' as applying to many. The rational universal is even more abstract, something like the mental concept considered purely in itself. The natural universal is the essence or nature considered in the external world, e.g., animal-ness existing in individual animals. The matn states that the logical and rational universals have not been proven to exist externally. The commentary explains why: if they existed externally, they would be individuated by matter and accidents, thus losing their universality. Logic only cares about whether concepts lead to unknown conclusions; the external existence of these universals is irrelevant. However, the natural universal is discussed in logic because it serves as examples (like 'animal' in a syllogism) and because it functions as genus, species, and differentia in definitions. An objection is raised: investigating the natural universal belongs to metaphysics, not logic. The commentator answers that it is included in logic only for clarifying examples, which is customary. The logical universal is omitted because its concept is understood through examples, and the rational universal is too obscure to be discussed. Thus the passage clarifies the scope of logic: it deals with universals only insofar as they aid in reasoning, not as metaphysical entities.

Key Terms
الكلي المنطقي
الكلي العقلي
الكلي الطبيعي
الصناعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The logical universal
B) The rational universal
C) The natural universal
D) All three

Key Takeaway
Logic only concerns itself with the natural universal, which functions in definitions and syllogisms, not with the metaphysical existence of universals.

?
1. Provide an example of a natural universal from Fiqh (e.g., 'salat' as an act of worship) and explain how it functions in a syllogism.
?
2. Identify which type of universal is used in the following statement: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' – natural, logical, or rational? Justify.

Why Logical and Rational Universals Cannot Exist Externally
Author explains impossibility of external existence for logical and rational universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that external existence of logical and rational universals would contradict their universality.

Core Idea
If a universal existed externally, it would be individuated and lose its universality.

This part of the commentary provides a logical reason why logical and rational universals are not investigated in logic: their external existence is impossible because it would lead to a contradiction. Understanding this argument clarifies the scope of logical inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Locate the claim
The passage states: 'their existence in the external world has not been established.'
2
Find the justification
Dassuqi says: 'because their existence would require their individuation, which contradicts their universality.'
3
Break down the reasoning
If something exists externally, it must have specific characteristics (individuation). But a universal by definition applies to many; individuation makes it particular. Hence, external existence and universality are incompatible.
4
Connect to logic's purpose
Logic only needs concepts that can be predicated; it does not require external existence, so this incompatibility is not a problem.
