The Purpose of Logic: Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Defines the purpose of logic and distinguishes tasawwur from tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (judgment) and explain the purpose of logic according to this text.

Core Idea
Logic evaluates thought's soundness in acquiring both concepts and judgments.

In this passage, the original author sets out the very purpose of logic: to know correct from corrupt thought. He then identifies two kinds of unknowns—concepts (tasawwurat) and judgments (tasdiqat)—and declares that logic serves both, each with its own principles and aims. The commentators unpack each term to show how this framework grounds the rest of the science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of logic
Read the matn statement: 'the purpose of logic is to know the correctness of thought and its corruption.' Note that logic is not about acquiring knowledge directly, but about the tool for evaluating thought.
2
Define 'thought' (al-fikr)
Al-Dasuqi explains that thought is 'arranging known matters to arrive at the unknown.' Sound thought meets certain conditions; unsound does not. This makes thought a process, not a single act.
3
Distinguish the two kinds of unknowns
The text divides unknowns into conceptual (tasawwuri) and judgmental (tasdiqi). Tasawwur is apprehension of a singular (e.g., 'human'). Tasdiq is apprehension of a relation (e.g., 'human is rational'). Identify examples of each from Islamic sciences.
4
Relate logic's two sides to principles and aims
Logic has two sides: concepts (tasawwurat) and judgments (tasdiqat). Each has mabadi' (principles) and maqasid (aims). The aims are what provide the unknown concepts and judgments—i.e., the explanatory statement (qawl sharh) for concepts and proofs (hujaj) for judgments.
Al-Attar notes a variant reading: 'two ways' (tariqan) instead of 'two sides' (tarafan), which simplifies the interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِى المُعَرِّفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ
[ تمهيد]
فَصْلٌ: فِي المُعَرِّفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ
اعلَمْ أَنَّ الغَرَضَ مِنَ المنطق معرفة صحة الفكر وفساده.
والفكر إما لتحصيل المجهولاتِ التَّصَوُّريَّةِ أو التَّصديقيَّة، فيكونُ للمنطقِ طرفانِ : تصورات وتصديقات، ولكلّ منهما مبادئ ومقاصد.
الدسوقي
فصل: في المعرِّفِ
أي في بيان ماهية المعرف
(قَوْلُهُ : وَأَقْسَامِهِ ) أي : من الحدّ التّامّ والنَّاقص، والرَّسم التَّامِ والنَّاقص.
(قَوْلُهُ : أَنَّ الْغَرَضَ) أي : المقصود.
و
(قَوْلُهُ : الْفِكْر ) أي : ترتيب أمورٍ معلومةٍ للتَّوصُّل إلى مجهول، وحينئذٍ؛ فصحَتُهُ عبارةٌ عن استجماعِهِ الشُّروط ، وفَسادُهُ : عبارةٌ عن عدم استجماعِهَا . (قَوْلُهُ: طَرَفَانِ) أي : مُجزآن . (قَوْلُهُ تَصَوُّرَاتٌ أَي : ما أفاد التصوراتِ من القولِ الشَّارح، والتصور إدراك المفرد. (قَوْلُهُ : وَتَصْدِيقَاتُ) أي : ما أفادَها من الحجج، والتصديقُ إدراك النسبة. (قَوْلُهُ: وَلِكُلِّ مِنْهُمَا ) أي : من التَّصوُّرات المجهولة، والتصديقات المجهولة. (قَوْلُهُ: وَمَقَاصِدُ) أي : مفيدٌ لِتلكَ التَّصوراتِ والتصديقات.
العطار
فصل: في المعرفِ وأقسامِهِ
أي: في تعريفه وما يتفرّعُ عليهِ مِمَّا يصحُ التّعريفُ بِهِ وأقسامِهِ إِلى الحدّ والرَّسم التّامّ والنَّاقص.
(قَوْلُهُ : اِعْلَمْ أَنَّ الْغَرَضَ ... إِلَخ) هذا تمهيدٌ لِقَولِ المصنفِ : معرّفُ الشَّيء . . . إلخ . (قَوْلُهُ : فَيَكُونُ لِلْمَنْطِقِ طَرَفَانِ) أي : قِسْمَانِ، وفي نُسخةٍ : طَريقانِ، فَيُرادُ مِنَ المنطقِ حينَئِذٍ مَقاصِدُ التَّصوراتِ ومَقاصِدُ التَّصديقات، وسقط ما تكلَّفَ بِهِ البعضُ هُنَا .
Chapter: On the Definer and Its Divisions
[Introduction]
Chapter: On the Definer and Its Divisions
Know that the purpose of logic is to know the correctness of thought and its corruption.
Thought is either for acquiring conceptual unknowns or judgmental unknowns. So logic has two sides: concepts and judgments. For each of them there are principles and aims.
Al-Dasuqi:
Chapter: On the Definer
That is, on explaining the essence of the definer.
(His statement: "and its divisions") i.e., from the complete and incomplete definition, and the complete and incomplete description.
(His statement: "the purpose") i.e., the intended aim.
And
(His statement: "thought") i.e., arranging known matters to arrive at the unknown; then its correctness is the gathering of its conditions, and its corruption is the lack of gathering them.
(His statement: "two sides") i.e., two parts.
(His statement: "concepts") i.e., what provides concepts from the explanatory statement; and concept is the apprehension of the singular.
(His statement: "and judgments") i.e., what provides them from proofs; and judgment is the apprehension of the relation.
(His statement: "and for each of them") i.e., from the unknown concepts and unknown judgments.
(His statement: "aims") i.e., what benefits those concepts and judgments.
Al-Attar:
Chapter: On the Definer and Its Divisions
That is, on its definition and what branches from it of what is valid for definition and its divisions into complete and incomplete definition and description.
(His statement: "Know that the purpose...etc.") This is an introduction to the author's statement: "The definer of a thing... etc."
(His statement: "so logic has two sides") i.e., two divisions; and in a copy: "two ways", so then what is meant by logic are the aims of concepts and aims of judgments, and what some have labored over here falls away.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage opens a chapter on the definer (al-mu'arraf) and its divisions. It first presents the matn, a terse statement by the original author, followed by expansive commentary from al-Dasuqi and a note from al-Attar. The matn establishes the purpose of logic: to distinguish sound thought from unsound. Thought is directed either at conceptual unknowns (tasawwurat) or judgmental unknowns (tasdiqat). Thus logic has two aspects: concepts and judgments, each with its own principles and goals.

Al-Dasuqi's commentary unpacks each phrase. He clarifies that the chapter is about the nature of the definer and its divisions into complete and incomplete hadd (definition) and rasm (description). 'Purpose' means the intended aim. 'Thought' is the arrangement of known items to reach the unknown; sound thought fulfills certain conditions, unsound does not. 'Two sides' means two parts. 'Concepts' are what the explanatory statement (qawl sharh) provides; a concept is the apprehension of a singular thing. 'Judgments' are what proofs (hujaj) provide; judgment is the apprehension of a relation (i.e., predication). 'Each of them' refers to unknown concepts and unknown judgments. 'Aims' are what benefit those concepts and judgments.

Al-Attar then adds a refinement. He notes that the chapter title means: defining the definer and explaining its valid branches—complete/incomplete hadd and rasm. He emphasizes that the matn phrase 'Know that the purpose...' is a preamble to the author's later statement 'The definer of a thing...'. Crucially, he addresses a textual variant: 'two sides' (tarafan) versus 'two ways' (tariqan). If read as 'two ways', then 'logic' here means the aims of concepts and aims of judgments, thereby resolving unnecessary complications some commentators introduced. This hashia shows careful attention to textual fidelity and conceptual clarity.

The Definer and Its Divisions: Hadd and Rasm
Defines the definer and its divisions into complete/incomplete hadd and rasm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given definition as complete or incomplete hadd or rasm.

Core Idea
Every definer is either a complete/incomplete definition or a complete/incomplete description.

After establishing logic's purpose, the text turns to the definer itself. The chapter title announces that we will study the definer (al-mu'arraf) and its divisions. Al-Dasuqi immediately breaks it down: the definer is either a complete or incomplete hadd (definition) or a complete or incomplete rasm (description). Al-Attar adds that this includes defining the definer itself. This lesson equips you to recognize and classify these four types.

Guided Path
1
Understand what a definer (mu'arraf) is
The definer is that which defines something. It is not the thing itself but the statement or tool that provides definition. Identify the definer in any definition you encounter.
2
Distinguish hadd from rasm
Hadd (definition) captures the essence (mahiyya) of a thing, typically using genus and differentia. Rasm (description) captures non-essential properties, often using genus and property ('arad). For example, 'human is a rational animal' is a hadd; 'human is a laughing animal' is a rasm.
The text does not give examples, but this is standard in the tradition.
3
Identify complete vs. incomplete for each
Complete hadd includes both genus and differentia; incomplete hadd includes only one (e.g., only genus or only differentia). Complete rasm includes genus and a property; incomplete rasm includes only one. When you see a definition, check how many components it has.
Al-Dasuqi explicitly mentions these four divisions.
4
Apply the classification to a sample definition
Take the definition 'Wine is an intoxicating beverage.' Genus: beverage; differentia: intoxicating. This is a complete hadd. If someone says 'Wine is a beverage' (only genus), it is an incomplete hadd. Practice with other examples from fiqh or kalam.

Chapter: On the Definer and Its Divisions
[Introduction]
Chapter: On the Definer and Its Divisions
Know that the purpose of logic is to know the correctness of thought and its corruption.
Thought is either for acquiring conceptual unknowns or judgmental unknowns. So logic has two sides: concepts and judgments. For each of them there are principles and aims.
Al-Dasuqi:
Chapter: On the Definer
That is, on explaining the essence of the definer.
(His statement: "and its divisions") i.e., from the complete and incomplete definition, and the complete and incomplete description.
(His statement: "the purpose") i.e., the intended aim.
And
(His statement: "thought") i.e., arranging known matters to arrive at the unknown; then its correctness is the gathering of its conditions, and its corruption is the lack of gathering them.
(His statement: "two sides") i.e., two parts.
(His statement: "concepts") i.e., what provides concepts from the explanatory statement; and concept is the apprehension of the singular.
(His statement: "and judgments") i.e., what provides them from proofs; and judgment is the apprehension of the relation.
(His statement: "and for each of them") i.e., from the unknown concepts and unknown judgments.
(His statement: "aims") i.e., what benefits those concepts and judgments.
Al-Attar:
Chapter: On the Definer and Its Divisions
That is, on its definition and what branches from it of what is valid for definition and its divisions into complete and incomplete definition and description.
(His statement: "Know that the purpose...etc.") This is an introduction to the author's statement: "The definer of a thing... etc."
(His statement: "so logic has two sides") i.e., two divisions; and in a copy: "two ways", so then what is meant by logic are the aims of concepts and aims of judgments, and what some have labored over here falls away.

Commentary
This passage opens a chapter on the definer (al-mu'arraf) and its divisions. It first presents the matn, a terse statement by the original author, followed by expansive commentary from al-Dasuqi and a note from al-Attar. The matn establishes the purpose of logic: to distinguish sound thought from unsound. Thought is directed either at conceptual unknowns (tasawwurat) or judgmental unknowns (tasdiqat). Thus logic has two aspects: concepts and judgments, each with its own principles and goals.

Al-Dasuqi's commentary unpacks each phrase. He clarifies that the chapter is about the nature of the definer and its divisions into complete and incomplete hadd (definition) and rasm (description). 'Purpose' means the intended aim. 'Thought' is the arrangement of known items to reach the unknown; sound thought fulfills certain conditions, unsound does not. 'Two sides' means two parts. 'Concepts' are what the explanatory statement (qawl sharh) provides; a concept is the apprehension of a singular thing. 'Judgments' are what proofs (hujaj) provide; judgment is the apprehension of a relation (i.e., predication). 'Each of them' refers to unknown concepts and unknown judgments. 'Aims' are what benefit those concepts and judgments.

Al-Attar then adds a refinement. He notes that the chapter title means: defining the definer and explaining its valid branches—complete/incomplete hadd and rasm. He emphasizes that the matn phrase 'Know that the purpose...' is a preamble to the author's later statement 'The definer of a thing...'. Crucially, he addresses a textual variant: 'two sides' (tarafan) versus 'two ways' (tariqan). If read as 'two ways', then 'logic' here means the aims of concepts and aims of judgments, thereby resolving unnecessary complications some commentators introduced. This hashia shows careful attention to textual fidelity and conceptual clarity.

Defending the Definition of al-Mu'arraf: Essential Conception vs. Accidental Conception
The commentator defends the definition of al-mu'arraf by clarifying the intended conception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why genus and common accident do not refute the definition of al-mu'arraf by distinguishing essential conception from accidental conception.

Core Idea
A definition must convey essential conception, not just any accidental or generic conception.

In logic, the definiendum (al-mu'arraf) is defined as 'what is said about something to give its conception.' An astute objection arises: genus and common accident also give some conception, yet they are not definitions. The commentator defends the definition by specifying that the intended conception is the conventional well-known essential conception, not any partial or accidental conception. This lesson unpacks that crucial refinement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition under attack
Find what is being defined (al-mu'arraf) and its definition: 'what is said about something to give its conception' (مَا يُقَالُ عَلَى الشَّيْءِ لِإِفَادَةِ تَصَوُّرِهِ).
This is the standard definition of the definiendum in classical logic.
2
Understand the objection
Genus (e.g., 'animal' for human) and common accident (e.g., 'walking') are also said about something to give some conception. So the definition would include them, but they are not definitions. Thus the definition would not be exclusive (māni‘).
The objection is that the definition is too broad.
3
Locate the commentator's defense
The commentator says the objection fails because 'conception' (taṣawwur) is not any conception (bi-wajh mā), but the conventionally well-known conception (al-muta‘ārif al-mashhūr) that reveals the essence.
The key phrase: 'لَا يُرَادُ بِالتَّصَوُّرِ تَصَوُّرُهُ بِوَجْهِ مَا'
4
Distinguish types of conception
Accidental conception (from genus or common accident) gives only a partial or accidental understanding; essential conception gives the reality of the thing. Only the latter is intended in the definition.
For example, 'animal' gives a generic conception of human, not the specific essence 'rational animal'.
5
Apply the distinction
Check: does 'animal' give essential conception of human? No, it only gives the genus, not the differentia. Therefore it is not a definition. The same applies to common accident.
This shows why genus and common accident are not counterexamples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ
ولا ينتقض بالجنسِ والعَرَضِ العام مع أنَّهما يُقالانِ على الشَّيء لإفادة تصوره
(قَوْلُهُ: وَلَا يَنْتَقِضُ بِالْجِنْسِ ... إلخ) أي: بحيثُ يكونُ التَّعريفُ غير مانع
لأنَّه لا يراد بالتَّصورِ تصوره بوجه ما، وإلا لجاز أن يكون الأعم والأخص معرّفاً
(قَوْلُهُ : لأَنَّهُ لَا يُرَادُ) علَّة لقوله : لا ينتقض ، وقولُهُ: تصوُّرُه بوجه (ما ) الأَوْلَى : تَصوُّرُهُ وَلَوْ بِوَجهِ مَا
والمتعارفِ المشهور، وهو المراد هَهُنَا
Chapter: On the Definiendum and its Division. And it is not refuted by genus and common accident, even though they are said about something to give its conception. (His statement: 'And it is not refuted by genus... etc.') i.e., such that the definition would not be exclusive. Because what is intended by conception is not its conception in any way; otherwise it would be permissible for the more general and the more specific to be a definition. (His statement: 'Because it is not intended') is the cause for his statement 'it is not refuted', and his statement: 'its conception in any way' — the better [reading] is: its conception even in any way. And the conventionally well-known [conception] is what is meant here.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from the hashiah of al-‘Aṭṭār (with al-Dasūqī’s marginalia) on the definition of 'al-mu‘arraf' (the definiendum). The original definition states: 'al-mu‘arraf is what is said about something to give its conception.' An objection is raised: genus (like 'animal' for man) and common accident (like 'walking' for man) are also said about something to give its conception, yet they are not definitions. Therefore the definition would not be exclusive (māni‘). The commentator defends the definition by clarifying what is meant by 'conception' (taṣawwur). It does not mean just any conception (taṣawwuruhu bi-wajh mā), which would include the incomplete or accidental conception given by genus and accident. Rather, the intended conception is the conventionally well-known type of conception that reveals the reality of the thing (al-muta‘ārif al-mashhūr). Hence, genus and common accident do not give this kind of conception, so the definition remains valid. This is a standard move in logic: the definiendum is defined by the definition that gives essential conception, not any conception. For example, 'human' is defined as 'rational animal' – 'animal' alone gives only a partial conception, not the full essential conception.

Why Common Accident Cannot Define
Explains the rejection of common accident as a definer and the disagreement among logicians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate why common accident fails as a definer and distinguish between the positions of earlier and later logicians on this issue.

Core Idea
Common accident cannot define because it gives neither complete distinction nor insight into essence.

In Islamic logic, definition is meant to either completely distinguish a thing from all others or to provide insight into its essential properties. Common accident, though a universal, does neither adequately. This has led to a notable disagreement: earlier logicians accepted it in incomplete descriptions, while later ones rejected it entirely. In this lesson, we'll unpack the reasoning behind the rejection and see why even combining common accident with essential parts adds no value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about common accident
The passage begins by stating that logicians did not consider definition by common accident. Locate this claim and note that it applies to using common accident alone or in combination with differentia or property.
2
Understand the two purposes of definition
The text explains that definition aims for either complete distinction (tamyiz tamm) from all other things or insight into essential properties (dhatiyyat). Check whether common accident can achieve either.
Example: Defining human as 'breather' fails because other animals also breathe.
3
Examine the dispute between early and later logicians
The commentary shows that earlier logicians (qudama') allowed common accident in an incomplete description (rasm naqis) because it provides some conception. Later logicians (muta'akhkhirun) rejected it entirely for lack of distinction. Identify the arguments for each side.
The later view is presented as the main position; the earlier view is acknowledged but critiqued.
4
Test the claim by combining common accident with differentia or property
The passage argues that if you combine common accident with differentia (fasl) or property (khassa), the distinction is already achieved by the essential part, making the accident redundant. Work through the example: 'walking rational' vs. 'rational' and see that 'walking' adds nothing essential.
Also consider: combining genus with differentia is beneficial because genus reveals part of the essence.
5
Address the counterexample of defining a subgroup by its species
Some object that definitions like 'the Roman is a human born in Rome' use species (human) to define a subgroup (Roman), which seems to contradict the rule. The response is that this is a nominal definition (ta'rif ismi) where 'human' is a nominal genus, not a real species. Identify why this does not challenge the core rule.
This highlights the difference between real definition (hadd haqiqi) and nominal definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ. وَلَم يَعتَبِرُوا التَّعرِيفَ بِالعَرَضِ العَام؛ فلا يصلُحُ مُعَرِّفاً؛ لقصوره عن إفادةِ التَّعريف. قَوْلُهُ: وَلمْ يَعْتَبِرُوا التَّعْرِيْفَ بِالْعَرَضِ الْعَامّ؛ أي: لا وحدَهُ، وَلَا مَضموماً للفصل أو الخاصة، وقولُهُ وَلمْ يعتبرُوا؛ أي أكثرهم، وإِنْ كانَ مُحقِّقوهُم اعتبرُوا التَّعريفَ بِهِ وَلَو وحده؛ لأنَّهُ يفيدُ تَصوُّر المعرَّفِ بوجه ما؛ وَلِأَنَّ ضَمَّهُ معَ الخاصَّة أكملُ من الخاصَّةِ وحدَّها. قَوْلُهُ: فَلَا يَصْلُحُ مُعَرِّفاً؛ لِقُصُوْرِهِ ... إلخ. وذلك لأَنَّكَ إِذا عَرَّفْتَ الإِنسانَ بِأَنَّهُ متنفّس؛ لا يميزه تمييزاً تاما، فَلِذلك لا يصلحُ مُعرّفاً. قَوْلُهُ: فَلَا يَصْلُحُ مُعَرِّفَاً إلى قولِهِ: واعلَمْ، عبارةُ السَّيِّدِ في حاشية القطب، قالَ بعدَهُ: وأمَّا الجنسُ؛ فهو وإنْ لم يكنْ لَهُ مَدخل في التَّعيين؛ لكن له مدخل في الاطلاع على الماهيَّةِ بِمَا هو ذاتي لَهَا، ثمَّ قَالَ: وَهَهُنَا بحث، وهو أَنَّ تَمييزَ الشَّيء ما عداه وقد يكون عن بعضه، والعرضُ العالم قد يفيدُ التَّمييز قد يكون عن جميع الثاني، فينبغي أن يعتبر في التعريف ا.هـ. وفي الحواشي الفتحية: متأخّرو المنطقيين لم يعتبروا العرض العام في التَّعريف أصلاً لعدم إفادته الامتياز عن جميعِ الأغيار، ولا الاطّلاع على شيءٍ من الذَّاتِيَّاتِ، والقدماء اعتبروه لإفادته تصوراً لا يحصل بدونِهِ، وجعلوا المعرّف المشتمل عليه رسماً ناقصاً، فإيراده في مباحثِ الكُلِّيَّاتِ على اصطلاح المتأخرينَ إنَّما هو بالعرض على سبيل الاستطراد، والمشهورُ أنَّ النَّوعَ غيرُ مُعتبر في التَّعريفاتِ عند المنطقيين مطلقاً، وذكره في هذه المباحثِ استطراديّ اتّفاقاً، وفيه بحث لا يخفى؛ لا سيّما على قاعدة القدماء، وقد ردَّ عليهم أنَّ تعريف الصنفِ بالنَّوعِ شائع؛ كما يُقالُ: الرُّومي إنسان متولد في بلادِ الرُّومِ؛ فكيفَ يصحُ حكمُهُم بعدم اعتبارِهِ في التّعريف مُطلقاً؟ وربَّما يُجابُ بأَنَّ تعريف الصنفِ بما ذكر تعريف اسمي لماهية اعتباريَّةٍ، وذكرُ النَّوع فيه إنَّما هو من حيثُ إِنَّهُ جنس اسمي؛ لا من حيثُ إِنَّهُ نوع حقيقي ا.هـ. قالَ الزَّاهديُّ: وكأنَّ اعتبارَهُ في الرُّسومِ النَّاقصة دون الحدودِ النَّاقصة مبني على جواز التعريف بالأعم، والتّعريفُ بالعرض العام وحدَهُ، فإنَّهُ كَمَا لا حاجة إليه مع وجودِ الفصل؛ لا حاجةً إليه مع وجودِ الخاصَّةِ .ا.هـ. وصوَّبَ السَّيِّدُ أنَّ المركَّبَ مِنَ العرضِ العام والخاصَّة ولا جزء معرّف؛ لأنَّه لو كان جزءاً، لكان إمَّا مع الخاصَّة أو الفصل. ولا فائدة في ضمه مع أحدهما فلهذا سَقَطَ العَرَضُ العام من الاعتبار في التعريفات، وإِنَّما ذكر في باب الكليات؛ استيفاء لأقسام الكلي. قَوْلُهُ: وَلَا فَائِدَةَ فِي ضَمِّهِ مَعَ أَحَدِهِمَا؛ أي: لأنَّ تمييز المعرَّفِ تمييزا تاما إِنَّما حصل بذلك الأحد. وقد يُقالُ: هما مُغنيانٍ أيضاً عن الجنس، فإنَّ حيوان مع ناطق أو ضاحك؛ لا فائدة فيه، ويُجابُ: بأنَّ فى ذكره فائدةً لا تؤخذُ منهما، وهي بيان جزء من الماهية بخلاف ما إذا ضَمِمْنَا مُتنفّس مع ناطق، فليس فيه فائدةٌ؛ لأنَّه ليسَ مِن أجزاء الماهية. وقولُهُ وَلَا فائدةً ... إلخ؛ أي: لأنَّ الغرضَ مِن التَّعريفِ إِمَّا التَّمييزُ التَّامُ أو الاطلاع على الذاتيات والعرضُ العالم لا يفيد شيئاً منهما، وحينئذ؛ فَلا فائدة في ضمِّهِ معَ أحدهما؛ إذ تمييز المعرَّفِ التَّمييز التَّامَّ إنّما حصل بأحدهما، وصار ذكرُ العرض مع أحدهما مستغنى عنه. وقوله: (لكانَ إمَّا مع الخاصَّة أو الفصل)؛ أي: لا مع الجنسِ؛ لِمَا عَلَّلَ بِهِ مِن عدم صلاحيّتِهِ مُعرّفاً. واللحاصلُ: أنَّ العرضَ وحدَهُ أو مع الجنس قاصر عن إفادة التعريف؛ لعدم إفادةِ التَّمييز التَّام، وإذا ضُمَّ لِلخاصَّة أو للفصل؛ فَالتَّمييرُ التَّامُ إِنَّما حصل من الخاصَّةِ أو الفصل، والعرضُ لا فائدة فيه. قَوْلُهُ: وَلَا فَائِدَةَ فِي ضَمِّهِ مَعَ أَحَدِهِمَا؛ أي: بحيثُ تعرّفه بالماشي الضاحك، أو الماشي الناطق.
Chapter: On the Definition and Its Divisions. And they did not consider definition by common accident; so it is not suitable as a definer, due to its deficiency in providing definition. His statement: 'And they did not consider definition by common accident' means: neither alone nor combined with the differentia or property, and his statement 'they did not consider' means most of them, although their verifying scholars considered definition by it even alone, because it gives some conception of the defined thing, and because combining it with the property is more complete than the property alone. His statement: 'So it is not suitable as a definer, due to its deficiency...' etc. That is because if you define 'human' as 'a breather,' it does not distinguish him completely, so it is not suitable as a definer. His statement: 'So it is not suitable as a definer' until his saying 'Know that' is the expression of al-Sayyid in the gloss on al-Qutb. He said after it: 'As for genus, although it has no role in specification, it has a role in insight into the quiddity insofar as it is essential to it.' Then he said: 'And here there is a discussion, namely that distinguishing a thing from what is other than it may be from some of them, and common accident may give distinction from all of the second, so it should be considered in definition.' End quote. And in al-Hawashi al-Fathiyya: 'The later logicians did not consider common accident in definition at all, because it does not provide distinction from all others, nor insight into any of the essential properties. The earlier logicians considered it because it provides a conception that is not obtained without it, and they made the definer that includes it an incomplete description. So presenting it in the discussions of universals according to the terminology of the later ones is only incidentally, by way of digression. And the well-known view is that species is absolutely not considered in definitions by the logicians, and mentioning it in these discussions is digressive by agreement. And there is a discussion that is not hidden, especially on the basis of the earlier ones, and they refuted them by saying that defining a subgroup by its species is common, as it is said: 'The Roman is a human born in the land of the Romans.' So how can their judgment that it is absolutely not considered in definition be valid? And perhaps it is answered that defining the subgroup by what is mentioned is a nominal definition of a mental quiddity, and mentioning the species in it is only insofar as it is a nominal genus, not insofar as it is a real species.' End quote. Al-Zahidi said: 'It seems that considering it in incomplete descriptions as opposed to incomplete definitions is based on the permissibility of definition by the more general, and definition by common accident alone, for just as there is no need for it with the existence of the differentia, so too there is no need for it with the existence of the property.' End quote. And al-Sayyid considered that the composite of common accident and property is not a part of a definer, because if it were a part, it would either be with the property or the differentia. And there is no benefit in combining it with either of them. Hence common accident fell out of consideration in definitions, and it is only mentioned in the chapter on universals in order to complete the divisions of the universal. His statement: 'And there is no benefit in combining it with either of them' means because the complete distinction of the defined is only obtained by that one. And it may be said: They two (property and differentia) also make the genus unnecessary, for 'animal with rational' or 'with laughing' has no benefit. And it is answered: In mentioning it there is a benefit not taken from them, namely clarifying a part of the quiddity. Unlike when we combine 'breather' with 'rational', there is no benefit in it because it is not from the parts of the quiddity. And his statement: 'And no benefit...' etc. means because the purpose of definition is either complete distinction or insight into essential properties, and common accident does not benefit either of them. Thus, there is no benefit in combining it with either of them, since complete distinction of the defined is obtained by either one, and mentioning the accident with either one is superfluous. And his statement: 'It would either be with the property or the differentia' means not with the genus, due to the reason he gave of its unsuitability as a definer. The result: The accident alone or with the genus is deficient in providing definition, because it does not provide complete distinction, and if it is combined with the property or the differentia, complete distinction is only obtained from the property or the differentia, and the accident has no benefit. His statement: 'And there is no benefit in combining it with either of them' means, such as defining it as 'the walking laugher' or 'the walking rational'.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on why the common accident ('arad 'amm) is not considered a valid definer (mu'arraf) in Islamic logic. The discussion begins by stating the general rule: logicians do not accept definition by common accident alone or in combination with differentia or property, because it fails to provide complete distinction (tamyiz tamm). The commentary by al-Dasuqi clarifies that this applies to most logicians, but some verifying scholars did allow it alone because it gives some conception, and combining it with property is better than property alone. However, the passage argues that defining 'human' as 'a breather' does not distinguish humans from all other breathing animals. The commentary then cites al-Sayyid's gloss, which discusses the role of genus (jins) in providing insight into essence, and introduces a debate: common accident can sometimes distinguish from all others, so why exclude it entirely? Al-Sayyid mentions that later logicians reject it absolutely, while earlier ones accept it as an incomplete description (rasm naqis). The text also addresses the objection that defining a subgroup (sinaf) by its species is common in everyday speech (e.g., 'the Roman is a human born in the land of the Romans') and that this seems to contradict the rule. The response is that such definitions are nominal (ismi) and involve a nominal genus, not a real one. Al-Zahidi notes that considering common accident in incomplete descriptions is based on the permissibility of definition by the more general. The conclusion is that common accident cannot be a part of a definer because if it were, it would be with property or differentia, but then it adds no benefit since the distinction is already achieved by the essential part. The passage analogizes: if you combine 'breather' with 'rational', there's no benefit because 'breather' is not part of the essence. In contrast, combining 'animal' with 'rational' does benefit by showing a part of the essence. Ultimately, common accident is only mentioned in the chapter on universals for completeness, not as a valid definer. This lesson guards against thinking that any property can define something; a valid definer must either provide complete distinction or insight into essential properties.

التعريف
الخاصة
الفصل
الجنس
الرسم الناقص

A) Because common accident is not part of the essence and the differentia alone already provides complete distinction.
B) Because common accident is more general than the differentia and causes confusion.
C) Because combining them creates a compound definition that is too long.

Key Takeaway
Common accident is not a valid definer because it fails to provide complete distinction from all others and does not reveal essential properties of the defined thing.

The A Fortiori Argument for Specific Definitions
Commentary argues for a fortiori preference of specific definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct an a fortiori argument to justify why defining by a more specific term is preferable.

Core Idea
If a general term can define, the specific term fits even better.

This passage introduces a logical principle: the more specific a definition, the closer it is to the thing defined. The commentator argues that if early scholars allowed definition by a general term, then definition by a more specific term is a fortiori valid. This reasoning is common in Islamic logic and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of definitions discussed
The passage contrasts definition by a more general term (الأعم) and by a more specific term (الأخص).
2
Understand the premise: proximity to the defined
The commentator claims the specific term is closer (أكثر قربا) to the defined object.
3
Apply the a fortiori principle
If they allowed the more general (which is less close), then allowing the more specific (which is closer) is even more appropriate (بطريق الأولى).
This is a 'قياس أولوي'.
4
Reflect on the logical structure
This is a type of analogical reasoning: if A is permitted, then B, which has a stronger reason, is also permitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلتُ: لأنَّ قُرْبَ الأخص إلى المعرَّفِ أكثر من قرب الأعم، فإذا جوزوا التّعريف بالأعم، فتجويز الأخص بطريق الأولى. والمتقدّمونَ المجوّزونَ للتَّعريف بالأعم والأخص يرونَ أنَّ الحدَّ مَا كانَ بالذَّاتِيَّاتِ كُلِّهَا أو بعضها، فإنْ كانَ بِكُلِّها كَالجنس والفصل القريبَيْنِ فحد تام، وإنْ كانَ بِبَعضِهَا كَالجنس فقط، أو الفصل فقط، أو الفصل مع الجنس البعيد فهو حد ناقص، وحينئذ فيجوزُ التَّعريفُ عندَهُم بالجنس فقط، وهو أعمُ مِنَ المعرَّفِ. قال العطار: الشَّيء إذا كان بالخواص كان رسماً كاملاً، وما كان منها أعم أو أخص كان ذلك الرسم رسماً ناقصاً. وإنما لم يمكن الحد بالأخص لأن الحد لا يكون إلا بالذاتي، والذاتي لا يكون إلا أعم أو مساوياً، ويمتنع كون جزء الشَّيء أخص منه.
I said: Because the proximity of the most specific to the defined is greater than the proximity of the more general, so if they allowed definition by the more general, then allowing the most specific is a fortiori. The early scholars who permitted definition by the more general and the most specific hold that the essential definition (hadd) is that which is by all the intrinsic parts (dhatiyyat) or some of them. If it is by all of them, like the proximate genus and differentia, it is a perfect essential definition. If by some of them, like the genus alone, or the differentia alone, or the differentia with the remote genus, it is an imperfect essential definition. Thus, they allow definition by the genus alone, which is more general than the defined. Al-Attar said: If a thing is defined by accidents (khawass), that is a complete descriptive definition (rasm). If any of those accidents is more general or more specific, that descriptive definition is incomplete. The essential definition cannot be by the most specific because essential definition is only by intrinsic parts, and an intrinsic part cannot be more specific than the whole; it must be more general or equal. It is impossible for a part of a thing to be more specific than it, otherwise the whole would exist without its part, which is obviously false.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the rules of definition. It begins by arguing that when defining, the most specific characteristic is preferable to the more general because it is closer to the defined. If definition by the more general is allowed, then definition by the more specific is even more appropriate (a fortiori). The text then presents the opinion of the early scholars who permitted both general and specific definitions. They divided definitions into two types: essential definition (hadd) and descriptive definition (rasm). Essential definition uses the intrinsic parts (dhatiyyat) of the thing: genus and differentia. If all intrinsic parts are used, it is a perfect hadd; if only some, it is imperfect. For example, defining human as 'rational animal' is a perfect hadd, while 'sensitive, growing body' would be imperfect because it uses only the genus. Descriptive definition uses accidents (khawass). Al-Attar's note clarifies that if the accidents used are characteristic (specific and exclusive), the rasm is complete; if they are general or not exclusive, the rasm is incomplete. Finally, the passage explains why an essential definition cannot be by the most specific part: an intrinsic part must be either more general than the whole or equal to it; it cannot be more specific. For instance, 'animal' is more general than 'human', and 'rational' is equal in extension to 'human'. If a part were more specific, the whole could not exist without that part, but the part would be more restricted, leading to absurdity.

Key Terms
الأخص
الأعم
التعريف بالأولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for preferring the most specific definition over the more general?
A) The most specific is easier to remember.
B) The most specific is closer to the essence of the defined.
C) The most specific is shorter.
D) The most specific is used by early scholars.

Key Takeaway
Definition by a more specific term is logically preferable to definition by a more general term.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct an a fortiori argument in usul al-fiqh: if selling wine is prohibited due to intoxication, then selling stronger intoxicants is even more prohibited. Explain how this mirrors the argument for specific definitions.

Perfect and Imperfect Essential Definitions (Hadd)
Commentary classifies hadd into perfect (using all dhatiyyat) and imperfect (using some).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given hadd as perfect or imperfect by checking whether it uses both proximate genus and differentia.

Core Idea
A perfect hadd uses both proximate genus and differentia; an imperfect hadd uses only one or a remote genus.

The early scholars who allowed definitions by both general and specific terms divided essential definitions into perfect and imperfect. A perfect hadd includes all intrinsic parts (proximate genus and differentia), while an imperfect hadd omits some. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the completeness of definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Pick the term being defined (e.g., 'human' in the examples).
2
Determine its intrinsic parts
Find the proximate genus (e.g., 'animal') and differentia (e.g., 'rational').
3
Check if both are used in the definition
If both are present, it is a perfect hadd (hadd tam). If only one is used (genus alone, differentia alone, or differentia with remote genus), it is imperfect (hadd naqis).
4
Apply to the examples in the passage
'Rational animal' is perfect; 'sensitive, growing body' is imperfect because it uses only remote genus and no explicit proximate genus or differentia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If it is by all of them, like the proximate genus and differentia, it is a perfect essential definition. If any of those accidents is more general or more specific, that descriptive definition is incomplete. It is impossible for a part of a thing to be more specific than it, otherwise the whole would exist without its part, which is obviously false.

If definition by the more general is allowed, then definition by the more specific is even more appropriate (a fortiori). Descriptive Definitions and the Impossibility of Hadd by the Most Specific
Commentary contrasts complete and incomplete rasm and explains why hadd cannot be by a more specific part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between complete and incomplete descriptive definitions (rasm) and explain why an essential definition cannot be composed of a part more specific than the defined.

Core Idea
Rasm uses accidents; complete rasm uses specific, exclusive accidents; and hadd cannot use a part that is more specific than the whole.

The passage concludes with two important points: first, al-Attar's classification of descriptive definitions (rasm) into complete and incomplete based on the accidents used; second, the logical proof that an essential definition cannot be by a part that is more specific than the whole. These clarify the boundaries of both essential and descriptive definitions.

Guided Path
1
Understand rasm (descriptive definition)
Rasm uses accidental properties (خواص) rather than intrinsic parts.
2
Differentiate complete from incomplete rasm
If the accidents are specific and exclusive (خاص), the rasm is complete; if they are more general or not exclusive, it is incomplete.
3
Recall the requirement for hadd
Hadd must be by intrinsic parts (ذاتيات).
4
Prove impossibility of hadd by the most specific
An intrinsic part cannot be more specific than the whole because a part must be more general or equal; if a part were more specific, the whole would not necessarily contain it.
5
Apply to examples
'Animal' is more general than 'human', 'rational' is equal. A part like 'palm' is more specific than 'human'? No, not a part. So hadd cannot use something like 'laughter' (an accident).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلتُ: لأنَّ قُرْبَ الأخص إلى المعرَّفِ أكثر من قرب الأعم، فإذا جوزوا التّعريف بالأعم، فتجويز الأخص بطريق الأولى. والمتقدّمونَ المجوّزونَ للتَّعريف بالأعم والأخص يرونَ أنَّ الحدَّ مَا كانَ بالذَّاتِيَّاتِ كُلِّهَا أو بعضها، فإنْ كانَ بِكُلِّها كَالجنس والفصل القريبَيْنِ فحد تام، وإنْ كانَ بِبَعضِهَا كَالجنس فقط، أو الفصل فقط، أو الفصل مع الجنس البعيد فهو حد ناقص، وحينئذ فيجوزُ التَّعريفُ عندَهُم بالجنس فقط، وهو أعمُ مِنَ المعرَّفِ. قال العطار: الشَّيء إذا كان بالخواص كان رسماً كاملاً، وما كان منها أعم أو أخص كان ذلك الرسم رسماً ناقصاً. وإنما لم يمكن الحد بالأخص لأن الحد لا يكون إلا بالذاتي، والذاتي لا يكون إلا أعم أو مساوياً، ويمتنع كون جزء الشَّيء أخص منه.
I said: Because the proximity of the most specific to the defined is greater than the proximity of the more general, so if they allowed definition by the more general, then allowing the most specific is a fortiori. The early scholars who permitted definition by the more general and the most specific hold that the essential definition (hadd) is that which is by all the intrinsic parts (dhatiyyat) or some of them. If it is by all of them, like the proximate genus and differentia, it is a perfect essential definition. If by some of them, like the genus alone, or the differentia alone, or the differentia with the remote genus, it is an imperfect essential definition. Thus, they allow definition by the genus alone, which is more general than the defined. Al-Attar said: If a thing is defined by accidents (khawass), that is a complete descriptive definition (rasm). If any of those accidents is more general or more specific, that descriptive definition is incomplete. The essential definition cannot be by the most specific because essential definition is only by intrinsic parts, and an intrinsic part cannot be more specific than the whole; it must be more general or equal. It is impossible for a part of a thing to be more specific than it, otherwise the whole would exist without its part, which is obviously false.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the rules of definition. It begins by arguing that when defining, the most specific characteristic is preferable to the more general because it is closer to the defined. If definition by the more general is allowed, then definition by the more specific is even more appropriate (a fortiori). The text then presents the opinion of the early scholars who permitted both general and specific definitions. They divided definitions into two types: essential definition (hadd) and descriptive definition (rasm). Essential definition uses the intrinsic parts (dhatiyyat) of the thing: genus and differentia. If all intrinsic parts are used, it is a perfect hadd; if only some, it is imperfect. For example, defining human as 'rational animal' is a perfect hadd, while 'sensitive, growing body' would be imperfect because it uses only the genus. Descriptive definition uses accidents (khawass). Al-Attar's note clarifies that if the accidents used are characteristic (specific and exclusive), the rasm is complete; if they are general or not exclusive, the rasm is incomplete. Finally, the passage explains why an essential definition cannot be by the most specific part: an intrinsic part must be either more general than the whole or equal to it; it cannot be more specific. For instance, 'animal' is more general than 'human', and 'rational' is equal in extension to 'human'. If a part were more specific, the whole could not exist without that part, but the part would be more restricted, leading to absurdity.

Key Terms
الرسم التام
الرسم الناقص
الخواص
الذاتي

A) Because parts are always more general.
B) Because a part cannot be more specific, otherwise the whole could exist without it.
C) Because the early scholars forbade it.
D) Because accidents must be used.

Key Takeaway
An essential definition must use intrinsic parts that are either more general or equal to the defined, never more specific.

Why the Totally Different Cannot Define
Commentary explains why mubayin is excluded from valid definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why mubayin (totally different) is invalid as a definition and apply the predication condition (haml) to test candidate definitions.

Core Idea
A valid definition must be predicable of the defined; the totally different fails this test.

In classical logic, not every expression that relates to the defined can serve as a definition. The commentators noted that the author omitted 'mubayin' (totally different) from the list of invalid definientes, even though it is also invalid. This lesson unpacks the reason: the condition of predication (haml) excludes both the totally different and the more specific. Understanding this helps us grasp the foundational rule that the definiens must be truly said of every instance of the definiendum.

Guided Path
1
Identify the listed invalid definientes
From the passage, list the types that cannot serve as definitions: al-a'amm (more general), al-akhass (more specific), al-musawi (coextensive), and al-akhfa (more hidden). Note that mubayin is not mentioned.
This list is from the original matn.
2
Notice the omission of mubayin
The commentator observes that al-mubayin (totally different) is left out, though it is also invalid. Why would the author skip it? Consider the relationship between al-a'amm and al-mubayin.
Mubayin is more remote than al-a'amm.
3
Understand the first explanation (a fortiori)
The commentator says: 'if definition by the more general is not allowed, then the totally different is even more ineligible, due to its extreme remoteness from the defined.' This is an argument from greater to lesser (a fortiori).
This is a valid rhetorical/logical move.
4
Grasp al-Attar's refined explanation (predication)
Al-Attar traces the exclusion back to the principle: 'the definer of a thing is that which is said of it' — i.e., predicable (mahmul) of it. Both al-akhass and al-mubayin fail because they are not truly predicable of every individual of the defined.
Predication is the key condition: it must be a universal affirmative.
5
Apply the predication test
Consider the definition 'human is a horse'. Is 'horse' said of every human? No, it is not said at all. That's mubayin. Consider 'human is a scholar'. Is 'scholar' said of every human? No, it is said of some only (akhass). Both fail because they lack universal predication.
Test any candidate definition with: 'Every definiendum is [definiens]'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْمَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ. يذكره اعتماداً على فهم المتعلّم واختصاراً في العبارة، وهذا كما قال في تعداد ما لا يقع معرّفاً: فلا يصح بالأعم، والأخص، والمساوي معرفةً، والأخفى، فترك المباين مع أنه لا يقع معرّفاً أيضاً، وإنما تركه بناءً على أن التعريف لما لم يجز بالأعم، فالمباين بطريق الأولى؛ لأنه في غاية البعد عن المعرّف. وقال العطار: والحق أن المباين والأخص خرجا بقول المصنف: معرف الشيء ما يقال عليه؛ أي ما يصح حمله عليه، وهذان ليسا كذلك.
Section: On the defined (mu'arraf) and its divisions. He mentions it relying on the understanding of the student and brevity of expression. This is like his statement in enumerating what cannot be a definition: 'It is not valid by the more general, the more specific, the coextensive as a definition, and the more hidden.' So he omitted the totally different (mubayin) even though it also cannot be a definition. He omitted it because if definition by the more general is not allowed, then the totally different is even more ineligible, due to its extreme remoteness from the defined. Al-Attar said: The truth is that the totally different and the more specific are excluded by the author's statement: 'The definer (mu'arif) of a thing is that which is said of it', i.e., that which is predicable of it. These two are not like that.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a section heading: 'Section: On the mu'arraf (the defined) and its divisions.' The commentator notes that the author omits mention of 'mubayin' (the totally different) in the list of things that cannot serve as definitions, even though it is also invalid. The reason is that if the more general (which is closer) cannot define, then the wholly different (which is far) is even more obviously invalid. The commentator then cites al-Attar's correction, which traces the exclusion back to the principle that a valid definition must be predicable of the defined (haml). The definer (mu'arif) is something that can be said of the defined. The mubayin (e.g., 'horse' for 'human') is not said of the thing at all, and the more specific (akhass) is only said of some individuals but not universally. Therefore, both are excluded. This underscores the foundational rule: the definiens must be applicable (mahmul) to the definiendum, i.e., it must be true of it in a universal affirmative proposition.

Key Terms
المعرف (al-mu'arraf: the defined)
المباين (al-mubayin: totally different)
الحمل (al-haml: predication)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is too complex
B) Because it is not predicable of the defined
C) Because it is more general
D) Because it is more specific

Key Takeaway
A valid definition requires that the definiens be universally predicable of the definiendum; the totally different (mubayin) fails this condition and is therefore invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Test the definition: 'Zakat is a charity given to the poor.' Is 'charity given to the poor' predicable of every instance of zakat? Is it too broad (a'amm) or too narrow? Check using the 'every definiendum is [definiens]' test.

Verbal Definition: Purpose and Epistemological Status
Commentators define verbal definition, give an example, and argue it is a type of conceptual inquiry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish verbal definition from real definition and explain the priority ordering of the four inquiries (matalib) in an Islamic epistemological context.

Core Idea
Verbal definition tells you which concept a word labels, not what that concept truly is.

This passage clarifies that verbal definition (al-ta'rīf al-lafẓī) simply connects an unfamiliar word to a known concept—it does not provide new essential knowledge. The passage also situates verbal definition within the broader framework of inquiries (matalib) that seek either conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) or assent (tasdiq), establishing a priority order that is crucial for any scholarly investigation in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens in the example
In the example 'ghadanfar' and 'asad', locate which is the unfamiliar word (definiendum) and which is the clearer word (definiens).
The definiens is 'al-asad' (the lion), a term already known to the questioner.
2
Extract the purpose of verbal definition
The passage states the purpose is to specify (ta'yin) what the word is placed for among all meanings. Note that the questioner already has the concept of lion—the answer merely connects the word to that concept.
Al-Dasuqi explains that no new mental image is produced.
3
Distinguish verbal from real definition
Contrast the example with a real definition (hadd) that would explain the essence of lion (e.g., genus and differentia). The passage notes that some scholars say verbal definition is not a definition at all.
Real definition presupposes knowledge of existence and seeks the quiddity.
4
Map the four inquiries and their priority
From al-Dawani's commentary, identify the two types of 'what' (explaining and real) and two types of 'whether' (simple and composite). Explain the necessary priority: explaining 'what' → simple 'whether' → real 'what' (with composite 'whether' optionally after real 'what').
The priority reflects epistemological dependence: you need to understand a term before asking if it exists, and then ask for its essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنَّما المراد تعيين ما وُضِعَ له اللَّفْظُ مِن سائر المعاني؛ ليُلتفت إليه، ويُعلم أنَّه موضوع بإزائه . الدسوقي وإذا قالَ السَّائلُ : ما الغَضَنْفَر ؟ ، فَقُلْتَ لهُ : الأسد ؛ فالمخاطب لم يجهل حقيقةً الأسد، بلْ مُتصوِّرٌ لَهَا كَغيرِهَا ، فَلَمْ تُفِدْهُ بِجوابِكَ حصول صورة الأسد في ذهنِهِ؛ لحصولها فيه قبل جوابِكَ له، وإنَّما أفدَّتَهُ أنَّ هذهِ الحقيقة دون غيرها من الحقائقِ الحاصلة عندك؛ مَوضوع بإزائِهَا لفظُ الغَضَنْفَر . وَلَمَّا كانَ مآلُ هذا التّصديقِ ، وَكَأَنَّكَ قُلْتَ لهُ : الغَضَنْفَرُ موضوع للأسد ؛ قالَ بعضُهم : التَّعريفُ اللفظي ليس تعريفاً أصلاً، فَضْلاً عن كونه ناقصاً . (قَوْلُهُ : إِنَّمَا الْمُرَادُ تَعْيِينُ مَا وُضِعَ لَهُ اللَّفْظُ أي : بلفظ أوضح منه؛ مُرادف له، أو أعم منه، أو أخص العطار . والمحقِّقُ الدَّوَانيُّ أَيَّدَ مَا ذهب إليه المصنِّفُ بأنَّهُ قد علَّلَ القومُ تقدُّمَ مَطلَبِ مَا الاسمية على جميع المطالب بأنَّهُ : ما لم يفهم معنى اللفظ؛ لم يمكِّن التصديق بوجودِهِ، فَلَا يتمشى طلب حقيقتِهِ وَلَا التَّصديقُ بالهيئة المركَّبةِ، فَإِنَّ ذلكَ الكلامَ إنَّما يتم إذا كانَ التَّعريفُ اللَّفظي داخلاً في مطلب «ما» كما لا يخفى ا.هـ. ويوضحه ما ذكره في الحواشي القديمة على الشارح الجديدِ للتَّجريدِ بأَنَّ لَنَا مَطلَبَيْنِ ؛ مَطلب «ما» ويُطلبُ بِهَا التَّصوُّرُ ، ومَطلب هل) ويُطلَبُ بِهَا التَّصديقُ والتَّصوُّرُ على قِسْمَيْنِ؛ تَصوُّرٌ بحسب الاسم وهو تصوُّرُ الشَّيء باعتبار مفهومه مع قطعِ النَّظرِ عن انطباقِهِ على طبيعة موجودة في الخارج، وهذا التَّصرُّرُ يجري في الموجودات قبل العلم بوجودِهَا، وفي المعدوماتِ أيضاً ، والطَّالبُ له «ما» الشَّارحة للاسم، وثانيهما : تصور بحسب الحقيقةِ ؛ أعني : تصرُّرَ الشَّيءِ الَّذي عُلِمَ وُجودُهُ، وَالطَّالبُ لهذا التَّصورِ مَا الحقيقيَّةُ، وكذلك التصديقُ ينقسم إلى التصديق بوجودِ الشَّيء في نفسه وإلى التصديق بثبوتِهِ لغيرِهِ والطَّالبُ لِلأَوَّلِ : «هَل» البسيطةُ، ولِلثَّاني: «هَل» المركَّبَةُ، ولا شُبهة في أنَّ مَطلَبَ مَا الشَّارحةِ مُقدَّم على هَل البسيطة، فإنَّ الشَّيءَ ما لم يتصوَّرُ مَفهومُهُ لم يمكِّن طلب التصديق بوجودِهِ، كَمَا أَنَّ مَطلَبٌ هَل» البسيطة فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ وحاصله : أن يُقصَدَ بِهِ تفسير صورة حاصلة من بين سائرِ الصُّورِ بأنَّها المرادَةُ بلفظة كذا . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : وَحَاصِلُهُ) أي : حاصل التعريف اللفظي . (قَوْلُهُ : مِنْ بَيْنِ مُتعلّق بتفسير؛ أي: تعيينها من بين سائرِ الصُّورِ بأنَّها ... إلخ؛ أي : بخلافِ التَّعريف الحقيقي، فإنَّما القصدُ بِهِ تَعيين وتفسير الماهية المجملة التي هي بمعنى اللّفظِ ، بدونِ الالتفات إلى تبيين مدلولِ اللَّفظِ من حيثُ كونُهُ مدلولاً . وإنْ كانَ حاصلاً التزاماً . (قَوْلُهُ: بِلَفْظَةِ كَذَا ) أي : من اللفظ الخفيّ كَالغَضَنْفَرِ والعُقار في الأمثلة السابقة. العطار مُقدَّم على مطلبِ مَا الحقيقيَّةِ ؛ إذْ مَا لم يعلم وجودُ الشَّيء؛ لم يكنْ أنْ يتصوَّرَ مِن حيثُ إِنَّهُ مَوجودٌ، وَلَا يكونُ التَّرتيبُ ضَروريًّا بينَ الهَليَّةِ المركبة والمائية الحقيقية، لكنَّ الأَوْلَى تقديم المائية .ا.هـ. وفي شرح سُلَّم العلوم: مطلب «ما» الشَّارحةِ مُتقدِّم على «ما» الحقيقية وجوباً؛ إذْ لَمَّا لم يصدّق بالوجوب ؛ كيف يطلب الحقيقة؟ وعلى المركَّبةِ استحساناً؛ بناءً على أنَّ لا كمالَ بعلم أحوال المعدوماتِ ومشكوك الوجود، وتقديمُ مَا الحقيقيَّةِ على هَل المركبة استحساني ؛ إِذِ الأَحَرَى مَعرفتُهُ للكنه أوَّلاً ، ثم العوارض، ثمَّ إِنَّ الأنسب لمجيبِ السَّائلِ بِـ «ما» الشَّارحة؛ الجواب بالحدّ ليستغني عن «مَا الحقيقيَّةِ ، كَمَا إذا سُئِلَ : مَا الزَّمان؟ فالجواب: الحش كم مُتَّصل غير قارٍ، لا أنَّه عدد الحركة، وللسائل بـ «لم»؛ الجوابُ بِاللّمِّيِّ لئلا يحتاج إلى سؤالِ اللّمّ بعدَهُ .ا.هـ. قال مير زاهد وتسمية إحدى الهليتين بالبسيطة والأُخرى بالمركَّبةِ؛ إنَّما هو بالنَّظر إلى ما صدقهما ؛ لا إلى مفهوم القضيَّةِ المقصودة، فإنَّ مصداق الهليَّةِ البسيطة هو نفس الموضوع من حيثُ يصح انتزاع وصفِ الوجودِ عنه، ومصداق الهلّيَّةِ المركَّبة هو الموضوعُ مَعَ شيءٍ آخر .ا.هـ. ومعنى كلام الدَّوَّاني ؛ أَنَّهُ علَّلَ القومُ ... إلخ ؛ أي: يدلّ على أنَّ الغرض منهُ التَّصويرُ ، تعليلُ القوم تقدُّمُ مَا » الاسميَّةِ ؛ أي : الشَّارحة لمعنى الاسم على بقيَّةِ المطالب، وهو داخل تحت مطلبِ (ما)) الاسميَّةِ، فيكونُ تَعريفاً لفظيًّا، إذ لو كان تعريفاً حقيقيًّا؛ لدخل تحت مطلبِ
The intention is only to specify what the word is placed for among all meanings, so that one attends to it and knows that it is placed in exchange for it. Al-Dasuqi: And if the questioner says: 'What is al-ghadanfar?' and you reply to him: 'al-asad (the lion)', then the addressee does not ignore the reality of the lion, but rather has a conception of it like other realities. So you have not benefited him by your answer with the occurrence of the image of the lion in his mind because it occurred in it before your answer. Rather, you have informed him that this reality, as distinct from other realities present with you, is the one in exchange for which the word 'ghadanfar' is placed. And since the outcome of this assent [is that] it is as if you said to him: 'ghadanfar is placed for the lion,' some said: 'Verbal definition is not a definition at all, let alone being deficient.' (His saying: 'The intention is only to specify what the word is placed for') i.e., with a clearer word, synonymous with it, or more general, or more specific. al-Attar. And the muhaqqiq al-Dawani supported what the author went for, that the people (logicians) justified the priority of the inquiry 'what' is name-based over all inquiries by saying: as long as the meaning of the word is not understood, assent to its existence is not possible, so the request for its reality or assent to the composite structure cannot proceed. For that statement is only complete if verbal definition is included in the inquiry of 'what,' as is not hidden. (End quote). And it is clarified by what he mentioned in the old glosses on the new commentary on al-Tajrid: that we have two inquiries: the inquiry 'what' by which conception is sought, and the inquiry 'whether' by which assent is sought. Conception is of two kinds: conception according to the name, which is the conception of the thing with regard to its concept irrespective of its applicability to an existing nature in the external world—this conception applies to existents before knowledge of their existence and also to non-existents; the seeker of this is 'what' explaining the name. The second is conception according to reality, i.e., the conception of the thing whose existence is known; the seeker of this conception is the real 'what.' Likewise, assent is divided into assent to the existence of the thing in itself and assent to its attribution to another. The seeker of the first is the simple 'whether,' and of the second is the composite 'whether.' There is no doubt that the inquiry of the explaining 'what' is prior to the simple 'whether,' because if the concept of a thing is not conceived, seeking assent to its existence is not possible. Just as the inquiry of the simple 'whether' is prior to the inquiry of the real 'what'—because as long as the existence of the thing is not known, it cannot be conceived insofar as it exists—and the ordering between the composite 'whether' and the real 'what' is not necessary, but it is preferable to give priority to the 'what' (end quote). And in the commentary on Sullam al-'Ulum: The inquiry of the explaining 'what' is necessarily prior to the real 'what'; for when existence is not assented to, how can one seek the reality? And [it is] preferably [prior] to the composite [whether], based on the fact that there is no perfection in knowing the states of non-existents and doubtfully existent things. And the priority of the real 'what' over the composite 'whether' is preferential, because it is more fitting to know the reality first, then the accidents. Further, the most appropriate for the respondent to the questioner with the explaining 'what' is to answer with the definition (hadd) to dispense with the real 'what', like if asked: 'What is time?' then the answer is: 'a flowing continuous quantity, not stationary,' not that it is the number of motion. And for the questioner with 'why' (lima), the answer should be with the 'because' to avoid needing the question of 'why' after it. (End quote). Mir Zahid said: The designation of one of the two 'whethers' as simple and the other as composite is in view of their referents, not the concept of the intended proposition. For the referent of the simple 'whether' is the subject itself insofar as the description of existence can be abstracted from it, and the referent of the composite 'whether' is the subject together with something else. (End quote). And the meaning of al-Dawani's statement: he justified the people's... etc., i.e., it indicates that the purpose of it is depiction. The justification of the people for the priority of the name-based 'what', i.e., the one explaining the meaning of the name over the rest of the inquiries, and it falls under the inquiry of the name-based 'what', so it is a verbal definition, because if it were a real definition, it would fall under the inquiry of
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage defines the nature and purpose of verbal definition (التعريف اللفظي) and distinguishes it from real definition (التعريف الحقيقي). It begins by stating that the aim of verbal definition is only to specify which meaning a word is placed for among all meanings, so that the listener knows that this word refers to that concept. The commentator al-Dasuqi then gives the classic example: if someone asks 'What is al-ghadanfar?' and you answer 'the lion,' the questioner already has the concept of a lion in his mind. Your answer does not give him a new mental image; rather it informs him that the word 'ghadanfar' is used to refer to that existing concept. This leads to the objection that verbal definition is not actually a definition at all, because it does not provide knowledge of the essence—it only connects a word to a known concept. The author then notes that some scholars say it is not a definition, let alone a deficient one. The marginal note by al-Attar clarifies that the clearer word used can be synonymous, more general, or more specific. Then al-Dawani's commentary is cited to support the author's view, arguing that the logicians' justification for placing the inquiry about the meaning of the name (مطلب ما الاسمية) before all other inquiries depends on verbal definition being included in that 'what' inquiry. Al-Dawani elaborates by distinguishing the two conceptions: conception according to the name (تصور بحسب الاسم) which is understanding the meaning of a word irrespective of whether the thing exists externally, and conception according to reality (تصور بحسب الحقيقة) which is of a thing whose existence is known. The first is sought by the explaining 'what' (ما الشارحة), the second by the real 'what' (ما الحقيقية). Similarly, assent is divided into simple assent to existence (هل البسيطة) and composite assent to attribution (هل المركبة). The passage then discusses the priority ordering among these inquiries: the explaining 'what' must precede the simple 'whether' (because you must understand what a word means before asking if it exists), and the simple 'whether' must precede the real 'what' (because you must know something exists before inquiring into its essence). The ordering between the real 'what' and the composite 'whether' is not necessary but preference is given to the real 'what'. A further quotation from Sharh Sullam al-'Ulum states that the explaining 'what' is necessarily prior to the real 'what' and preferably prior to the composite 'whether'. It also gives practical advice: the respondent to an explaining 'what' question should answer with a real definition (hadd) if possible, and the respondent to a 'why' question should answer with a causal explanation. Mir Zahid clarifies that the terms simple and composite 'whether' refer to the referents, not the propositional form. The passage concludes by reiterating al-Dawani's point: the priority of the name-based 'what' shows that its purpose is depiction (taswir), and because it falls under the name-based 'what', it is a verbal definition, distinct from real definition which falls under the real 'what'.

Key Terms

التعريف الحقيقي
مطلب ما
مطلب هل
ما الشارحة
ما الحقيقية
هل البسيطة
هل المركبة

A) A real definition of the essence of the lion.
B) A verbal definition that connects the word 'ghadanfar' to the concept of lion.
C) A demonstration that lions exist.
D) A logical proof that ghadanfar is a lion.

Key Takeaway
Verbal definition does not give new conceptual content; it only links a word to a known concept, a fundamental distinction for all Islamic reasoning.

Definition of Khabar and Resolving the Circularity
Author defines khabar and resolves circularity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the definition of khabar and how the potential circularity in its definition is resolved by distinguishing aspects.

Core Idea
A definitional circle is avoided when dependence is from different aspects.

This lesson unpacks the definition of khabar (statement) as the logical unit that can be true or false. The author addresses a subtle circularity: if khabar is defined by truth, but truth depends on khabar, a circle appears. He resolves it by distinguishing the aspect of initial definition from secondary recognition. Let's work through his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the definition of 'khabar' in the text. The author defines it as a statement containing a ratio (nisba) that is a report (hikaya) about reality.
The definition is not given in a single sentence but emerges from the discussion.
2
Locate the circularity objection
The text states: 'khabar in its presence secondarily depends on truth, and its occurrence initially depends on truth' – this seems circular. Identify exactly where the circle is alleged.
The circle is: khabar is defined by truth, but truth is defined by correspondence of khabar.
3
Find the resolution
The author says the circle is avoided because the dependence is not from the same aspect. Khabar in its secondary presence (as a known concept) depends on truth, but its initial occurrence (as a definition) does not depend on truth in the same way.
The concept of khabar can be brought to mind (ta'rif tanbihi) without requiring its truth.
4
Understand ta'rif tanbihi
Definition by indication aims to bring to mind something already known in the 'treasury' (of knowledge). It does not generate new knowledge, so the circle does not apply.
This type of definition is permissible even if it uses a property that depends on the thing defined.
5
Apply to a similar case in fiqh
Consider defining 'command' ('amr) as 'that which requires obedience'. If obedience is defined by response to command, a circle appears. The same resolution – distinguishing the aspect of definition from the aspect of application – avoids it.
This shows the tool's utility in legal theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالخبر (۱) في حضورِهِ مَوقوف على حصولِ الصِّدقِ، وفي حصوله ابتداء موقوف عليه لهُ ، فَكَونُهُ موقوفاً وموقوفاً عليه ليس من جهةٍ واحدةٍ ؛ فَلا دور ؛ لأنَّ الغرضَ مِنَ التّعريفِ التَّنبيهيّ : إحضارُ الشَّيء في المدركةِ بعد حصوله في الخزانة، ويجوز أن يحصل هذا الغرضُ مِن أمرٍ يتوقَّفُ في الحصول على ذلكَ الشَّيء، ومنها أنَّهُ لا يلزمُ مِن جريان الدورِ في الخبر جريانه في القضيَّة؛ إذ قد يكون هذا المفهوم ؛ معلوماً هُنَا ؛ من حيثُ كونه خبراً مجهولاً ، من حيثُ كونِهِ قضيَّةٌ، فَلَا يتوقَّفُ الصِّدقُ والكذب على القضيَّةِ المجهولة؛ بل على الخبر المعلوم، ومنها أنَّ الصدق هو مطابقةُ الأمر الذهني، ونظر فيه الجلالُ بأنَّ التَّصوراتِ مُطابقةٌ ولا توصفُ بالصَّدق أصلاً، وأجابَ مير زاهد بأنَّ المراد مطابقةُ النِّسبةِ الَّتِي هِيَ حكايةٌ عن الواقع، وهذه المطابقة غير المطابقة التي في التَّصوراتِ ، فإِنَّ مَرجِعَ المطابقة فيها ؛ الحملُ على ذي الصُّورة أو على المأخذ، ومرجع هذه المطابقة هو الوقوع في نفس الأمر ، قال : ويظهرُ مِن ذلكَ أنَّ المطابقة أوَّلاً وبالذَّاتِ للنّسبةِ، وثانياً وبالعرض للخبر المشتمل عليها .ا.هـ. والمراد باحتمال الصدقِ والكذب ؛ تجويز العقل لهما بالنَّظر إلى المفهوم مع قطعِ النَّظَرِ عَمَّا هو في الواقع، ومنشأ ذلك اشتماله على نسبةٍ هِيَ حكايةٌ عَن أمر واقع ، فَإِنَّ شأن الحكاية أن توصفَ بالمطابقة وعدمها ؛ بخلافِ النّسب الإنشائية ؛ فإنَّها ليست حكاية عن أمرٍ واقع، فلا يجري فيها الصِّدقُ والكذبُ ، فإن قلت : ما ذلك الأمر الواقع؟ قُلْنَا : هو في الحمليَّاتِ ؛ كون الموضوع في نفسه، بحيثُ يصح الحكم بأنَّه هو المحمول، وهذه الحيثيَّةُ حسب اختلاف الحمل ؛ مثلاً في حملِ الذَّاتِيَّاتِ : نفس حيثية ذات الموضوع، وفي حمل الوجودِ : حيثيَّة استناده إلى الجاعل ، وفي حمل الأوصاف العينية : قيام مبدأ المحمول بالموضوع، وفي حمل العدميَّاتِ : حيثيَّةُ نسبة عدمِ مُصاحبتِهِ لأمرٍ آخر، وفي حمل الإضافيات : حيثيَّةُ نسبة أمرٍ مُباين، وأما في الشرطيَّاتِ ؛ فهو كون المعنيين في نفسَيْهِمَا ؛ بحيثُ يصحُ الحُكم بثبوتِ أحدهما على تقدير ثبوتِ الآخر، أو كونهما في نفسهما بحيثُ يصح الحكم بالانفصال بينَهُما ، وهاتان الحيثيتان أيضاً يختلفان باختلافِ الاتصال والانفصال، قال مير زاهد : وبالجملة؛ الحكاية هي نفس مفهوم القضيَّة، والمحكي عنه هو مصداقها، والنّسبةُ إنَّما هي في الحكاية دون المحكي، والتَّغايرُ بينَهُما تغايرُ بالذَّاتِ لا بالاعتبار ا.هـ. (۱) (قولُ العطَّارِ : فالخبرُ ... إلخ) العبارة محرَّفةٌ ، ولعلَّ صحتها : فالخبرُ في حضوره ثانياً موقوف على حصولِ الصِّدقِ له، وحصوله ابتداءً موقوفٌ عليه (أي : بِكسبِهِ) وبذلك تفهم العبارة بسهولة كما يرشد إليه تفريعه وتعليله ا.هـ. الشَّرنوبي.
The statement (khabar) in its presence secondarily depends on the occurrence of truth for it, and its occurrence initially depends on it, so its being both dependent and depended upon is not from one aspect; thus there is no circle. Because the purpose of the definition by indication (ta'rif tanbihi) is to bring the thing to mind after its occurrence in the treasury, and it is possible that this purpose be achieved from a thing that depends for its occurrence on that thing. And among (the responses) is that the occurrence of a circle in the statement does not necessitate its occurrence in the proposition (qadiyya); for this concept may be known here insofar as it is a statement, unknown insofar as it is a proposition; thus truth and falsity do not depend on the unknown proposition, but on the known statement. And among (the responses) is that truth is the correspondence (mutabaqa) of the mental affair, and al-Jalal objected to this because concepts (tasawwurat) are also correspondences but are not described as truthful at all. Mir Zahid answered that what is meant is the correspondence of the ratio (nisba) which is a report (hikaya) about reality, and this correspondence is not the correspondence found in concepts; for the basis of correspondence in concepts is predication (haml) upon the possessor of the form or upon the source, while the basis of this correspondence is the occurrence in actual fact. He said: From this it appears that correspondence primarily and essentially belongs to the ratio, and secondarily and accidentally to the statement that contains it. End quote. And what is meant by the possibility of truth and falsity is the intellect's deeming both possible with regard to the concept, disregarding what is in reality. The source of this is its inclusion of a ratio that is a report about an actual matter; for the nature of a report is to be described by correspondence or lack thereof, unlike performative (insha'iyya) ratios, for they are not reports about an actual matter, so truth and falsity do not apply to them. If you ask: What is that actual matter? We say: In categorical (hamli) propositions, it is the subject's being in itself such that it is correct to judge that it is the predicate. This aspect (haythiyya) varies according to the type of predication; for example, in the predication of essential attributes, it is the very aspect of the subject's essence; in the predication of existence, it is the aspect of its dependence on the maker; in the predication of real attributes, it is the subsistence of the predicate's basis in the subject; in the predication of privations, it is the aspect of the relation of non-existence of its accompaniment with another thing; in the predication of relatives, it is the aspect of the relation of a distinct thing. As for conditional (shartiyya) propositions, it is the two meanings being in themselves such that it is correct to judge the establishment of one given the establishment of the other, or their being in themselves such that it is correct to judge the separation between them. These two aspects also vary according to conjunction (ittisal) and disjunction (infisal). Mir Zahid said: In summary, the hikaya is the very concept of the proposition, and the thing reported (mahki 'anhu) is its referent (musadaq), and the ratio is only in the hikaya, not in the mahki, and the distinction between them is a distinction in essence, not by consideration. End quote.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definitional and epistemological expansion of the nature of khabar (statement) and the conditions for truth and falsity. The author begins by addressing a potential circularity: if a statement's truth depends on its correspondence to reality, but we only know that correspondence through the statement itself, a circle seems to arise. He resolves this by distinguishing two aspects: the statement as a known concept (tasawwur) and as a judgment (tasdiq). The definitional purpose (ta'rif tanbihi) is to bring the known concept to mind, which can be achieved by something that itself depends on that concept. Thus, the circle is avoided because the dependence is not from the same aspect. Next, the author clarifies that truth is correspondence of a mental affair (amr dhihni) with reality. An objection by al-Jalal (likely al-Jalal al-Dawwani) is raised: concepts (tasawwurat) also involve correspondence (e.g., a mental image corresponds to its object), yet they are not called true or false. Mir Zahid answers that the correspondence relevant to statements is specifically the correspondence of a ratio (nisba) that is a report (hikaya) about reality. Concepts involve a different kind of correspondence (via predication or representation). The ratio is the primary bearer of truth/falsity; the statement (khabar) that contains it is true or false derivatively. The author then explains the meaning of 'possibility of truth and falsity' (ihtimal al-sidq wa al-kidhb): it refers to the intellect's ability to consider both possibilities for the concept of the proposition, without reference to actual reality. This possibility arises because the proposition includes a ratio that is a report about an actual matter. Performative (insha'iyya) ratios are not reports, so they cannot be true or false. For categorical (hamli) propositions, the actual matter (amr waqi') is the subject being such that it is correct to predicate the predicate of it. This 'aspect' (haythiyya) varies with the type of predication: essential, existential, attributive, privative, and relative. For conditional propositions, the actual matter is the relationship of conjunction or disjunction between the two clauses. Mir Zahid concludes by distinguishing the hikaya (the report, i.e., the concept of the proposition) from the mahki 'anhu (the reality reported about). The ratio exists only in the hikaya, not in the external reality. This distinction prevents confusion and ensures that truth is a property of the propositional content, not of external things. The footnote by al-Sharnubi corrects a corruption in the main text, clarifying that the statement in its secondary presence depends on truth, while its initial occurrence depends on earning (kash) that truth.

صدق
نسبة

Key Takeaway
The circularity in defining khabar is resolved because its secondary presence depends on truth, but its initial occurrence does not; the dependence is from different aspects.

What Is Mantiq?
