Truth as Correspondence of the Ratio (Nisba)
Author defines truth as correspondence of the ratio, not concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish truth in propositions (sidq) from correspondence in concepts (tasawwur).

Core Idea
Truth applies only to reports, not to simple concepts.

The author clarifies that truth is correspondence (mutabaqa) of a mental affair with reality. But an objection by al-Jalal points out that concepts also involve correspondence, yet they are not called true or false. Mir Zahid's answer shows that the correspondence in statements is specifically of the ratio (nisba) that reports on reality. This distinction is crucial for understanding the scope of truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify what is being defined
The author defines truth (sidq) as correspondence of the mental affair (amr dhihni) with reality. Locate this definition in the text.
This is the classical correspondence theory of truth.
2
Locate al-Jalal's objection
Al-Jalal objects that concepts (tasawwurat) also involve correspondence (e.g., a mental image of a horse corresponds to a real horse), yet concepts are not described as true or false. Find this objection in the text.
This objection challenges the universality of the correspondence definition.
3
Analyze Mir Zahid's answer
Mir Zahid responds that the correspondence in concepts is based on predication (haml) or representation, while the correspondence in statements is based on the ratio being a report (hikaya) about actual reality. The basis of correspondence is different.
Concepts correspond via predication (e.g., 'this is a horse'), while ratios correspond by reporting a state of affairs.
4
Understand the implication
The ratio (nisba) is the primary bearer of truth; the statement (khabar) containing it is true secondarily. This means that truth primarily belongs to the propositional content, not to the linguistic expression.
This is a key insight for understanding propositional truth in logic.
5
Apply to a kalam example
Consider the statement 'The world is originated'. Its truth lies in the ratio that the world has origination matching reality. The concept 'world' alone does not admit truth or falsity.
This distinction is used in kalam to argue about the status of divine attributes.

العينية : قيام مبدأ المحمول بالموضوع، وفي حمل العدميَّاتِ : حيثيَّةُ نسبة عدمِ مُصاحبتِهِ لأمرٍ آخر، وفي حمل الإضافيات : حيثيَّةُ نسبة أمرٍ مُباين، وأما في الشرطيَّاتِ ؛ فهو كون المعنيين في نفسَيْهِمَا ؛ بحيثُ يصحُ الحُكم بثبوتِ أحدهما على تقدير ثبوتِ الآخر، أو كونهما في نفسهما بحيثُ يصح الحكم بالانفصال بينَهُما ، وهاتان الحيثيتان أيضاً يختلفان باختلافِ الاتصال والانفصال، قال مير زاهد : وبالجملة؛ الحكاية هي نفس مفهوم القضيَّة، والمحكي عنه هو مصداقها، والنّسبةُ إنَّما هي في الحكاية دون المحكي، والتَّغايرُ بينَهُما تغايرُ بالذَّاتِ لا بالاعتبار ا.هـ. (۱) (قولُ العطَّارِ : فالخبرُ ... إلخ) العبارة محرَّفةٌ ، ولعلَّ صحتها : فالخبرُ في حضوره ثانياً موقوف على حصولِ الصِّدقِ له، وحصوله ابتداءً موقوفٌ عليه (أي : بِكسبِهِ) وبذلك تفهم العبارة بسهولة كما يرشد إليه تفريعه وتعليله ا.هـ. الشَّرنوبي.
The statement (khabar) in its presence secondarily depends on the occurrence of truth for it, and its occurrence initially depends on it, so its being both dependent and depended upon is not from one aspect; thus there is no circle. Because the purpose of the definition by indication (ta'rif tanbihi) is to bring the thing to mind after its occurrence in the treasury, and it is possible that this purpose be achieved from a thing that depends for its occurrence on that thing. And among (the responses) is that the occurrence of a circle in the statement does not necessitate its occurrence in the proposition (qadiyya); for this concept may be known here insofar as it is a statement, unknown insofar as it is a proposition; thus truth and falsity do not depend on the unknown proposition, but on the known statement. And among (the responses) is that truth is the correspondence (mutabaqa) of the mental affair, and al-Jalal objected to this because concepts (tasawwurat) are also correspondences but are not described as truthful at all. Mir Zahid answered that what is meant is the correspondence of the ratio (nisba) which is a report (hikaya) about reality, and this correspondence is not the correspondence found in concepts; for the basis of correspondence in concepts is predication (haml) upon the possessor of the form or upon the source, while the basis of this correspondence is the occurrence in actual fact. He said: From this it appears that correspondence primarily and essentially belongs to the ratio, and secondarily and accidentally to the statement that contains it. End quote. And what is meant by the possibility of truth and falsity is the intellect's deeming both possible with regard to the concept, disregarding what is in reality. The source of this is its inclusion of a ratio that is a report about an actual matter; for the nature of a report is to be described by correspondence or lack thereof, unlike performative (insha'iyya) ratios, for they are not reports about an actual matter, so truth and falsity do not apply to them. If you ask: What is that actual matter? We say: In categorical (hamli) propositions, it is the subject's being in itself such that it is correct to judge that it is the predicate. This aspect (haythiyya) varies according to the type of predication; for example, in the predication of essential attributes, it is the very aspect of the subject's essence; in the predication of existence, it is the aspect of its dependence on the maker; in the predication of real attributes, it is the subsistence of the predicate's basis in the subject; in the predication of privations, it is the aspect of the relation of non-existence of its accompaniment with another thing; in the predication of relatives, it is the aspect of the relation of a distinct thing. As for conditional (shartiyya) propositions, it is the two meanings being in themselves such that it is correct to judge the establishment of one given the establishment of the other, or their being in themselves such that it is correct to judge the separation between them. These two aspects also vary according to conjunction (ittisal) and disjunction (infisal). Mir Zahid said: In summary, the hikaya is the very concept of the proposition, and the thing reported (mahki 'anhu) is its referent (musadaq), and the ratio is only in the hikaya, not in the mahki, and the distinction between them is a distinction in essence, not by consideration. End quote.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definitional and epistemological expansion of the nature of khabar (statement) and the conditions for truth and falsity. The author begins by addressing a potential circularity: if a statement's truth depends on its correspondence to reality, but we only know that correspondence through the statement itself, a circle seems to arise. He resolves this by distinguishing two aspects: the statement as a known concept (tasawwur) and as a judgment (tasdiq). The definitional purpose (ta'rif tanbihi) is to bring the known concept to mind, which can be achieved by something that itself depends on that concept. Thus, the circle is avoided because the dependence is not from the same aspect. Next, the author clarifies that truth is correspondence of a mental affair (amr dhihni) with reality. An objection by al-Jalal (likely al-Jalal al-Dawwani) is raised: concepts (tasawwurat) also involve correspondence (e.g., a mental image corresponds to its object), yet they are not called true or false. Mir Zahid answers that the correspondence relevant to statements is specifically the correspondence of a ratio (nisba) that is a report (hikaya) about reality. Concepts involve a different kind of correspondence (via predication or representation). The ratio is the primary bearer of truth/falsity; the statement (khabar) that contains it is true or false derivatively. The author then explains the meaning of 'possibility of truth and falsity' (ihtimal al-sidq wa al-kidhb): it refers to the intellect's ability to consider both possibilities for the concept of the proposition, without reference to actual reality. This possibility arises because the proposition includes a ratio that is a report about an actual matter. Performative (insha'iyya) ratios are not reports, so they cannot be true or false. For categorical (hamli) propositions, the actual matter (amr waqi') is the subject being such that it is correct to predicate the predicate of it. This 'aspect' (haythiyya) varies with the type of predication: essential, existential, attributive, privative, and relative. For conditional propositions, the actual matter is the relationship of conjunction or disjunction between the two clauses. Mir Zahid concludes by distinguishing the hikaya (the report, i.e., the concept of the proposition) from the mahki 'anhu (the reality reported about). The ratio exists only in the hikaya, not in the external reality. This distinction prevents confusion and ensures that truth is a property of the propositional content, not of external things. The footnote by al-Sharnubi corrects a corruption in the main text, clarifying that the statement in its secondary presence depends on truth, while its initial occurrence depends on earning (kash) that truth.

نسبة
حكاية
مطابقة
قضية

A) The concept (tasawwur)
B) The ratio (nisba) that is a report
C) The external object
D) The word (lafz)

Key Takeaway
Truth primarily belongs to the ratio (nisba) that is a report about reality, and only secondarily to the proposition containing it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the statement 'The Quran is the word of Allah.' Identify the ratio that is a report and explain why it can be true or false, whereas the concept 'Quran' alone cannot.
?
Find a performative (insha'iyya) statement in Arabic (e.g., 'I divorce you') and explain why it cannot be true or false according to the author's criteria.

What Is Mantiq?

5 min
The Varying Aspect (Haythiyya) of the Actual Matter in Propositions
Author details the varying aspect (haythiyya) for different predications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the appropriate haythiyya (aspect) for different types of predication in categorical and conditional propositions.

Core Idea
The truth condition varies with the type of predication.

The author explains that the 'actual matter' (amr waqi') that makes a proposition true differs depending on the type of predication (haml). For essential predicates, it's the subject's essence; for existence, it's dependence on the agent; for real attributes, it's the basis of the attribute; for privations, it's a relation of absence; for relatives, it's a relation of distinct things. Similarly for conditionals. This taxonomy helps us precisely determine when a proposition is true.

Guided Path
1
List the five types of predication
From the text, identify the five types of haml (categorical predication): essential (dhatiyyat), existence (wujud), real attributes (awsaf 'ayniyya), privations ('adamiyyat), and relatives (idafiyyat).
These correspond to different ways a predicate can relate to a subject.
2
Identify the haythiyya for each type
For each type, the text specifies the aspect (haythiyya) that constitutes the truth condition. For essential: the subject's essence itself. For existence: dependence on the maker. For real attributes: subsistence of the predicate's basis in the subject. For privations: relation of non-existence of accompaniment. For relatives: relation of a distinct thing.
The haythiyya is the precise respect in which the subject must be for the proposition to be true.
3
Understand the haythiyya for conditionals
For conditional propositions, the truth condition is the relation between the two clauses: for conjunctive (mutasila), it is that one follows from the other; for disjunctive (munfasila), it is that they are separate. These also vary.
The text mentions two types: ittisal (conjunction) and infisal (disjunction).
4
Apply to a categorical example
Consider the proposition 'Zayd is seeing.' This is a real attribute (basar is an attribute). The truth condition is that the basis of sight (basar) subsists in Zayd.
This is an example from the category of real attributes.
5
Apply to a conditional example
Consider: 'If the sun rises, then day comes.' The truth condition is that the two events are such that day follows sunrise necessarily.
This is a conjunctive conditional (ittisal).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالخبر (۱) في حضورِهِ مَوقوف على حصولِ الصِّدقِ، وفي حصوله ابتداء موقوف عليه لهُ ، فَكَونُهُ موقوفاً وموقوفاً عليه ليس من جهةٍ واحدةٍ ؛ فَلا دور ؛ لأنَّ الغرضَ مِنَ التّعريفِ التَّنبيهيّ : إحضارُ الشَّيء في المدركةِ بعد حصوله في الخزانة، ويجوز أن يحصل هذا الغرضُ مِن أمرٍ يتوقَّفُ في الحصول على ذلكَ الشَّيء، ومنها أنَّهُ لا يلزمُ مِن جريان الدورِ في الخبر جريانه في القضيَّة؛ إذ قد يكون هذا المفهوم ؛ معلوماً هُنَا ؛ من حيثُ كونه خبراً مجهولاً ، من حيثُ كونِهِ قضيَّةٌ، فَلَا يتوقَّفُ الصِّدقُ والكذب على القضيَّةِ المجهولة؛ بل على الخبر المعلوم، ومنها أنَّ الصدق هو مطابقةُ الأمر الذهني، ونظر فيه الجلالُ بأنَّ التَّصوراتِ مُطابقةٌ ولا توصفُ بالصَّدق أصلاً، وأجابَ مير زاهد بأنَّ المراد مطابقةُ النِّسبةِ الَّتِي هِيَ حكايةٌ عن الواقع، وهذه المطابقة غير المطابقة التي في التَّصوراتِ ، فإِنَّ مَرجِعَ المطابقة فيها ؛ الحملُ على ذي الصُّورة أو على المأخذ، ومرجع هذه المطابقة هو الوقوع في نفس الأمر ، قال : ويظهرُ مِن ذلكَ أنَّ المطابقة أوَّلاً وبالذَّاتِ للنّسبةِ، وثانياً وبالعرض للخبر المشتمل عليها .ا.هـ. والمراد باحتمال الصدقِ والكذب ؛ تجويز العقل لهما بالنَّظر إلى المفهوم مع قطعِ النَّظَرِ عَمَّا هو في الواقع، ومنشأ ذلك اشتماله على نسبةٍ هِيَ حكايةٌ عَن أمر واقع ، فَإِنَّ شأن الحكاية أن توصفَ بالمطابقة وعدمها ؛ بخلافِ النّسب الإنشائية ؛ فإنَّها ليست حكاية عن أمرٍ واقع، فلا يجري فيها الصِّدقُ والكذبُ ، فإن قلت : ما ذلك الأمر الواقع؟ قُلْنَا : هو في الحمليَّاتِ ؛ كون الموضوع في نفسه، بحيثُ يصح الحكم بأنَّه هو المحمول، وهذه الحيثيَّةُ حسب اختلاف الحمل ؛ مثلاً في حملِ الذَّاتِيَّاتِ : نفس حيثية ذات الموضوع، وفي حمل الوجودِ : حيثيَّة استناده إلى الجاعل ، وفي حمل الأوصاف What Is Mantiq?
beginner

Defining al-Qawl: Composite Utterance in Logic
Begins the chapter on definition and defines al-qawl as composite speech, with commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the primary and secondary senses of qawl and explain why it is not univocal.

Core Idea
Qawl is composite speech, not any word — its parts reveal meaning piece by piece.

In this lesson, we open the chapter on definition (al-ma'rif). The author clarifies the foundational term qawl, which is the building block of all logical propositions. The commentary shows that qawl is not a simple univocal term but has two senses: uttered composite and intelligible composite. Understanding this precision is crucial before moving to definitions proper.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find where the author says 'al-qawl — it is the composite utterance' (al-lafz al-murakkab). This is the core definition.
2
Identify the two senses
The commentary mentions qawl is used for uttered (malafuth) and intelligible (ma'qul). Recognize that these are two distinct applications.
The author's phrasing indicates qawl is not univocal.
3
Understand the logical implication of non-univocity
If qawl were univocal (mushtarak ma'nawi), it would directly include both senses, but the definition prioritizes the uttered sense. The commentary notes that otherwise they would say 'it comprises the uttered and the intelligible.'
4
Test the composite condition
A composite utterance is defined as 'that whose part indicates a part of its meaning'. Apply this to an example like 'Zayd qa'im' (Zayd is standing): 'Zayd' indicates the subject, 'qa'im' indicates the predicate.
This condition distinguishes composite from simple utterances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ فالقول ـ وهو اللَّفْظُ المركَّبُ (قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ اللَّفْظُ الْمُرَكَّبُ) بأن تلفّظت يزيد قائم مثلاً (قَوْلُهُ : فَالْقَوْلُ هُوَ اللَّفْظُ الْمُرَكَّبُ هذه العبارةُ كَقَولِهم : القولُ يُطلقُ تارةً على الملفوظ وتارة على المعقول ; مُشعرةٌ بأنَّهُ ليسَ مُشتركا معنويا، وإلا ، لقالُوا : وهو يعمُ الملفوظ والمعقول، قال عبد الحكيم : القولُ يرادف المركَّبَ، والمركَّبُ صفةُ اللَّفظِ ; لأنَّهُ ما دلَّ جزؤُهُ على جزء معناه والمعنى إنَّما يوصَفُ بِهِ بالعرض بناءً على ما نصَّ
Section: On the definition and its divisions. So al-qawl — it is the composite utterance. (His statement: 'and it is the composite utterance') that you utter like 'Zayd is standing' for example. (His statement: 'so al-qawl is the composite utterance') this expression is like their statement: 'al-qawl is used sometimes for the uttered and sometimes for the intelligible,' indicating that it is not univocal; otherwise they would say: 'it comprises the uttered and the intelligible.' Abd al-Hakim said: al-qawl is synonymous with composite, and composite is an attribute of utterance because it is that whose part indicates a part of its meaning, and meaning is described by it only accidentally, based on what he stated.
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter on definition (ma'rif) and its divisions. The author defines al-qawl as 'the composite utterance' (al-lafz al-murakkab). The commentary explains that al-qawl is not a univocal term (mushtarak ma'nawi) but is used in two senses: for uttered composite (lafz) and for intelligible composite (ma'qul). If it were univocal, it would be said to include both directly. Instead, it primarily applies to the uttered composite, like saying 'Zayd is standing.' Abd al-Hakim further clarifies that qawl is synonymous with 'composite' (murakkab), and composite is an attribute of utterance (lafz) because a composite utterance is one whose part indicates a part of its meaning. Meaning is described as composite only accidentally. This establishes the basic definition in logic: qawl is the building block of propositions. For example, in fiqh, 'Zakat is obligatory' is a qawl because it is a composite utterance with parts indicating separate meanings.

Key Terms
قول
لفظ مركب
معقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is qawl NOT a univocal (mushtarak ma'nawi) term?
A) Because it only refers to uttered speech.
B) Because if it were univocal, it would directly include both uttered and intelligible, but it doesn't.
C) Because it is synonymous with composite.
D) Because it has parts that indicate meaning.

Key Takeaway
Qawl in logic is specifically the composite utterance, not any word; its primary sense is uttered, and its intelligible sense is secondary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Definition of Qadhiyya and Its Metaphorical Extension to Mushkuk
Author defines khabar and qadhiyya, arguing mushkuk is metaphorically khabar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the definition of qadhiyya to classify any statement, and distinguish between real and metaphorical usage of khabar for doubtful statements.

Core Idea
A proposition is defined by its form, not the speaker's certainty.

In Islamic logic, the proposition (qadhiyya) is the building block of all argumentation. But what qualifies as a proposition? This lesson unpacks the definition from the Tadhheeb, showing how it includes even certain and impossible statements, and explains why doubtful statements (mushkuk) are only metaphorically called khabar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of qadhiyya
Find the phrase 'هي القول المحتمل للصدق والكذب من حيث ذاته' in the Arabic. Note the key qualifier 'من حيث ذاته' (in respect of its essence), which separates the statement itself from the speaker's knowledge.
This definition is found in the passage after 'وأما القضية'.
2
Test the definition with examples
The author gives four examples: speech of Allah (certainly true), speech of Musaylima (certainly false), your saying 'the sky is above us' (true), and 'the earth is above us' (false). Apply the definition: each admits truth and falsehood in itself, so all are qadhiyya.
This shows that actual truth value does not affect propositional status.
3
Understand the problem with mushkuk
The doubter's statement (e.g., 'maybe it will rain') is not certain from the speaker's perspective, but the definition disregards the speaker. Thus, it has the form of a proposition. The author calls it khabar metaphorically because it resembles khabar in form or contains most of its parts.
The commentator al-Dasuqi clarifies that the definition is about the statement itself.
4
Distinguish the two types of metaphorical extension
Al-'Attar explains two possibilities: (1) simile (isti'ara) due to formal resemblance, and (2) metonymy (majaz mursal) because mushkuk contains most parts of khabar (subject, predicate, relation) but lacks the judgment. The first is a declared simile, the second a part-whole relation.
This distinction is crucial for understanding how terms extend from real to metaphorical usage.

وحينئذ؛ يدخل في التعريف الكلام المقطوع بصدقه بالنظر لقائله، أو لمطابقته للواقع جزماً؛ نحو: كلام الله، وكلام رسله، وقولك السماء فوقنا، والأرض تحتنا، فإن هذا محتمل للصدق والكذب من حيث ذاته، وإن كان غير محتمل للكذب بل مقطوع بصدقه بالنظر لقائله، أو مطابقته للواقع.

Chapter: On the Known and Its Divisions. Know that applying the term 'khabar' to the doubtful is not in a real sense; because khabar is what admits truth and falsehood, and the doubtful is not so, but rather metaphorically. Al-Dasuqi: In a sense of certainty, or belief, or compound ignorance, or tradition; then the doubter[?] has no judgment, and his khabar is devoid of judgment in this sense. As for the proposition (qadhiyya), it is the statement that admits truth and falsehood in respect of its essence; i.e., disregarding its speaker. So the restriction of 'in respect of' is observed in the author's definition. Then the definition includes speech that is certain in truth with respect to its speaker, or because it matches reality decisively, such as: the speech of Allah, the speech of His messengers, and your saying 'the sky is above us, and the earth is below us,' for this admits truth and falsehood in respect of its essence, even though it does not admit falsehood but is certain in truth with respect to its speaker, or its matching reality. And it also includes speech that is certain in falsehood with respect to its speaker or because it does not match reality, such as the saying of Musaylima and your saying 'the earth is above us, and the sky is below us,' for it admits truth and falsehood in respect of its essence, even though it does not admit truth, but certainty of its falsehood with respect to its speaker, due to its opposition to reality. So all of this is called khabar and qadhiyya. Likewise, the khabar of the doubter enters, for it admits truth and falsehood in respect of its essence, not with respect to its speaker. So the commentator's statement that the doubtful proposition is devoid of judgment is not accepted; because he does not consider the speaker himself, and he considers his speech in itself, and there is no doubt that his speech includes a relation that admits truth and falsehood. Do you not see that they included the liar's speech and disregarded the speaker? So reflect. (His saying: 'And know that applying...' etc.) This is based on his earlier point that the doubtful is excluded from the definition. (His saying: 'is not in a real sense') The ba' is extra in the predicate of 'laysa'. Al-'Attar: (His saying: 'And know') is the beginning of speech and commentaries in explaining the application of khabar to the doubtful, and whoever said that it is an answer to an implied question arising from the answer to the question—the estimation being: since the doubtful is devoid of judgment, how is it called khabar? So he answered with his saying 'And know...' etc.—he has erred. Either by considering that its form is the form of khabar, or by considering that it contains most parts of khabar. [Divisions of the proposition] Then the proposition is either categorical or conditional, as he said. Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: 'Either by considering that its form...' etc.) So it is a metaphorical usage by way of simile, and the relation is similarity in form. The summary: we likened the doubtful khabars to those that contain judgment, with the commonality of similarity in form, and the name of the object of simile was borrowed for the simile, a declared simile. (His saying: 'Or by considering...' etc.) i.e., it is a metonymy, and the relation is whole-part; i.e., we used khabar, which is the name of the whole, and intended the part, which is the doubtful, by metonymy. (His saying: 'its containing') i.e., the doubtful. (His saying: 'most parts of khabar') which are the subject of judgment, the predicate, and the verbal relation. This is based on the fact that khabar contains the subject of judgment, the predicate, the judgmental relation, and the judgment. Al-'Attar: (His saying: 'Either by considering that its form is the form of khabar' so it is a declared simile of the type of applying the name of a thing to what resembles it in form, so the relation is formal similarity, not as it was said: it is a metonymy; for that is an error. (His saying: 'Or by considering that it contains most parts of khabar') which are other than the judgment: the predicate, the subject, and the relation.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and expansionary commentary on the concepts of khabar and qadhiyya, specifically addressing whether doubtful statements (mushkuk) qualify as khabar. The author begins by stating that applying the term 'khabar' to doubtful statements is not real (haqiqa) but metaphorical (majaz), because real khabar is defined as that which is capable of truth and falsehood, whereas a doubtful statement is not considered capable of both from the speaker's perspective—it is merely uncertain. However, the commentary by al-Dasuqi clarifies that the definition of qadhiyya (proposition) focuses on the statement itself (bi-haythu dhatihi), disregarding the speaker. Thus, even certain statements like the speech of Allah or obviously false statements like those of Musaylima are included, because in themselves they are capable of truth and falsehood. The author then argues that even the statement of a doubter (shakk) should be included, because it has the form of khabar and contains a relation capable of truth and falsehood. This is supported by the example of a liar's statement, which is considered a khabar despite its falsehood. The passage then discusses the metaphorical application of 'khabar' to doubtful statements in two ways: either by analogy (simile) because the form resembles real khabar, or by metonymy because it contains most parts of khabar (subject, predicate, relation) even if lacking the judgment. Al-'Attar corrects a previous commentator who considered both as metonymy, specifying that the first is a declared simile. This passage establishes the foundational definition of qadhiyya that will be used throughout logic.

خبر
قضية
مشكوك
مجاز

Key Takeaway
A proposition is defined by its capacity for truth and falsehood in itself, not by the speaker's certainty, and doubtful statements are included metaphorically due to formal similarity or containing most parts of a proposition.

Defining the Categorical Proposition
Defines the categorical proposition and illustrates with a negative example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given proposition is categorical (hamliyya) by checking if it affirms or denies a predicate of a subject.

Core Idea
The categorical proposition affirms or denies a predicate of a subject.

In Islamic logic, every reasoning starts with propositions. The most fundamental type is the categorical proposition (al-qadiyya al-ḥamliyya), which simply says that something is or is not the case. This lesson will help you recognise it and distinguish it from conditional propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the judgment
Look for a hukm (judgment) that either affirms (thubūt) or negates (nafy) a predicate for a subject. The passage states: 'If the judgment is the affirmation of something for something or the negation of something from it, then it is categorical.'
2
Distinguish from conditional
The categorical proposition is unconditional. If the judgment depends on a condition (e.g., 'if ... then ...'), it is not categorical. The passage implies this by not mentioning any condition.
Conditional propositions are covered later.
3
Apply the definition
Test a given proposition: Does it judge that something is (affirmative) or is not (negative) of something? The example provided is 'La shay'a min al-insani bihajar' (Nothing of man is a stone), which negates 'being a stone' for 'man'.
This is a negative categorical proposition.
4
Check completeness
Ensure the definition covers both affirmation and negation. The passage explicitly includes both, so it is complete as a definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْمَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ. إِنْ كَانَ الْحُكْمُ بِثُبُوتِ شَيْءٍ لِشَيْءٍ أَوْ بِنَفْيِ شَيْءٍ عَنْهُ فَحَمْلِيَّةٌ. كَقَوْلِنَا: لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ.
A chapter on the definition and its divisions. If the judgment is the affirmation of something for something or the negation of something from it, then it is categorical. Like our saying: Nothing of man is a stone.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage defines the categorical proposition. It states that a proposition is categorical if it judges either that something is affirmed of something else (positive) or that something is negated of something else (negative). The example given is 'No man is a stone', which is a negative categorical proposition. The definition establishes the genus (judgment) and the differentia (affirmation/negation of something for something).

Key Terms

Key Takeaway
A categorical proposition is defined by its judgment of affirmation or negation of a predicate for a subject, without condition.

What Is Mantiq?

The Subject of a Categorical Proposition
Defining the subject and its role in the categorical proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject in any categorical proposition and explain the three necessary parts of a categorical proposition according to classical logic.

Core Idea
The subject is what the judgment is about; a categorical proposition needs subject, predicate, and relation.

This lesson introduces the first component of a categorical proposition: the subject (al-mawḍūʿ). You will learn how logicians define the subject, why they call the proposition 'ḥamliyya' (predicative) rather than 'waḍ‘iyya', and how later scholars debated whether the copula should be a separate part. We will use examples from Islamic sciences to ground these concepts.

Guided Path
1
Define a categorical proposition
Recall that a categorical proposition (qaḍiyya ḥamliyya) affirms or denies something about something. It must have a subject, a predicate, and a relation (the judgment).
2
Identify the subject in examples
In 'Zayd is standing', the subject is Zayd. Note that the subject may appear later in the sentence, as in verbal sentences where the agent (fā‘il) is the logical subject.
Al-Sayyid al-Jurjani explains that 'Zayd said' means 'Zayd is a speaker' – thus Zayd is the subject.
3
Understand why it is called ḥamliyya
Al-Dasuqi argues that predication (ḥaml) is nobler than the subject (waḍ‘) because the benefit of the statement depends on the predicate. Hence the proposition is named after predication.
4
Contrast ancient and later views on the number of parts
Ancient logicians said three parts (subject, predicate, judgment). Later scholars added a fourth – the copula – because they saw the relation as a distinct object of perception. The passage notes that al-Dawwani reconciles this by distinguishing assenting and non-assenting perception.
This nuance affects how we analyze propositions in advanced logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعْرِفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ - (وَ) إِمَّا (سَالِبَةٌ) إِن حُكم فيها بالنفي المذكور . الحملية لا بُدَّ لها من ثلاثة أمورٍ : ١. [الموضوع]: الأوَّل: المحكومُ عليه وَيُسَمَّى الْمَحكُومُ عَليه مَوْضُوعاً)؛ الدسوقي : وقد يُجابُ : بأنَّ تسمية ما حكم فيها بثبوتِ شيءٍ لشيء، أو نفيه عنه : حمليَّةٌ ؛ نظراً لوجودِ الحمل في بعض الصُّوَر ، وإِنَّما نُسبَتْ للحمل دون الوضع؛ مع اشتمالها عليه أيضاً ؛ نظراً إلى أنَّ الحمْلَ مِن حيثُ توقُفُ تمام الفائدة عليه أشرفُ مِنَ الوضْعِ . (قَوْلُهُ : مِنْ ثَلَاثَةِ أُمُوْرِ) أي: أجزاء. (قَوْلُهُ : الْمَحْكُوْمُ عَلَيْهِ) أي : سواءٌ تقدَّمَ في اللفظ أو تأخر، فالأوَّلُ كَ: «زيد قائم» . العطار : (قَوْلُهُ: ثُمَّ الْحَمْلِيَّةُ لَا بُدَّ لَهَا مِنْ ثَلَاثَةِ أُمُوْرِ) هذا على مذهب القدماء، إذْ عندهم إدراكُ النِّسبةِ الثَّابتة بين الموضوع والمحمول هو الحكم، وليس مسبوقاً عندَهُم بِتَصَوُّرِ نسبةٍ هي موردُ الحكم، فإنَّ إثبات تلك النسبةِ مِن تَدقيقاتِ المتأخّرينَ، حيثُ رأوا أنَّ في صورةِ الشَّكُ ينضم إلى الإدراكات الحاصلة إدراك آخَرُ كما يشهدُ بِهِ الوجدانُ لا أَنَّهُ يزولُ إدراك ويحصلُ إدراك آخَرُ بَدَلَهُ، وَلِلمناقشة فيه مجال؛ إذ لأحدٍ أن يلتزم أنَّ المدرك في صورةِ الشَّلِّ هو بعينه المدركُ في صورة الحكم؛ أعني : الوقوع واللاوقوع، والتّفاوت في الإدراكِ؛ فإنَّهُ في الأَوَّلِ : مُدرَك بإدراك غير إذعاني، وفي الثاني : بإدراك إذعاني؛ قالَهُ الجَلالُ، وكأَنَّ الشَّارحَ اختار مذهب المتقدمين هنا مِن أنَّهُ في بحثِ التَّصديق ؛ مرَّ على أنَّ الأجزاء أربعة للاحتياج على رأي المتأخّرينَ، إلى أنْ يُقالَ : الرَّابطةُ دلَّتْ على الجزء الثَّالث والرَّابع معاً ، إحداهُمَا : دلالةٌ مُطابقةٌ، والثانية : دلالة التزام. (قَوْلُهُ : مَوْضُوْعَاً) قالَ السَّيِّدُ : يتناولُ المبتدأ والفاعل أيضاً، فإنَّ زيداً في : قالَ زيد؛ موضوع، وقال محمولٌ ؛ لأنَّ مُحصّل معناه : زيد قائل، أو ذو قول في الزَّمانِ الماضي ا.هـ.
Chapter on definition and its divisions: and it is either (negative) if judgment is made with the mentioned negation. The categorical must have three matters: 1. [the subject]: the first: that which is judged upon, and it is called the subject. al-Dasuqi: And it may be answered: that what is judged in it by affirmation of something for something, or negation from it, is called categorical in view of the existence of predication in some forms; and it is attributed to predication rather than to the subject (position), despite also including it, in view of the fact that predication, because the completion of the benefit depends on it, is nobler than the subject. (His saying: from three matters) i.e., parts. (His saying: that which is judged upon) i.e., whether it precedes in the utterance or follows, the first like 'Zayd is standing'. al-Attar: (His saying: then the categorical must have three matters) this is according to the doctrine of the ancients, for according to them the perception of the fixed relation between subject and predicate is the judgment, and it is not preceded for them by a perception of a relation that is the locus of judgment, for establishing that relation is among the investigations of the later scholars, where they saw that in the case of doubt another perception joins the obtained perceptions, as conscience witnesses, not that a perception disappears and another perception takes its place. There is room for discussion in this, for one can adhere that the perception in the case of doubt is exactly the perception in the case of judgment; that is, occurrence and non-occurrence, and the difference is in the perception itself: in the first it is perceived by a non-assenting perception, and in the second by an assenting perception. al-Jalal said it. It is as if the commentator chose the doctrine of the ancients here, from that it is in the discussion of assent; he passed over that the parts are four due to the need according to the view of the later scholars, so that it could be said: the copula indicates the third and fourth parts together, one of them a literal indication and the other an implicative indication. (His saying: subject) al-Sayyid said: it includes the mubtada' and the fa'il as well, for Zayd in 'Zayd said' is a subject, and 'said' is a predicate, because the gist of its meaning is 'Zayd is a speaker' or 'one who spoke in the past'.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage opens a new section (فصل) on the definition (المعرف) and its divisions. The first part of the commentary defines the subject (موضوع) of a categorical proposition (حمليّة). The author states that a categorical proposition must have three components: the subject, the predicate, and the relation (implied). The first component is the subject, defined as 'that which is judged upon' (المحكوم عليه). Al-Dasuqi then provides an answer to a potential objection: why is the proposition attributed to predication (حمل) rather than to the subject (وضع) even though it includes both? Because predication is nobler since the completion of the benefit (تمام الفائدة) depends on it. Next, the commentator explains that the phrase 'from three matters' means 'parts'. He clarifies that the subject is 'what is judged upon' whether it appears first or last in the utterance; for example, in 'Zayd is standing', Zayd is the subject. Al-Attar then offers a deeper discussion: the requirement of three parts is according to the ancient logicians (قدماء), who considered the perception of the relation between subject and predicate as the judgment itself, without a prior separate perception of a relation. Later scholars (متأخرون) added a fourth part (the copula) to account for the relation as a distinct object of perception. He notes that this is debated, citing al-Jalal (al-Dawwani) who suggests that the difference between doubt and certainty lies in the quality of perception (non-assenting vs. assenting) rather than a change in the object perceived. Finally, al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani elaborates that the term 'subject' (موضوع) in logical propositions includes both the مبتدأ (topic) in a nominal sentence and the فاعل (agent) in a verbal sentence, as in 'Zayd said', which analytically means 'Zayd is a speaker'.

Key Terms
موضوع
محمول
حمل
حمليّة
رابطة

Checkpoint
According to al-Sayyid in the passage, what does the term 'subject' (موضوع) in a categorical proposition include?
A) Only the مبتدأ in nominal sentences.
B) Only the فاعل in verbal sentences.
C) Both the مبتدأ and the فاعل, because verbal sentences can be analyzed as nominal.
D) The grammatical object of a verb.

Key Takeaway
The subject (al-mawḍūʿ) is the component of a categorical proposition that is judged upon; it can be the grammatical topic (mubtadaʾ) or agent (fāʿil), and the proposition is called ḥamliyya because predication is the essential element.

What Is Mantiq?

Critique of the Connector's Definition: Al-Attar's Refinement
Al-Attar critiques the connector definition and addresses al-Isam's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate al-Attar's critique of the definition of the connector and explain how the objection regarding the kasra is resolved.

Core Idea
The connector is an instrument for a non-independent meaning, but its form need not be a particle.

In this passage, al-Attar critically examines the claim that the connector (rabita) is an instrument (ada). He presents a syllogistic proof, reports an objection from al-Isam about the kasra as a counterexample, and then refines the definition to address nuances about non-independence and the diverse forms the connector can take. This discussion sharpens the logical understanding of how relations are expressed in Arabic propositions.

Guided Path
1
Reconstruct the syllogism for the claim
Identify the three parts: al-Attar states the claim, then provides a minor premise ('because it indicates the relation...') and a major premise ('whatever indicates a non-independent meaning is an instrument'). Write the conclusion explicitly to see the first-figure form.
The syllogism is: Minor: The connector indicates a non-independent meaning. Major: Whatever indicates a non-independent meaning is an instrument. Conclusion: The connector is an instrument.
2
Extract al-Isam's objection
Find al-Isam's counterexample: the kasra in 'dabir' (book) functions as a connector (possession) but is not a word (lafz) because it is a single vowel. Why does this challenge the definition?
The definition says the connector is a word; the kasra is not a word by some definitions.
3
Analyze the response to the objection
Note the two-part response: first, a terminological difference between logicians and grammarians (logical 'ada' = grammatical 'harf'), and second, the decisive answer that non-independence is conceptual (bi al-mufhimiyya) – the meaning is observed purely as an instrument for knowing another state.
This refines the definition without requiring the connector to be a full word.
4
Understand the refinement about non-independence
Consider why 'kana' (was) and derived nouns (mushtaqqat) could be problematic. 'Kana' indicates both relation and time; derived nouns indicate relation implicitly. The decisive answer clarifies that non-independence means being a 'mirror' for observing the state of another, not lacking any meaning.
This prevents overgeneralization (e.g., all relational nouns becoming instruments).
5
Identify the corrective about forms
Notice that al-Attar adds: the connector may appear in the form of a noun (e.g., 'huwa' in 'Zayd huwa 'alim') or a verb (e.g., 'kana'). This corrects the impression that the connector is always a particle.
The corrective (istidrak) expands the scope of what counts as a connector.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العطار (قَوْلُهُ : ثُمَّ الرَّابِطَةُ أَدَاةٌ) أي : حرفٌ، وهذهِ دَعوى برهن عليها بقياس اقتراني مِنَ الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ، أشارَ لِصُغراهُ بقولِهِ : لأنَّها تدلُّ على النِّسبةِ... إلخ)، وقولُهُ: «لِتَوقُفِهَا ... إلخ)؛ دليلُ الصُّغرى، وَلِكُبراهُ بِقَولِهِ : (والدَّالُّ على المعنى... إلخ)، والنَّتيجةُ قوله : ( فالرابطة أداة، قال العصامُ : وَفِيهِ أَنَّ الدَّعوى باطلةٌ ؛ لأنَّ كسرة دبير رابطةٌ وليست بأداةٍ؛ لأنَّها ليست بلفظ ؛ لأنَّ أقلَّ مَا يُطلقُ عليهِ اللَّفْظُ : حرفٌ واحدٌ؛ صرَّحَ بِهِ الشَّيخُ ابنُ الحاجبِ، إِلَّا أَنْ يثبت تخالفُ الاصطلاحين في اللفظ لكن ما ذكرَهُ السَّيِّدُ في بعض تصانيفِهِ؛ أَنَّ مَا يُسمِّيهِ القوم أداةً ؛ هو الحرفُ عند النحاة؛، یرد التخالف . هي حالة بين (قَوْلُهُ: الَّتِي هِيَ غَيْرُ مُسْتَقِلَّةٍ لأنَّ النِّسبة مُتعلّقةٌ مِن حيثُ الموضوع ،والمحمول، وآلةٌ لتعرُّفِ حالِهِمَا ، فَلَا يكونُ مَعنِّى مُستقلاً يصلحُ لِأَنْ فَاللَّفظُ الدَّالُ عليها يكون أداةٌ، ثمَّ إِنْ أُرِيْدَ بِدلالةِ الرَّابطة به يكون محكوماً عليه أو على النّسبةِ الدَّلالة المطابقيَّةُ ؛ لَزِمَ خروج «كان» ؛ لِدَلالَتِها على النِّسبةِ الرَّابطة بالتَّضمُّنِ؛ لأنَّها تدلُّ على الزَّمانِ أيضاً ، وإِن أُرِيدَ أعمُّ مِنَ المطابقيَّةِ والتَّضمنيَّةِ ؛ يلزمُ أنْ تكونَ المشتقَاتُ أداةً لِلدلالتِهَا على النِّسبةِ تَضمناً. (قَوْلُهُ: لِتَوَقُفِهَا عَلَى الْمَحْكُوْمِ عَلَيْهِ وَبِهِ ) أي : وكلُّ ما هو كذلِكَ فَهُوَ غَيرُ مُستقلِّ، فَهَهُنا كُبرى مطويَّةٌ لدليل صُغرى القياس الأوَّل . والدَّالُّ على المعنى الغير المستقل يكون أداةً، فالرابطة أداة، لكنها قد تكون في قالب الاسم، كـ «هو» في : زيد هو عالم، وقد تكون في قالب الكلمة. (قَوْلُهُ : وَالدَّالُ عَلَى الْمَعْنَى الْغَيْرِ الْمُسْتَقِلَّ يَكُوْنُ أَدَاةٌ) أوردَ عليهِ أَنَّهُ يلزمُ أَنْ تكونَ جميع الأسماءِ الدَّالَّةِ على النِّسَبِ والإضافات أدوات، وأُجِيبُ : بأنَّ الأدواتِ لا استقلال لمعناها المطابقي وَلَا لِما دخل فيه، والأسماءُ الدَّالَّة على النِّسَبِ والإضافات، وإن لم تستقل باعتبارِ معناها المطابقيّ؛ لكنَّها مُستقلَةٌ بِاعتبارِ ما دخلَ فيه، ولا كذلكَ الأدواتُ ، وَردَّ بأن جعلَ كانَ مِنَ الأدوات يدلّ على إرادة عدم الاستقلال، وَلَو باعتبار ما دخلَ في المعنى المطابقيّ، والجواب الحاسمُ أنَّ المراد بعدم استقلال المعنى بالمفهوميَّة؛ هو أن يكون مُلاحظاً من حيثُ كونُهُ آلَةً ومِرآة لملاحظةِ حالِ الغيرِ، على نحو ما قيل في معنى الحرفِ؛ تَأَمَّلْ (قَوْلُهُ : لَكِنَّهَا قَدْ تَكُونُ فِي قَالَبِ الْإِسْم استدراك على قولِهِ : فالرابطة أداةٌ ، فإِنَّهُ يوهمُ أَنَّ الرَّابطةَ مِن حيثُ هِي لا تكون إلا في قوالب الحروفِ، فَرفع ذلك الإيهام بالاستدراك .
Al-Attar: (His statement: 'Then the connector is an instrument') i.e., a particle. This is a claim proven by a conjunctive syllogism of the first figure. He indicated its minor premise by his statement: 'because it indicates the relation... etc.' and his statement: 'due to its dependence... etc.' is evidence for the minor, and for the major by his statement: 'and whatever indicates a meaning... etc.' and the conclusion is his statement: 'so the connector is an instrument.' Al-Isam said: 'In it is that the claim is false because the kasra of 'dabir' is a connector but not an instrument because it is not a word; for the least that can be called a word is one letter, as stated by Shaykh Ibn al-Hajib. Unless it is established that there is a difference in terminology regarding 'word', but what al-Sayyid mentioned in some of his works, that what the logicians call an instrument is what the grammarians call a particle, so the disagreement is dismissed.' It is a state between... (His statement: 'which is non-independent' because the relation is dependent on the subject and predicate, and an instrument for knowing their state, so it is not an independent meaning suitable for... So the word indicating it is an instrument. Then if by the connector's indication is meant the literal (mutabaqi) indication, 'kana' would be excluded because it indicates the relational implication (tadammun) since it also indicates time. And if a broader meaning including literal and implicative is intended, then derived nouns would be instruments because they indicate the relation implicitly. (His statement: 'due to its dependence on the subject and predicate') means: everything that is like that is non-independent. So here the major premise is implied for the proof of the minor of the first syllogism.' And the word indicating a non-independent meaning is an instrument, so the connector is an instrument, but it may be in the form of a noun, like 'huwa' in 'Zayd is a scholar', and it may be in the form of a verb. (His statement: 'And whatever indicates a non-independent meaning is an instrument') An objection was raised against it that it would necessitate that all nouns indicating relations and additions be instruments. It was answered: that instruments have no independence for their literal meaning nor for what is included in it, while nouns indicating relations and additions, even if they are not independent in terms of their literal meaning, are independent in terms of what is included in it, unlike instruments. It was refuted that making 'kana' an instrument indicates the intention of non-independence, even in terms of what is included in the literal meaning. And the decisive answer is that what is meant by non-independence of meaning in terms of conceptuality is that it be observed as an instrument and mirror for observing the state of another, similar to what is said about the meaning of the particle; reflect. (His statement: 'But it may be in the form of a noun' is a corrective (istidrak) to his statement: 'So the connector is an instrument', because it gives the impression that the connector as such is only in the forms of particles, so he removes that impression with the corrective.)
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage presents al-Attar's critical refinement of the definition of the connector (rabita). Al-Attar begins by stating that the claim 'the connector is an instrument' is proven by a categorical syllogism of the first figure. He then reports al-Isam's objection: the kasra (a grammatical case mark) in the word 'dabir' (as in 'dabir-i', meaning 'my book') acts as a connector (indicating possession) but is not a word because it is a single vowel, contradicting the definition that the connector is a 'word' (lafz). The response acknowledges a terminological difference: logicians use 'instrument' (ada) to correspond to 'particle' (harf) in grammar, and the kasra may be considered a word in that sense. Al-Attar then delves into the meaning of 'non-independent', explaining that the relation between subject and predicate is dependent and instrumental. He considers the implications for the verb 'kana' (to be), which indicates both the relation and time; if 'kana' is considered a connector, it only indicates the relation implicitly (tadammunan), not literally. Similarly, derived nouns (mushtaqqat) would become instruments if they indicate relations. To resolve this, al-Attar clarifies that 'non-independence' is understood conceptually (bi al-mufhimiyya): the meaning is considered purely as an instrument and mirror for observing the state of another, just as the meaning of a grammatical particle is understood. Finally, he notes that the connector can appear in the form of a noun (e.g., 'huwa' meaning 'he is') or in the form of a verb (e.g., 'kana'), which is a corrective (istidrak) to prevent the misconception that the connector is always a particle. Throughout, al-Attar engages in a dialectical refinement of the definition, addressing objections and clarifying nuances.

Key Terms
أداة (ada): In this context, 'ada' is defined by al-Attar as a word that indicates a non-independent meaning, such that it is an instrument for observing the state of something else, like a mirror. [Note: The term is debated between logicians and grammarians; here it is equated with 'harf' (particle) in grammar.]
كسرة (kasra): A grammatical case mark (kasra) used as a connector in Arabic, e.g., the kasra in 'dabir' indicating possession; it is raised as a counterexample to the definition. [Note: Not a standard term in logic; arises from the intersection of logic and Arabic grammar.]
قالب الاسم (qalab al-ism): The form of a noun; the connector may appear in the form of a noun like 'huwa' (he) which is a pronoun, but functionally an instrument. [Note: This is a nuanced point: logicians consider 'huwa' a connector even though its form is a pronoun.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Attar's discussion, what is the decisive answer to the objection that all nouns indicating relations would become instruments?
A) Because instruments have no independent meaning at all, even within their scope.
B) Because nouns are always independent.
C) Because the definition is wrong.
D) Because the connector is only ever a particle.

Key Takeaway
The connector is an instrument indicating a non-independent relation, but its definition must be refined to allow for conceptual non-independence and varying forms, not limited to particles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a first-figure categorical syllogism to prove that the proposition 'The world is temporally originated' is valid, using an explicit connector in the minor premise. Explain how al-Attar's refined definition applies.
?

What is a Conditional Proposition?
Defines conditional proposition and previews its types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a conditional proposition from a categorical one and identify its two types (conjunctive and disjunctive).

Core Idea
Conditional propositions judge under a condition, not a simple subject-predicate link.

The passage defines the conditional proposition (al-qadiyya al-shartiyya) in contrast to the categorical. Where categorical propositions make a direct affirmation or negation, conditional propositions involve a condition such as 'if…then' or 'either…or'. Understanding this distinction is foundational for working with complex arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining feature
Notice that the passage says the conditional is defined by the judgment being 'otherwise' than direct affirmation/negation. The key is that the judgment depends on a condition.
This is the genus-differentia approach: genus = proposition, differentia = judgment involves a condition.
2
Contrast with categorical
Categorical propositions are 'subject-predicate' (e.g., 'Zayd is standing'). Conditional propositions involve a relation between two statements (e.g., 'If A then B').
This contrast helps you classify any given proposition.
3
Identify the two types
The passage previews conjunctive (muttasila) with 'if…then' and disjunctive (munfasila) with 'either…or'. Conjunctive has one relation conditional on another; disjunctive involves conflict or non-conflict between two relations.
Examples: 'If the sun is rising, daytime exists' (conjunctive); 'The number is either even or odd' (disjunctive).
4
Test with examples
Using the passage's examples, identify which are categorical, which conjunctive conditional, and which disjunctive conditional.
The passage also gives a disjunctive non-conflict example: 'It is not the case that Zayd is either knowledgeable or devout.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِلا (إلا) ، أي : وإن لم يكن الحكم في القضيَّة بالثبوتِ والنَّفي المذكورين ؛ (فَشَرْطِيَّةٌ) ، أي : فالقضيَّة شرطيَّةٌ . فالحمليّة : هي التي حُكِمَ فيها بثبوتِ شيء لشيء، أو بنفي شيء عن شيءٍ، والشَّرطيَّةُ : هي التي حُكِمَ فيها بغير ذلك، كما سيجيءُ مِنْ أَنَّ الشَّرطية هي التي حكم فيها بثبوت نسبة أو بنفيها، على تقدير نسبة أخرى إن كانت متصلةً، وبتنافي نسبتين أو لا تنافيهما إِنْ كَانَت مُنفصلةٌ. ثمَّ إنَّ الحكماءَ لَمَّا نقلوا الحكمةَ مِنَ اللُّغةِ اليونانيَّةِ لِلُّغةِ العربيَّةِ؛ وجدوا بإزاء كلِّ جزء من أجزاء القضيَّةِ لفظاً مستقلاً دالا عليهِ دونَ النّسبة، فقد وجدوا الحركاتِ الإعرابيَّةَ دالَّةٌ عليها ؛ فاستعارُوا كلمةَ «هو» بإزاء النِّسبة بدلاً عن : هست وأستين، واستعارُوا كانَ ) بدلاً عن بود ، وإِنَّما اختارُوا «هو» ؛ لأنَّها من المبهمات، والكنايات، والنسبة تشاركهما في الإبهام والخفاء، وبعبارة «هست» بالفارسية، و«أستين» باليونانية، وهي التي تدلُّ على ربط المحمول الاسم بالموضوع ربطاً غير زماني . وَلَمَّا لم يجدوا في العربيَّة في أوَّلِ وضعها لفظاً يقومُ مَقامَ ذلك بخلافِ الرَّبط الزَّماني، فإنَّ الكلمة الوجوديَّةً مثل كان، ويكون، وسيكون»، تدلُّ على ذلكَ الرَّبطِ في لغة العرب؛ اختار بعضُهُم لفظ «هو». (قَوْلُهُ : فَشَرْطِيَّةٌ) لِوجودِ الشَّرطِ فيها . (قَوْلُهُ : بِتُبُوتِ نِسْبَةٍ نحو : إِنْ كانَتِ الشَّمس طالعة؛ فالنَّهارُ مَوجود. (قَوْلُهُ: أَوْ بِنَفْيِها نحو ليسَ إنْ كانَتِ الشَّمس طالعة؛ فاللَّيلُ موجود (قَوْلُهُ : وَبِتَنَا فِي نِسْبَتَيْنِ) نحو : العددُ إمَّا زوج أو فرد، وقوله: (أوْ لَا تُنافيهما)؛ نحو : ليس زيدٌ إما أن يكون عالماً أو عابداً، فهذا حكم فيه بسلبِ التّنافي . (قَوْلُهُ : بِغَيْرِ ذَلِكَ هذا صريح في أنَّ الشرطيَّةَ يكون الحكم فيها بغيرِ الاتصال والانفصال؛ نحو: رأيتُ إِمَّا زيداً وإِمَّا عَمْراً، والعالِمُ إِمَّا أَنْ يعبدَ اللهَ وَإِمَّا أَنْ ينفع النَّاسَ ، فالبيانُ بقولِهِ كَما سيجيء مِن أنَّ ... إلخ؛ أخصُّ مِنَ المبين.
And if not (illa), i.e., if the judgment in the proposition is not by the mentioned affirmation and negation, then (it is conditional), i.e., the proposition is conditional. The categorical proposition is that in which a thing is judged to be affirmed of a thing or negated from a thing, and the conditional is that in which it is judged otherwise, as will come: that the conditional is that in which judgment is made of affirmation of a relation or its negation on the condition of another relation if it is conjunctive, and of the conflict of two relations or their non-conflict if it is disjunctive. Then, when the philosophers translated the wisdom from the Greek language into the Arabic language, they found for every part of the proposition an independent word indicating it except the relation; they found the inflectional vowels indicating it; so they borrowed the word 'huwa' for the relation instead of 'hast' and 'astein', and they borrowed 'kana' instead of 'bud'. They chose 'huwa' because it is among the ambiguous expressions and pronouns, and the relation shares with them in ambiguity and hiddenness, and by the expression 'hast' in Persian and 'astein' in Greek, which indicate the linking of the predicate to the subject with a non-temporal link. When they did not find in Arabic at its first construction a word to stand in that place, unlike the temporal link, for the existential word like 'kana', 'yakunu', 'sayakunu' indicate that link in the Arabic language, some of them chose the word 'huwa'. (His statement: 'fa-shartiyya') because of the presence of the condition in it. (His statement: 'by affirmation of a relation' like: If the sun is rising, then the daytime exists. (His statement: 'or by its negation' like: It is not the case that if the sun is rising, then the night exists. (His statement: 'and by conflict of two relations') like: The number is either even or odd. And his statement: 'or their non-conflict' like: It is not the case that Zayd is either knowledgeable or devout; this is a judgment involving the negation of conflict. (His statement: 'otherwise') this is explicit in that the conditional may involve a judgment other than conjunction and disjunction; like: I saw either Zayd or 'Amr, and the scholar is either to worship God or to benefit people. So the explanation by his saying 'as will come...' is more specific than what is explained.
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional proposition (al-qadiyya al-shartiyya) in contrast to the categorical (hamliyya). The author states that if the judgment in a proposition is not by the simple affirmation or negation of a predicate for a subject, then it is conditional. The categorical is characterized by a direct subject-predicate relation, while the conditional involves a judgment under a condition. The author then previews the two types: conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). In the conjunctive, there is an affirmation or negation of one relation on the condition of another (e.g., 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists'). In the disjunctive, there is a judgment of conflict or non-conflict between two relations (e.g., 'The number is either even or odd' for conflict, and 'It is not that Zayd is either knowledgeable or devout' for non-conflict). The passage then discusses an interesting historical point: when translating Greek logic into Arabic, the philosophers found that Arabic had no single word for the non-temporal relation (the copula) as distinct from tense. They borrowed the word 'huwa' (he/it) to represent the link between subject and predicate, analogous to Greek 'hast' and 'astein'. The temporal link, however, could be expressed by existential verbs like 'kana' (was). This shows the effort to adapt logical structures into the Arabic language. The commentary (by al-Dasuqi and al-'Attar) then elaborates on the definitions with examples and notes that the preliminary definition of conditional is more general than the technical one given later.

Key Terms
قضية شرطية
شرطية متصلة
شرطية منفصلة
حمليّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, which of the following is an example of a disjunctive conditional?
A) If the sun is rising, then daytime exists.
B) The number is either even or odd.
C) Zayd is knowledgeable.
D) The world is created.

Key Takeaway
A conditional proposition is identified by the presence of a condition affecting the judgment; it is not a direct subject-predicate statement.

?

?
Identify the antecedent and consequent in: 'If the traveler does not shorten the prayer, then he is not traveling.'

The Copula 'Huwa': Adapting Greek Logic to Arabic
Explains the borrowing of 'huwa' as a copula from Greek.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Arabic logicians borrowed the word 'huwa' as a copula and how it differs from the temporal link.

Core Idea
Arabic lacked a non-temporal copula, so logicians borrowed 'huwa' from pronouns.

This passage also explains a fascinating historical adaptation: when Greek logic was translated into Arabic, philosophers found no word for the non-temporal relation (the copula). They borrowed the ambiguous pronoun 'huwa' (he/it) to fill this gap. This shows how technical terms were consciously coined to serve the needs of logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Greek logic had separate words for the non-temporal copula ('hast', 'astein'), but Arabic had none. Arabic had inflectional vowels to indicate relation but no independent word.
The passage states: 'they found for every part of the proposition an independent word... except the relation.'
2
Find the solution
They borrowed the word 'huwa' (he/it) from pronouns and the word 'kana' (was/were) for temporal relations. 'Huwa' was chosen because it is ambiguous and pronominal, like the relation itself.
The passage says 'huwa' is among the 'ambiguous expressions and pronouns' and shares in 'ambiguity and hiddenness' with the relation.
3
Contrast with temporal link
The temporal link (past, present, future) was already expressible with existential verbs like 'kana' (was), 'yakunu' (is), 'sayakunu' (will be). The non-temporal copula was the missing piece.
The passage explicitly contrasts: 'unlike the temporal link, for the existential word like kana...'
4
Apply the concept
In a sentence like 'Zaydun huwa 'alimun', the 'huwa' functions as the non-temporal copula linking the subject 'Zayd' to the predicate ''alim' (knowledgeable). This is a direct legacy of the Greek borrowing.
Modern Arabic grammar does not require 'huwa' as a copula, but logical texts used it explicitly.

Four Types of Modified Categorical Propositions
Defines and exemplifies four types of modified categorical propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify any categorical proposition as having a modified subject, modified predicate, both, or neither, and determine its affirmation or negation.

Core Idea
In modified propositions, negation lives inside the term, not the copula—changing the logical form.

The classical logicians distinguish propositions where the subject or predicate term contains a negation. This lesson walks through the four possible types using examples from al-Dasuqi's commentary. Mastering this classification is essential for accurate syllogistic reasoning in Islamic sciences, especially in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the copula quality
First, determine if the proposition is affirmative or negative by looking at the copula (e.g., 'is' or 'is not'). For example, 'The non-living is inanimate' is affirmative because 'is' is positive.
Do not confuse the copula with negation inside terms.
2
Check subject for negation
Examine the subject term: does it contain a word like 'non-' or 'لا'? If so, the subject is modified. In 'The non-living is inanimate', 'non-living' contains 'non-', so subject is modified.
3
Check predicate for negation
Similarly, check the predicate term for negation. In 'The inanimate is not knowing', the predicate 'not knowing' contains 'not', so predicate is modified.
4
Classify into one of four types
Combine the copula quality with modified status of subject and predicate. For example: affirmative + modified subject = affirmative with modified subject. If both terms are modified, it is 'both modified' (معدولتهما).
The passage also gives examples where both terms are modified in both affirmation and negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كَقولنا : «اللَّاحَي جمادٌ»، و«الجمادُ لا عالم»، و«لا شيء من اللاحَيّ بعالم أو : من العالم بلا حي». الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : اللَّأحَيُّ جَمَادٌ) أي: أنَّ ما يصدقُ عليه أنَّه غيرُ حي؛ يصدقُ عليه أنَّه جمادٌ، وهذا مثال للموجبة معدولة الموضوع. وقوله : (الجمادُ لَا عالم مثال للموجبة معدولة المحمول، وقوله : (لا شيء مِنَ اللَّاحِيّ بعالم؛ مثالٌ لِلسَّالبة معدولة الموضوع. وقولُهُ: (أو مِنَ العالِم بلا حيّ ؛ مثالٌ لِلسَّالبة معدولة المحمول، وترك مثال معدولتهما في الموجبة والسالبة، ومثال معدولتِهما في الموجبة : كلُّ لَا حيوان هو لا إنسان)؛ أي: إِنَّ كلَّ ما صدق عليهِ أنَّه غيرُ حيوانٍ ؛ صدق عليهِ أَنَّهُ غيرُ إنسان. ومثالُ مَعدولتِهما في السَّالبة: (ليس غيرُ الحيوانِ بغير جماد)، فقد حكم بسلْبِ حدم الجماديَّةِ عَن غيرِ الحيوان، وإذا سُلِبَ (۱) عدم الجماديَّة عن غير الحيوان؛ كان جماداً.
Like our saying: 'The non-living is inanimate', and 'The inanimate is not knowing', and 'Nothing of the non-living is knowing', or 'of the knowing is without life'. al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'The non-living is inanimate' means that what is true of it that it is non-living, it is true that it is inanimate. This is an example of an affirmative with modified subject. And his statement: 'The inanimate is not knowing' is an example of an affirmative with modified predicate. And his statement: 'Nothing of the non-living is knowing' is an example of a negative with modified subject. And his statement: 'or of the knowing is without life' is an example of a negative with modified predicate. And he left the example of both modified in affirmation and negation. The example of both modified in affirmation: 'Every non-animal is non-human' i.e., everything of which it is true that it is non-animal, is true that it is non-human. And the example of both modified in negation: 'Non-animal is not non-inanimate'. So he judged by negating the non-inanimateness from non-animal. And when the non-inanimateness is negated from non-animal, it is inanimate.
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage defines and exemplifies the four types of modified categorical propositions: (1) affirmative with modified subject, (2) affirmative with modified predicate, (3) negative with modified subject, (4) negative with modified predicate. The commentator, al-Dasuqi, explains each example given in the original text. The first example, 'The non-living is inanimate', is an affirmative whose subject 'non-living' contains a negation, so it is an affirmative with modified subject (معدولة الموضوع). The second, 'The inanimate is not knowing', is an affirmative but the predicate contains negation, so it is an affirmative with modified predicate (معدولة المحمول). The third, 'Nothing of the non-living is knowing', is a negative with modified subject. The fourth, 'of the knowing is without life', is a negative with modified predicate. The author then adds examples where both subject and predicate are modified: in the affirmative, 'Every non-animal is non-human'; in the negative, 'Non-animal is not non-inanimate'. This covers all possibilities. The commentary explains the logic: 'non-animal' is not inanimate, but negating 'non-inanimate' yields inanimate.

Key Terms
معدولة الموضوع
معدولة المحمول
معدولتهما

Key Takeaway
Modified propositions embed negation within the term, and there are exactly four types determined by which term(s) are modified and the copula's quality.

Understanding Subject and Predicate: The Four Possibilities
Author enumerates four possible meanings of subject and predicate and identifies the correct one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which combination of subject and predicate (individuals vs concept) yields a valid universal proposition and explain why the other three are invalid.

Core Idea
In a valid proposition, the subject refers to its individuals, the predicate to its concept.

This lesson clarifies a foundational rule in logical predication: how do we correctly interpret the subject and predicate terms? The author presents four possible combinations and identifies only one as valid. Mastering this distinction is crucial for understanding how universal propositions function in Islamic sciences like Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish 'essence of subject' and 'description of subject'
The subject term's individuals are called dhat al-mawdu', and the predicate term's concept is called wasf al-mawdu'. Understand these definitions before analyzing the combinations.
2
List the four combinations
Write down the four possibilities: (1) both terms taken as concept, (2) both taken as individuals, (3) subject individuals + predicate concept, (4) subject concept + predicate individuals. This is the author's enumeration.
3
Test with a concrete example
Take 'Every human is rational'. Apply each combination: (1) 'Human as concept, rational as concept' yields a natural universal (pointless). (2) 'Human as individuals, rational as individuals' yields identity (each human is the individual rational being). (3) 'Human individuals, rational concept' yields correct predication. (4) 'Human concept, rational individuals' yields an unnatural reading. See which matches the author's verdict.
4
Recognize the correct combination and its rationale
Only combination (3) is valid. The subject must be taken extensionally (its individuals) so the predicate applies to each individual; the predicate must be taken intensionally (its concept) so that the attribute is common. This avoids natural or identity propositions.
5
Apply to an Islamic science example
Verify with 'The world is temporally originated'. Subject 'world' refers to its instances (each created world), predicate 'temporally originated' refers to the attribute. This combination is used in kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المُعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ
واعلَمْ أنَّ ما صَدَقَ عليه الموضوع يُقال له ذات الموضوع، وما صَدَقَ عليه المحمول يُقال له وصف الموضوع. ثم إن الاحتمالات أربعة: الأول: أن يُراد المفهوم من كل منهما وهو باطل. الثاني: أن يُراد الماصدق منهما وهو باطل. الثالث: أن يُراد من الأول الماصدق ومن الثاني المفهوم وهو الصحيح. الرابع: العكس وهو باطل. وهذا هو المعتبر.
Chapter: On the definition and its divisions. Know that what the subject applies to is called the essence of the subject, and what the predicate applies to is called the description of the subject. Then the possibilities are four: First: that the concept is intended from each of them, and that is invalid. Second: that the denotation is intended from each of them, and that is also invalid. Third: that the denotation is intended from the first and the concept from the second, and that is correct. Fourth: the opposite, and that is invalid. And this is what is considered valid.
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the correct usage of subject and predicate terms in logical propositions. It begins with a chapter heading 'On the definition and its divisions' and then clarifies what is meant by 'essence of the subject' (dhat al-mawdu') and 'description of the subject' (wasf al-mawdu'): the subject term denotes its individuals, and the predicate term denotes its concept. The author then enumerates the four possible combinations of how subject and predicate can be taken: both as concepts (invalid), both as individuals (invalid), subject as individuals and predicate as concept (correct), and the reverse (invalid). The key insight is that for a universal affirmative proposition like 'Every human is an animal', the subject 'human' must be considered in terms of its individual instances (e.g., Zayd, Bakr) while the predicate 'animal' must be taken as a concept (the meaning of animal). If both were taken as concepts, the proposition would be a natural universal, which is not used in sciences. If both were taken as individuals, the predicate would be identical to the subject, making the proposition trivial or contradictory. Thus only the third combination yields a valid predicative statement.

Key Terms
ذات الموضوع (dhat al-mawdu'): The individuals (afrad) that fall under the subject term, not its conceptual meaning.
وصف الموضوع (wasf al-mawdu'): The conceptual meaning (mafhum) of the predicate term, not its individuals.
الماصدق (al-masadaq): The denotation or extension of a term, i.e., the individuals that it applies to. [Note: In later logic texts, masadaq is often used interchangeably with afrad, but here it specifically contrasts with mafhum.]
المفهوم (al-mafhum): The intension or conceptual content of a term, its definitional meaning.
الحمل (al-haml): Predication: the attribution of a predicate to a subject in a proposition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which combination of subject and predicate is correct for a valid universal proposition?
A) Subject as concept, predicate as concept
B) Subject as individuals, predicate as individuals
C) Subject as individuals, predicate as concept
D) Subject as concept, predicate as individuals

Key Takeaway
In every valid universal proposition, the subject term is taken extensionally (its individuals) and the predicate term intensionally (its concept).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Consider the proposition 'All prophets are truthful' (kullu al-anbiyā'i ṣādiqūn). Identify whether the subject 'prophets' is taken as its individuals or its concept, and whether the predicate 'truthful' is taken as its individuals or its concept. Then state which of the four combinations is being used.
?
2. Take a proposition from Usul al-Fiqh: 'Every obligation is commanded by Allah' (kullu wājibin ma'mūrun bihī). Apply the same analysis and explain why the correct combination is essential for the logical validity of the statement.

The Absolute Perpetual Proposition: Definition and Rationale
Author defines the absolute perpetual proposition and explains its name.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an absolute perpetual proposition and distinguish it from other modal proposition types.

Core Idea
The absolute perpetual proposition is the simplest modality: judgment holds as long as the subject exists.

This passage introduces a fundamental type of proposition in Islamic logic: the absolute perpetual (al-da'imah al-mutlaqah). This is the default modality for universal statements in syllogistic arguments. Understanding it is crucial because it forms the basis for more complex modalities like necessity and conditionality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the definiendum in the Arabic: الدائمة المطلقة. The author defines it as a proposition where the judgment is perpetual as long as the subject's essence exists.
Note that the definition is given in a conditional form: 'if the judgment is by perpetuity as long as the subject exists, then it is an absolute perpetual proposition.'
2
Extract the condition for perpetuity
The key condition is 'ما دام ذات الموضوع موجودة' (as long as the essence of the subject exists). This determines the duration of the judgment.
Unlike necessary propositions, no extra condition (like necessity) is attached.
3
Analyze why it is called 'absolute' (مطلقة)
The perpetuity is not restricted by any additional description or time; it is unqualified. Hence it is 'absolute'.
Contrast with propositions where perpetuity is qualified, e.g., 'as long as it is night' or 'by necessity'.
4
Apply the definition to examples
The author gives two examples: 'Every human is an animal perpetually' and 'No human is a stone perpetually'. Verify that for each instance of human, the judgment (affirmation or negation) holds as long as that human exists.
These examples cover both affirmative and negative forms of absolute perpetual propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْمَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ (الدَّائمةُ المطلقة) : أَوْ بِدَوَامِهَا) عطفٌ على قوله : ( بضرورة النّسبَةِ )؛ أي: إن كانَ الحكم فيها بدوام النّسبةِ (مَا دَامَ الذَّاتُ)؛ أي : ما دامَ ذاتُ الموضوعِ موجودةً ; (فَدَائِمَةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ) . وَإِنَّما سُمِّيَتْ دائمةً؛ لاشتمالها على الدوام، وإِنَّما سُمّيت مطلقة؛ لأنَّ الدَّوامَ فيها غير مقيَّد بوصف أو وقت، تقولِنَا : (كلُّ إنسان حيوان دائماً)، و(لا شيء من الإنسان بحجرٍ دائماً)، فإنَّ الحكم فيها بدوام ثبوتِ الحيوانية للإنسان وسلب الحَجَرِيَّةِ عنه
Section: On the definition and its divisions. (The absolute perpetual proposition): (Or by its perpetuity) is connected to his saying: (by the necessity of the relation); that is: if the judgment in it is by the perpetuity of the relation (as long as the subject); that is: as long as the essence of the subject exists; (so it is an absolute perpetual proposition). And it is only called perpetual because it includes perpetuity, and it is only called absolute because the perpetuity in it is not restricted by a description or time. Our saying: (Every human is an animal perpetually), and (No human is a stone perpetually), for the judgment in it is by perpetuity of affirmation of animality for human and negation of stone-ness from him.
Source pages p. 557

Commentary
This passage defines one of the types of quantified propositions in Islamic logic: the absolute perpetual (al-da'imah al-mutlaqah). The author states that if the judgment (the relation between subject and predicate) is perpetual as long as the subject's essence exists, then it is called an absolute perpetual proposition. 'Perpetual' because the judgment continues without interruption; 'absolute' because the perpetuity is not qualified by a specific time or condition (unlike, e.g., the necessary proposition which is restricted by a condition). Examples are given: 'Every human is an animal perpetually' (affirmative) and 'No human is a stone perpetually' (negative). In both, the judgment holds for every instance as long as the subject exists. This type is foundational for syllogistic reasoning, particularly in the first figure where universal affirmative premises are required.

Key Terms
الدائمة المطلقة
الدوام
الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The absolute perpetual proposition is defined by the condition that the judgment holds for every instance of the subject as long as the subject exists, with no additional restrictions.
